Answer to the Fourth Objection: Motion and Indivisible Parts
Razi answers the objection that motion is transition not instantaneous presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the objection that motion is transition, not presence, and how it reinforces the concept of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Motion as transition does not refute the instantaneous present; it forces the indivisible atom.

This lesson examines a critical objection against the existence of indivisible particles (al-jawhar al-fard). The objector argues that motion is a transition between places, not a state of presence in a place, thus the earlier argument about present motion fails. Razi systematically dismantles this objection by exploring the logical possibilities during a state of transition and showing that all are necessarily false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims motion is transition, not presence. Grasp why this challenges the previous argument for indivisible atoms.
This objection targets the idea that instantaneous motion can be divided.
2
Follow Razi's three-case analysis
Razi examines three possibilities for the moving thing during transition: present in a specific place, present in a non-specific place, or not in any place. Notice how each leads to absurdity.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the conclusion
All possibilities are necessarily false, so the objection fails. Understand how this supports the existence of indivisible atoms.
Razi appeals to necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحصل في ذلك الشيء الحاضر جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بحسب الزمان أو لا يمكن . فإن حصل هذا النوع من القسمة ، لم يكن المجموع حاصلاً . وكلامنا في الحاصل . هذا خلف . وإن امتنع حصول هذا النوع من القسمة ، كان المقصود حاصلاً ، لأن القدر المتحرك عليه من المسافة بذلك القدر من الحركة. إن كان منقسماً ، كانت الحركة إلى نصفه سابقة بالزمان على الحركة من نصفه ، إلى آخره فحينئذ يفرض في ذلك القدر من الحركة جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بالزمان . وقد فرضنا : أنه ليس كذلك. هذا خلف . وإن لم يكن ذلك القدر من المسافة قابلا للقسمة ، فذلك هو الجوهر الفرد. فثبت : أن مقصودنا في هذا الدليل يتم ببيان أن القدر الحاضر من الحركة، لا يقبل القسمة التي يكون أحد الجزءين سابقاً على الآخر. وعن السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : ( الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز ، وليست عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، فنقول : إنه حال كونه منتقلا من حيز إلى حيز ، فإما أن يكون حاصلا في حيز معين . وذلك يبطل قولكم إن كونه متحركاً ، ينافي كونه حاصلاً في حيز معين ) . وإما أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز غير معين . أو يقال : إنه غير حاصل في شيء من الأحياز. والعلم بفساد كل واحد من هذين القسمين : علم ضروري فزال السؤال والله أعلم.
In that present thing, two parts occur such that one is anterior to the other in time or not possible. If this type of division occurs, then the composite is not existent. Our discussion concerns what is existent. This is a contradiction. And if this type of division is impossible, then the intended is existent, because the extent of distance moved over by that extent of motion—if it is divisible, then the motion to its half is anterior in time to the motion from its half to its end; then at that point two parts are posited in that extent of motion such that one is anterior to the other in time. But we have assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction. And if that extent of distance is not susceptible to division, then that is the indivisible atom. So it is established that our purpose in this argument is fulfilled by showing that the present extent of motion does not admit division such that one of the two parts is anterior to the other. And regarding the fourth question: which is his statement: (Motion is transition from place to place, and it is not defined as being present in a place. So we say: In the state of his being in transition from place to place, either he is present in a specific place—and that invalidates your statement that his being in motion contradicts his being present in a specific place—or he is present in a non-specific place, or it is said that he is not present in any of the places. And knowledge of the corruption of each of these two categories is necessary knowledge. So the objection is removed. And God knows best.)
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It begins by continuing the response to the fourth objection about motion. The argument proceeds by considering the present moment of motion: if it has two parts with one temporally prior, the whole is not instantaneous (contradiction). If not divisible, the distance is composed of indivisible atoms. Then it directly answers the objection: the objector claims motion is transition, not presence. The response considers three possibilities during transition: the moving thing is present in a specific place (contradicts motion), present in a non-specific place, or not in any place. All are necessarily false, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الحركة
القسمة
القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument for indivisible parts relies on motion being continuous, but here it uses the impossibility of temporal priority in an instantaneous present.
Correction: The argument does not assume continuity; it focuses on the present moment of motion and shows that any temporal division within that moment leads to contradiction, thereby forcing the acceptance of indivisible atoms.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the argument about the present extent of motion?
A) It is divisible into two temporally prior parts.
B) It does not admit division with temporal priority of one part.
C) It is composed of an infinite number of parts.
D) It is the same as the indivisible atom.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the objection that motion is transition fails because all possible interpretations of 'being in transition' lead to logical impossibilities, thus preserving the argument for indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi forces you to see that every moment of motion contains an indivisible present. In your life, do you treat each moment as a discrete, present reality before God, or do you let your days blur into a vague, undefined transition? What would change if you lived each moment as an indivisible atom of accountability?
?
The argument dismisses three possibilities as necessarily false through immediate knowledge. What are three comfortable falsehoods in your own understanding of time and action that you have never subjected to such rigorous scrutiny? Will you apply the same logical standard to your own assumptions?
?
If motion itself cannot escape the necessity of atomic presence, then your every action—even your thoughts—must be composed of present, distinct moments. Each instant is a chance to turn to Allah or away. Right now, are you present in this moment with sincerity, or are you merely transitioning through a routine? Commit to being fully present in your next act of worship.

Razi's Second Proof: The Line is Composed of Indivisible Points
Razi proves that a line is composed of indivisible points through the movement of a point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's second proof uses the motion of a point to demonstrate that a line must be composed of indivisible atomic parts (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
A moving point must touch every part of a line, proving the line is made of points.

Razi presents a geometric argument to establish the existence of atoms. By analyzing how a cube's corner point traces a line, he shows that the only way to avoid a logical absurdity (the 'leap') is for the line to be composed of indivisible points. This proof is a cornerstone of Islamic atomism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the cube model
Visualize a cube with six finite surfaces, each surface bounded by four finite lines, each line ending in two points. When the cube is dragged, its corner point traces a line.
The point is the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) in motion.
2
Follow the logical deduction
Notice that the dragged point must meet all of the traced line – if it skipped any part, that would constitute a 'leap' (ṭafrah), which is impossible. Therefore, the point meets every part of the line.
Razi assumes the falsehood of the leap without proof here.
3
Apply the principle: point meets only point
Since a point can only contact another point, the entire line must be composed of points – nothing else can be contacted. Thus, the line is an aggregate of indivisible points.
This step links contact to composition.
4
Consider the finite/infinite dilemma
The points are either finite or infinite. If infinite, they cannot be traversed in finite time; if finite, the desired conclusion (atomism) is established. Either way, the argument holds.
This strengthens the proof by eliminating an alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة ، في أحياز متلاصقة : أن نقول : الجسم المكعب يحيط به سطوح ستة متناهية ، وكل واحد من تلك السطوح يحيط به خطوط أربعة متناهية ، وكل واحد من تلك الخطوط له طرفان ، هما نقطتان . فإذا انجر ذلك المكعب على سطح ، فقد انجرت تلك النقطة . فنقول : إن تلك النقطة لما انجرت على ذلك السطح إلى أخره : خط ، لا محالة . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن تلك النقطة ، لقيت جميع ذلك الخط . لأنها لو لقيت بعض أجزاء ذلك الخط ، ثم لقيت جزءاً آخر ، يباين الجزء الأول من غير أن يمر بما بينهما . فحينئذ يلزم القول بالطفرة . وإنه باطل فثبت : أن تلك النقطة لقيت جميع ذلك الخط . ومعلوم أن النقطة لا تلاقي إلا النقطة . فلما ثبت أن تلك النقطة لقيت كل الخط ، وثبت : أن النقطة لا تلاقي إلا النقطة ، لزم القطع بأن ذلك الخط متألف من النقط المتشافعة المتلاصقة . وذلك يوجب تألف الخط من النقط التي لا تتجزا وذلك يفيد المطلوب. ثم نقول : تلك النقط إن كانت غير متناهية ، قطعها في زمان متناهي. وإن كانت متناهية العدد فذلك هو المطلوب فيمتنع.
The second proof in explanation that motion is successive obtainings in contiguous places: we say: The cube is surrounded by six finite surfaces, and each of those surfaces is surrounded by four finite lines, and each of those lines has two ends, which are two points. So when that cube is dragged on a surface, that point is dragged. We say: That point, when dragged on that surface to its end, inevitably traces a line. Once this is established, we say: That point met that entire line. Because if it had met only some parts of that line, then met another part that is distinct from the first without passing through what is between them, then at that point it would be necessary to affirm the leap (ṭafrah), which is false. So it is established that that point met all of that line. And it is known that a point only meets a point. Thus, when it is established that that point met all the line, and it is established that a point only meets a point, it is necessarily concluded that that line is composed of mutually contiguous points. That necessitates the line being composed of points that are not further divisible, and that yields the desired conclusion. Then we say: Those points, if they are infinite, then it is impossible for them to be traversed in a finite time; and if they are finite in number, that is the desired conclusion, so it is impossible.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of atomic parts (jawhar fard). The argument uses a geometric model: a cube dragged on a surface causes one of its corner points to trace a line. Since the point must contact every part of that line to avoid a leap (ṭafrah), and a point only contacts a point, the line must be composed of points. These points are either finite or infinite. If infinite, traversing them in finite time is impossible; if finite, the desired conclusion is reached. The proof thus establishes that the line is made of indivisible atoms.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
النقطة
الخط
الطفرة
المتلاصقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A moving point might skip over parts of a line, allowing the line to be continuous rather than atomic.
Correction: Razi argues that skipping parts (the leap) is impossible; therefore the point must contact every part, proving the line is made of points.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, what would occur if a moving point did NOT meet every part of the line?
A) The cube would break.
B) A leap (ṭafrah) would be required, which is impossible.
C) The point would move infinitely fast.
D) The line would be composed of atoms automatically.

Key Takeaway
By showing that a moving point must contact every part of a line, Razi proves that a line is composed of indivisible atomic points.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that a moving point cannot skip over any part of a line—it must meet every part. In your own life, where have you tried to 'leap' over a truth or avoid confronting something, only to realize that you must face every part of it? What step are you avoiding right now that you know you must take?
?
If every point on a line must be traversed, no part is irrelevant. What does this teach you about the moments of your day? Are there 'small' moments you treat as if they don't matter, even though they are all points that make up the line of your life before Allah? How will you change your attention to them starting today?
?
The proof concludes that the points must be finite—they cannot go on forever. Reflect on your own accountability: your deeds are finite, each one counted. Do you live as if every action is a point that will be met by Allah's justice? What would it mean for you to truly believe that nothing is skipped over—not a single word, thought, or deed?

Refinement of the Second Proof: Instantaneous Contact
Razi explains how to present the proof without need for motion and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refines the second proof for the indivisible atom by focusing on the instantaneous nature of contact and separation, without requiring motion or time.

Core Idea
Contact between points is instantaneous, not gradual.

Razi presents a refined version of the proof for the indivisible atom. He argues that the contact and separation of points happen instantly, which avoids the need to invoke motion or time. This strengthens the case for atomism by focusing purely on the geometry of points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the second proof for the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) originally involved motion and time. Razi now shows a more elegant formulation that dispenses with those concepts.
This refinement makes the proof more fundamental.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi begins by stating the aim: a proof without needing motion and time.
3
Identify the key claims
Razi claims that contact between a moving point and a point on the distance occurs instantaneously (دفعة). Similarly, the removal of that contact is instantaneous. Identify these two parallel statements.
The reasoning is that gradual contact would divide the point, which is impossible.
4
Follow the argument about intermediates
After the first contact ends, a new contact begins with another point. Razi argues that no intermediate can exist between these two points, because any intermediate would either be a point (acceptable) or divisible (impossible).
This supports the conclusion that space is composed of indivisible points.
5
Grasp the overall conclusion
Understand that this refined proof establishes the atom (جوهر فرد) without appealing to motion or time, using only the instantaneous contact and separation of points.
This is a methodological improvement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنك إذا أوردت هذا الكلام على هذا الوجه ، كان ذلك دليلاً على إثبات الجوهر الفرد ، من غير حاجة إلى بيان الحركة ، والزمان . أما إذا قلنا : إن تلك النقطة المنجرة ، إن لقيت نقطة من المسافة . فحصول ذلك اللقاء يكون دفعة ، إذا لو حصل ذلك اللقاء على التدريج لانقسمت النقطة . وهو محال . وكما كان حصول تلك الملاقاة دفعة ، فكذلك زوالها يكون دفعة . وإلا لعاد المحذور المذكور . وعند زوال الملاقاة الأولى ، تحصل الملاقاة أيضاً بنقطة أخرى ، لعين الدليل المذكور ، ويمتنع أن يحصل بين هاتين النقطتين شيء يتوسطهما . لأن ذلك المتوسط ، إن كان نقطة ، فذلك لا يضرنا . لأنا بينا : أن حصول ملاقاة النقطة يكون دفعة وعدمه أيضاً يكون دفعة وإن كان شيئاً منقسماً ، فذلك محال . لأن النقطة يمتنع أن تلاقي إلا النقطة
And know: If you present this speech in this manner, it is a proof for establishing the indivisible atom without needing to explain motion and time. But if we say: That dragged point, if it meets a point of the distance, then the occurrence of that meeting is instantaneous, because if that meeting occurred gradually, the point would divide—which is impossible. And just as the occurrence of that meeting is instantaneous, so also its removal is instantaneous; otherwise the aforementioned difficulty would return. And when the first meeting ceases, a meeting also occurs with another point, by the same argument mentioned, and it is impossible for something to be intermediate between these two points. Because that intermediate, if it is a point, then that does not harm us, for we have shown that the occurrence of the meeting of a point is instantaneous and its non-occurrence is also instantaneous; and if it is something divisible, that is impossible, because a point cannot meet anything but a point.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a methodological refinement of the second proof. It explains that the argument can be presented without invoking motion and time, focusing solely on the instantaneous nature of a point's contact with another point. The key idea is that contact and separation happen instantly; otherwise the point would be divisible. The absence of any intermediate between two points (when one contact ends and another begins) further supports that space consists of indivisible parts. This strengthens the claim that the proof for the atom is independent of considerations of motion and time.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
اللقاء
الدفعة
النقطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for the indivisible atom necessarily relies on the concepts of motion and time.
Correction: Razi shows that the proof can be formulated solely on the basis of instantaneous contact and separation, thereby eliminating any dependence on motion or time.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim about the meeting of a point with another point?
A) It occurs gradually.
B) It occurs instantaneously.
C) It cannot occur.
D) It requires motion.

Key Takeaway
Razi refines the proof for the indivisible atom by showing that contact and separation between points are instantaneous, making the argument independent of motion and time.

Proof That Change in Quality Occurs in Indivisible Instants
The author proves change requires discrete instants via a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical dilemma about blackening a body demonstrates that all change, including change in quality and motion in place, must occur in discrete indivisible instants.

Core Idea
Change cannot be gradual; it must happen in indivisible instants.

This passage presents a key kalam proof that change in quality—like a body turning black—cannot occur gradually. The author uses a logical dilemma to show that a single attribute can only come into existence or cease at a single, indivisible moment. This conclusion leads to the doctrine of the succession of instants, which then applies to motion in place as well. Let's work through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a series of dilemmas.
The argument uses the example of blackness (sawād).
2
Understand the initial dilemma
The author asks: when the body begins to blacken, does any blackness occur? If no blackness occurs, it remains unchanged. If some occurs, does any remain? Work through the possibilities.
This is a classic 'if-then' logical analysis.
3
Follow the impossibility of gradual existence
If a part of blackness occurs and another part remains, then the thing that occurred is either identical to what remains (contradiction) or different (then each exists wholly). This shows that a single thing cannot exist or cease gradually.
The key is the impossibility of one thing being both existent and non-existent.
4
Connect to instants and motion
From the impossibility of gradual change, the author concludes that each attribute exists only for a single instant, necessitating a succession of instants. This then applies to motion in place.
See how the argument builds from quality to motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولتقرر هذا الكلام في الكيف، ليظهر وجه الكلام فيه: فنقول: الجسم في أول ما يأخذ في الاسوداد. فهل حصل فيه شيء من السواد، أو لم يحصل؟ فإن لم يحصل، فهو بعد باقي على العدم الأصلي. وإن حصل فيه شيء، فهل بقي من ذلك السواد شيء، أو لم يبق؟ فإن تم ولم يبق منه شيء، فقد كان ذلك الحدوث والحصول واقعاً دفعة. وإن بقي منه شيء، فالذي وجد، إن كان عين الذي بقى، فالشيء الواحد يصدق عليه: أنه وجد، وأنه بعد لم يوجد. وذلك محال. وإن كان غيره، فحينئذ الذي وجد، فقد وجد بتمامه، والذي بعد لم يوجد، فهو معدوم بتمامه. وذلك يدل على أن الشيء الواحد، لا يوجد ولا يعدم، إلا دفعة واحدة. إذا ثبت هذا، فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال: إن ذلك التغير والتبدل والحركة: عبارة عن صفات متعاقبة متوالية، كل واحد منها لا يوجد إلا أناً واحداً. وذلك يوجب القول بتتالي الآنات. ومتى ثبت هذا، وجب أن تكون الحركة في الأين: عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة، غير منقسمة. وذلك هو المطلوب.
And to confirm this statement regarding quality, so that the aspect of the statement in it becomes clear: We say: The body at the first moment it begins to blacken. Did any part of blackness occur in it, or did it not occur? If it did not occur, then it remains in its original non-existence. And if some part occurred in it, then did any of that blackness remain, or did it not remain? If it completed and nothing remained of it, then that occurrence and attainment happened at once. And if something remained of it, then that which came into existence, if it is identical to that which remained, then the one thing is described as having come into existence and as not yet having come into existence. That is impossible. And if it is other than it, then that which came into existence has come into existence in its entirety, and that which has not yet come into existence is entirely non-existent. That indicates that a single thing does not come into existence nor cease to exist except at a single moment. If this is established, then it follows that it must be said that that change, alteration, and motion are expressions of successive, consecutive attributes, each of which does not exist except for a single instant. That necessitates the assertion of the succession of instants. And when this is established, it becomes necessary that motion in place (al-ayn) is an expression of successive, indivisible occurrences. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a proof. The author begins by establishing the argument in the context of quality (al-kayf), specifically the example of a body becoming black. He poses a dilemma: when a body first begins to blacken, either some blackness occurs or it does not. If no blackness occurs, the body remains in its original state of non-existence of blackness. If some blackness occurs, then it either remains or does not. If it does not remain, the change occurred instantaneously. If some remains, then the part that came into existence is either identical to the remainder (impossible, as one thing cannot both have come and not come) or different, in which case the part that came exists wholly and the part that has not yet come is wholly non-existent. This shows that a single thing can only come into or cease to exist at one moment. From this, the author concludes that change, alteration, and motion consist of successive attributes, each existing only for an instant. This necessitates the succession of instants (tatālī al-ānāt). Finally, he extends this to motion in place (al-ayn), which must be a succession of indivisible occurrences. The proof follows the author's own order: a dilemma, its analysis, and the conclusion.

Key Terms
الكيف (al-kayf)
السواد (al-sawād)
الآن (al-ān)
تتالي الآنات (tatālī al-ānāt)
الحركة في الأين (al-ḥarakah fī al-ayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is too abstract to apply to real change.
Correction: The proof uses a concrete example of a body turning black to show a logical necessity—the impossibility of gradual existence of a single attribute. It is a fundamental metaphysical principle.

Checkpoint
According to the author's argument, why can a single thing not come into existence gradually?
A) Because gradual existence would require it to be both existent and non-existent at the same time.
B) Because blackness cannot be divided.
C) Because the body remains in its original state.
D) Because motion is continuous.

Key Takeaway
All change, whether in quality or motion, must occur in indivisible instants because gradual change leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a single attribute cannot be partially existent—it either is or is not. In your own spiritual life, is there any area where you are trying to hold onto both obedience and disobedience at the same time? What moment of decision is God calling you to make now?
?
The proof concludes that change must happen in an indivisible instant. Have you been putting off a decisive change in your character or repentance, thinking you can gradually phase out sin? What would it look like for you to embrace that instantaneous turning today?
?
If your entire existence were reduced to a single instant of moral choice, what would that instant reveal about your true priorities before Allah? How can you align your next 'instant' with His command?

Refutation of al-Farabi's Claim about Instants
The author refutes al-Farabi's potential instants argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that instants in time must be actually distinct, not merely potential.

Core Idea
Instants are actual because they contain distinct attributes.

This passage presents a sharp refutation of al-Farabi's view that the instants involved in motion exist only potentially. The author argues that the actual succession of different attributes in each instant forces us to conclude that the instants themselves are distinct in actuality. This demonstrates how careful analysis of motion leads to necessary conclusions about the nature of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: report of opponent's view, rejection, reasoning.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify al-Farabi's claim that instants are potential, and the author's opposing claim that they are actual.
The author explicitly says 'This answer is not fitting for one like him.'
3
Analyze the reasoning
Trace the logical chain: during motion, different attributes succeed each other contiguously; each instant is a container for a unique attribute; therefore the instants must be distinct in actuality, not potentially.
Pay attention to the terms 'تعاقب' (succession) and 'تلاصق' (contiguity).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this argument shows that the potential/actual distinction applies to instants themselves, not just to attributes.
The author ends with 'That is the desired result.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن الشيخ أبا نصر الفارابي، قد دار حول هذا البحث في كتاب: التعليقات، ثم أجاب عنه. فقال: إن تلك الآنات موجودة بالقوة لا بالفعل. وأقول: هذا الجواب غير لائق بمثله. وذلك لأنه لما ثبت أنه حصل في زمان الحركة: صفات مختلفة بالماهية على سبيل التعاقب والتلاصق. فكل واحد من تلك الآنات، مختص بكونه ظرفاً ووعاء لصفة أخرى، مخالفة للصفة الحاصلة في الآن الثاني. وذلك يوجب القطع بتغاير الآنات على سبيل الفعل، لا على سبيل القوة. وذلك هو المطلوب والله أعلم.
And know that the master Abū Naṣr al-Fārābī discussed this research in the book al-Taʿlīqāt, then responded to it. He said: These instants exist potentially, not actually. And I say: This answer is not fitting for one like him. That is because when it is established that during the time of motion there occurred attributes different in essence (al-māhiyyah) in a successive and contiguous manner, each one of those instants is specifically a container and vessel for another attribute, contrary to the attribute occurring in the second instant. That necessitates the decisive judgment that the instants are distinct actually, not potentially. That is what is sought, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author first mentions al-Fārābī's position from his book al-Taʿlīqāt: that the instants (al-ānāt) involved in motion exist potentially (bi-l-quwwah), not actually (bi-l-fiʿl). The author then states that this answer is not fitting for someone of al-Fārābī's stature. The reason: it has been established that during the time of motion, different attributes succeed each other in a contiguous manner. Each instant is uniquely a vessel for a specific attribute, different from the attribute of the next instant. This distinctness of qualities in successive instants forces us to conclude that the instants themselves are distinct in actuality, not merely potential. Otherwise, the actual distinction between the attributes would be unexplained. The author concludes that this is the desired result. The commentary follows the order: reporting the opponent's view, stating its inadequacy, and providing the logical reason.

التعاقب (al-taʿāqub)
التلاصق (al-talāṣuq)
الماهية (al-māhiyyah)

Correction: The author provides a reasoned objection: the actual succession of attributes proves the instants are actually distinct.

Checkpoint

B) Because the distinct attributes in each instant prove the instants are actually distinct.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of successive attributes in motion proves that instants are actually distinct, not merely potential.

First Proof that Time Is Composed of Successive Instants
The author proves the indivisibility of the present instant as the first support for the composite nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the present instant is indivisible and how this supports the claim that time is composed of instants.

Core Idea
The present instant cannot be divided, or it would cease to be present.

This lesson presents the first of several proofs that time is not continuous but composed of discrete instants. The author begins by establishing the necessity of the present instant's existence, then demonstrates that it cannot be divided into parts. Understanding this argument is key to the Islamic theological view of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the style of argumentation in kalam.
Pay attention to the logical steps: first existence, then indivisibility.
2
Identify the main claim
Identify the overall claim about time and the specific proof being presented.
The proof aims to show that time is composed of instants.
3
Follow the logic
Trace each step: why must the present instant exist? Then why can't it be divided?
The author uses necessary knowledge and hypothetical reasoning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this proof supports the view that time is composed of instants.
The indivisibility of the present instant implies time is discrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من مطالب هذا الفصل في بيان أن الزمان يجب أن يكون مركباً من الآنات المتتالية، والدفعات المتعاقبة: فنقول: الذي يدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: إنا نعلم بالضرورة: أن الآن الحاضر موجود. لأنه لو لم يكن موجوداً، لامتنع أن يصير ماضياً أو مستقبلاً. ولأنا نعلم: أن الآن الحاضر نهاية للماضي، وبداية للمستقبل. ولو لم يكن موجوداً، لكان إما ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، والماضي لا يكون نهاية للماضي، والمستقبل لا يكون نهاية للماضي، والمستقبل لا يكون بداية للمستقبل. ولأنا نعلم حضور الشيء في الآن، وذلك يتوقف على حصول الآن. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: هذا الآن الحاضر. إما أن يفترض فيه قسمان، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر، وإما أن يمتنع ذلك. والأول باطل وإلا لكان عند حصول النصف الأول، لم يكن النصف الثاني حاصلاً، وعند حصول النصف الثاني يكون النصف الأول فائتاً. فيلزم أن لا يكون الحاضر: حاضراً. هذا خلف. فثبت: أنه لا يقبل هذا النوع من القسمة. ثم إن عدم هذا الآن. إما أن يكون على التدريج أو دفعة. والأول باطل لفساد ذلك.
The second type of the requirements of this chapter is to explain that time must be composed of successive instants and consecutive moments. We say: What indicates this are [several] aspects. The first proof: We know by necessity that the present instant exists. Because if it did not exist, it would be impossible for it to become past or future. And because we know that the present instant is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. And if it did not exist, it would be either past or future, and the past cannot be the end of the past, and the future cannot be the end of the past, and the future cannot be the beginning of the future. And because we know the presence of a thing in an instant, and that depends on the occurrence of the instant. If this is established, we say: This present instant either can be conceived as having two parts such that one precedes the other, or it cannot be so. The first is false; otherwise, when the first half occurs, the second half has not occurred, and when the second half occurs, the first half has passed. Thus it would follow that the present is not present. This is a contradiction. So it is established that it does not admit this type of division. Then the non-existence of this instant either occurs gradually or instantaneously. The first is false due to its corruption.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (al-nawʿ al-thānī) of the chapter, which aims to prove that time (al-zamān) is composed of successive instants and consecutive moments. The author states that there are multiple proofs (wujūh), and presents the first proof (al-ḥujjah al-ūlā). The proof begins by establishing, through necessary knowledge (ḍarūrah), that the present instant exists. The argument: if it did not exist, it could not become past or future. Also, the present instant is the end of the past and the beginning of the future; if it were non-existent, it would have to be either past or future, neither of which can serve as the boundary. Furthermore, the presence of a thing in an instant depends on the instant's existence. Having established existence, the author then asks whether the present instant admits division into two parts, one before the other. If it did, then when the first half exists, the second is not yet present, and when the second exists, the first has passed, so the present would not be truly present – a contradiction. Thus it is indivisible. Finally, the author considers whether the non-existence (passing away) of this instant is gradual or instantaneous; the first is false (due to the same reasoning). The text cuts off at 'li-fasād dhālika', but the main conclusion is clear: the present instant is indivisible, supporting the overall claim that time is composed of indivisible instants.

الزمان
الآنات المتتالية
الدفعات المتعاقبة
الضرورة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is about physical measurement of time.
Correction: It is a logical argument about the conceptual nature of the present moment as an indivisible boundary.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason the author gives for the present instant not being divisible?
A) Because it is too small to divide.
B) Because if divided, one part would be past and the other future, so the present would not be present.
C) Because division would require infinite time.
D) Because the present instant is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The present instant is indivisible because division would negate its presence, establishing that time is composed of discrete instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe the present instant is indivisible and escapes your grasp. How often do you miss the present by dividing your attention between regrets of the past and anxieties of the future? What one habit will you change today to live in the indivisible now?
?
If your present deeds are the only thing that truly exist before God, why do you spend so much time in what is already past or not yet happened? Can you commit to being fully present in your next prayer, focusing only on this instant?
?
The proof shows that the present instant must exist, yet it vanishes in an instant. Are you treating each passing instant as a sacred opportunity that cannot be retrieved? What will you do with this present moment that you have right now?

Time as Successive Instants – First Proof
First proof for time as successive instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam proof that time is composed of successive, indivisible instants, based on the necessary existence and indivisibility of the present moment.

Core Idea
The present instant is indivisible; its passing must be instantaneous, forcing a succession of instants.

This passage presents the first of several proofs that time is composed of successive, indivisible instants. The argument begins with a straightforward necessity: the present instant must exist. From there, the author demonstrates that this instant cannot be divided into parts, and its disappearance must be instantaneous, leading inevitably to a sequence of discrete instants. Follow the reasoning closely — each step is tightly connected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage aims to prove that time is composed of successive instants. Locate the statement of the claim at the beginning.
3
Follow the proof for the existence of the present instant
The author gives three reasons why the present instant must exist. List them mentally.
These are based on its role as boundary between past and future.
4
Understand the indivisibility argument
The author shows that division would destroy the 'presentness' of the instant. Trace the contradiction.
Key: if divisible, no part is fully present.
5
See how instantaneous passing leads to succession
The instant's non-existence must be instantaneous (not gradual). This forces a next instant, and so on.
The same reasoning applies to each subsequent instant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من مطالب هذا الفصل في بيان أن الزمان يجب أن يكون مركباً من الآنات المتتالية ، والدفعات المتعاقبة : فنقول : الذي يدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الآن الحاضر موجود . لأنه لو لم يكن موجوداً ، لامتنع أن يصير ماضياً أو مستقبلاً . ولأنا نعلم : أن الآن الحاضر نهاية للماضي ، وبداية للمستقبل . ولو لم يكن موجوداً . لكان إما ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، والماضي لا يكون نهاية للماضي ، والمستقبل لا يكون نهاية للماضي ، والمستقبل لا يكون بداية للمستقبل. ولأنا نعلم حضور الشيء في الآن ، وذلك يتوقف على حصول الآن . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : هذا الآن الحاضر. إما أن يفترض فيه قسمان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر ، وإما أن يمتنع ذلك . والأول باطل وإلا لكان عند حصول النصف الأول ، لم يكن النصف الثاني حاصلاً ، وعند حصول النصف الثاني يكون النصف الأول فائتاً . فيلزم أن لا يكون الحاضر : حاضراً. هذا خلف . فثبت : أنه لا يقبل هذا النوع من القسمة . ثم إن عدم هذا الآن . إما أن يكون على التدريج أو دفعة . والأول باطل لوجوه : أحدهما : إنا وللنا على أن العدم على سبيل التدريج محال . وثانيها : إن بتقدير أن يعقل ذلك في الجملة ، إلا أنه ههنا غير معقول . وإلا لصار الآن منقسماً . وقد دللنا على أنه غير منقسم . وإذا بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن عدمه يكون دفعة . فيكون أن عدمه : متصلا بأن وجوده بعد تتالي أنات ثم الكلام في كيفية عدم الآن الثاني كما في الأول . وذلك يوجب تتالي الآنات . وهو المطلوب
The second type of the demands of this chapter is to explain that time must be composed of successive instants and consecutive moments. So we say: That which indicates it are several proofs. The first proof: We know by necessity that the present instant exists. Because if it were nonexistent, it would be impossible for it to become past or future. And because we know that the present instant is the endpoint of the past and the beginning of the future. And if it were nonexistent, it would be either past or future. But the past cannot be the endpoint of the past, and the future cannot be the endpoint of the past, and the future cannot be the beginning of the future. And because we know the presence of a thing in the instant, and that depends on the occurrence of the instant. If this is established, we say: This present instant is either divisible into two parts such that one precedes the other, or that is impossible. The first is false, because if the first half occurs, the second half has not occurred, and when the second half occurs, the first half has passed. Thus it follows that the present is not present; this is a contradiction. So it is established that it does not accept this type of division. Then the non-existence of this instant is either gradual or instantaneous. The first is false for two reasons: one, we have argued that gradual non-existence is impossible; second, even if it could be conceived in general, it is inconceivable here, otherwise the instant would become divisible, and we have shown that it is indivisible. Once this alternative is invalid, it is established that its non-existence is instantaneous. So its non-existence is contiguous with its existence after a succession of instants. Then the discussion about the non-existence of the second instant is like that of the first. And that necessitates the succession of instants. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of inquiry: proving that time is composed of successive, indivisible instants. The author presents the first proof, which is based on the necessary existence of the present instant. He argues that if the present instant did not exist, it could not become past or future, but we know it is the boundary between past and future. Furthermore, the presence of a thing in an instant requires the instant to exist. Then he shows that the present instant is indivisible (cannot be split into two parts), because if it were divisible, when the first half exists, the second half is not yet, and when the second half exists, the first is gone, so no actual present exists—contradiction. Thus the instant is indivisible. Then he argues that the passing away of the instant must be instantaneous (not gradual), because gradual passing would imply division of the instant. Since it passes instantaneously, its non-existence immediately follows its existence, leading to a sequence of successive instants. The same applies to the second instant, etc., thus establishing the required succession of instants.

حاضر
قسمة
تدريج
دفعة
تتالي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the present instant is a duration that can be divided into smaller parts.
Correction: The author proves that division would destroy the 'presentness', so the instant must be indivisible.

Checkpoint

A) Division would make it past or future
B) If divisible, when the first half exists, the second half does not, so no actual present
C) The instant is too small to divide
D) The mind cannot conceive division

Key Takeaway
Time must be composed of successive indivisible instants because the present instant cannot be divided and its passing is instantaneous, forcing a sequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each present instant is indivisible and vanishes instantaneously, how much of your current instant are you truly living for Allah? Are you fragmented across many concerns, or wholly present in this moment of worship or obedience?
?
Consider a sin you committed in a single past instant. That instant is gone forever. But the next instant is here. What one thing will you do in this instant to turn back to Allah before it too becomes irrevocable?
?
This proof shows that time is a chain of disappearing instants. You will never get a single instant back. If you knew that your next instant could be your last, would your priorities shift right now? What would you change in the present instant?

Refuting the Denial of the Present Instant
Refutes the objection that the present instant does not exist using mental judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the present instant lacks existence by invoking an analogy with mental judgments of necessity, possibility, and impossibility.

Core Idea
Mental judgments about existence do not imply external reality, as shown by the status of necessity, possibility, and impossibility.

The objection before us challenges the very existence of the present instant (al-ān). The author counters by drawing a parallel with how the mind judges things as necessary, possible, or impossible—judgments that do not make those categories real entities in the external world. This passage unpacks that analogy and defends it against the charge of infinite regress, clarifying the relationship between mental concepts and external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
First, identify the objection: 'We do not accept the existence of the instant.' Notice that the objector denies the reality of the present moment based on a logical principle.
The objection is a common one in kalam, often used to argue against temporal atomism.
2
Understand the author's analogy
The author responds by noting that the mind judges things as necessary, possible, or impossible, yet these judgments do not make those categories exist externally. This is the core of the refutation.
3
Analyze the infinite regress argument
If necessity were a real external attribute, it would itself be subject to the same categories, leading to infinite regress. Trace the logic: necessity would be either necessary or possible, requiring another necessity, etc.
The author then notes that this regress does not necessarily follow, subtly shifting the burden.
4
Consider the additional point about non-existence
The author adds that the intellect can judge something non-existent without making non-existence a real entity. This reinforces the original analogy.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the author uses conceptual distinctions (judgments vs. external reality) to defend the instant's existence. The key is that mental acts do not create external entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم وجود الآن . قوله : ( الذهن يحكم بتحققه وثبوته في نفسه ، قلنا : وكذلك الشيء قد يحكم الذهن والعقل عليه ، بكونه واجباً أو ممكناً أو ممتنعاً. أن الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع : مفهومات لا ثبوت لها في مع الخارج . وإلا لكانت إما واجبة أو ممكنة أو ممتنعة . فكان وجوبها وإمكانها وامتناعها زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل . مع أنه لا يلزم . وأيضاً : فالعقل قد يحكم بكون الشيء معدوماً في نفسه ، منه أن يكون العدم أمراً ثابتاً، لامتناع أن يكون أحد النقيضين عين الثاني .
If it is said: We do not accept the existence of the instant. His statement: 'The mind judges its actuality and its subsistence in itself,' we say: Similarly, the mind and reason may judge a thing to be necessary, possible, or impossible. Necessity, possibility, and impossibility are concepts that have no subsistence in external existence. Otherwise they would be either necessary, possible, or impossible, so their necessity, possibility, or impossibility would be additional to them, leading to infinite regress. Moreover, it does not lead to that. Also, the intellect may judge a thing to be nonexistent in itself, but that does not mean that non-existence is a fixed entity, because it is impossible for one of the two contradictories to be the other.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's response. The objector denies that the present instant exists, arguing that the mind's judgment of its existence does not prove actual existence. The author responds by analogy: the mind judges things as necessary, possible, or impossible, but these are mere concepts, not real entities. If they were real, they would themselves be subject to the same categories, leading to infinite regress. However, that regress does not necessarily follow. Additionally, the intellect can judge something as nonexistent, but that does not make non-existence a real thing, because two contradictories cannot be the same. Thus, the mind's judgment of the instant's existence does not entail that the instant itself is a real entity beyond the judgment.

Key Terms
ذهن
حكم
وجود
مفهوم
وجوب
إمكان
امتناع
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the mind judges something to exist, it must be a real external entity.
Correction: The author shows that the mind can judge things as necessary, possible, or impossible without those categories being real externally. Likewise, the judgment that the instant exists does not make it an external entity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of the concepts of necessity, possibility, and impossibility?
A) They are real entities in the external world
B) They are mental concepts with no external subsistence
C) They are attributes of God
D) They are the foundation of all existence

Key Takeaway
Mental judgments about existence, necessity, or nonexistence do not create external realities; they are conceptual assessments that do not imply ontological status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson shows that logical categories like necessity and possibility are mental. Does that realization shake the confidence you place in rational arguments for God's existence? How can you rely on reasoning without falling into the error of reifying mental concepts?

The Now's Nonexistence Is Instantaneous
Two proofs that the now's nonexistence cannot be gradual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the 'now' cannot cease to exist gradually and must disappear all at once, based on its indivisibility.

Core Idea
The now is indivisible, so its nonexistence cannot be gradual.

The author presents two concise proofs that the 'now' (al-ān) must cease to exist instantaneously. The first proof asserts that gradual nonexistence is impossible in principle. The second proof shows that even if gradual nonexistence were conceivable elsewhere, it cannot apply to the now because that would imply divisibility of what is proven indivisible. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the nature of time in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the two proofs introduced by 'One of them...' and 'And the second...'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine the first proof
The author states that gradual nonexistence is impossible. Consider why gradual cessation might be problematic for a simple entity like the now.
3
Examine the second proof
Even if gradual nonexistence were allowed in general, it cannot apply here because it would make the now divisible. Since the now is indivisible (a previously established fact), gradualness is impossible.
This proof relies on the indivisibility of the now.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the now's nonexistence is instantaneous. Moreover, this leads to a succession of nows, each ceasing instantaneously, which is the desired conclusion.
The argument supports a theory of time as discrete moments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : إنا وللنا على أن العدم على سبيل التدريج محال . وثانيها : إن بتقدير أن يعقل ذلك في الجملة ، إلا أنه ههنا غير معقول . وإلا لصار الآن منقسماً . وقد دللنا على أنه غير منقسم . وإذا بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن عدمه يكون دفعة . فيكون أن عدمه : متصلا بأن وجوده بعد تتالي أنات ثم الكلام في كيفية عدم الآن الثاني كما في الأول . وذلك يوجب تتالي الآنات . وهو المطلوب
One of them: We hold that nonexistence in a gradual manner is impossible. And the second: Even if that is conceivable in general, it is inconceivable here. Otherwise the now would become divisible. And we have proven that it is indivisible. And if this possibility is invalidated, it is established that its nonexistence is instantaneous. So its nonexistence is connected to its existence after a succession of moments. Then the discussion concerning the manner of the nonexistence of the second now is like that of the first. And that necessitates a succession of moments. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the nonexistence of the 'now' (al-ān) cannot occur gradually and must be instantaneous. The author presents two arguments. First: We hold that nonexistence in a gradual manner is impossible. Second: Even if gradual nonexistence were conceivable in general, it is not conceivable here, because if the now disappeared gradually, it would become divisible, but it has been proven that the now is indivisible. Therefore, when gradual nonexistence is ruled out, it follows that the now's nonexistence occurs all at once. Consequently, the nonexistence of the now is immediately connected to its existence after a succession of moments. The same reasoning applies to the second now, leading to an infinite succession of nows, which is the desired conclusion.

العدم

Misconception: The now could cease to exist gradually, like a period of time fading away.
Correction: The now is indivisible; gradual cessation would require parts, which is impossible. Therefore its nonexistence is instantaneous.

A) The now is indivisible.
B) The now is eternal.
C) The now is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The now's indivisibility forces its nonexistence to be instantaneous, grounding a discrete view of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each 'now' in your life disappears instantaneously, never to return, how are you using the present indivisible moment to draw closer to your Creator? Write one concrete action you will take in this very now.
?
The proof argues that the now's nonexistence must be instantaneous because it is indivisible. Do you live as if each moment is a complete gift from Allah, indivisible and unrepeatable? What thought pattern or habit prevents you from treasuring each now as a fresh creation?
?
Just as the now ceases instantly only to be followed by another, your life is a succession of nows. If death were to come in the next now, would your current preoccupation be worthy of that final instant? What would you change right now?

Refuting the Objection: The Now's Existence Through Mental Judgment
Refutation of the objection against the now's existence via mental judgment analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the 'now' does not exist by distinguishing between mental judgments and external reality, and by using the example of modal concepts to avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
Mental existence does not entail external existence, just like modal concepts.

This lesson addresses a key objection in the discussion of time: if the 'now' has no real existence outside the mind, how can we speak of it? The author presents a clever response using analogy with the three rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible). By showing that even modal concepts exist only in the mind without causing infinite regress, he defends the reality of the now as a mental concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the opponent's claim that the now does not exist. The opponent quotes the author's own statement about the mind judging its actuality.
2
Identify the author's response
Notice how the author uses the mind's judgment on modal concepts as an analogy to defend the mental existence of the now.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
See what would happen if modal concepts had external existence: they would require superadded properties leading to infinite regress.
4
Connect to the now
Apply the same logic to defend the mental existence of the now: the mind's judgment does not imply external reality, but it is sufficient for conceptual reasoning.
5
Reflect on the significance
Understand that mental existence is sufficient for rational discourse about the now, just as it is for modal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم وجود الآن . قوله : ( الذهن يحكم بتحققه وثبوته في نفسه )، قلنا : وكذلك الشيء قد يحكم الذهن والعقل عليه ، بكونه واجباً أو ممكناً أو ممتنعاً. أن الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع : مفهومات لا ثبوت لها في مع الخارج . وإلا لكانت إما واجبة أو ممكنة أو ممتنعة . فكان وجوبها وإمكانها وامتناعها زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل مع أنه لا يلزم . وأيضاً : فالعقل قد يحكم بكون الشيء معدوماً في نفسه ، منه أن يكون العدم أمراً ثابتاً، لامتناع أن يكون أحد النقيضين عين الثاني .
For it is said: We do not affirm the existence of the now. His saying: "The mind judges its actuality and subsistence in itself." We say: Likewise, the mind and intellect may judge a thing to be necessary, possible, or impossible. That necessity, possibility, and impossibility are concepts that have no subsistence in external reality. Otherwise they would be either necessary, possible, or impossible. Thus their necessity, possibility, and impossibility would be superadded to them, leading to infinite regress—yet it does not lead. Also, the intellect may judge a thing to be nonexistent in itself, such that nonexistence is an established matter, because it is impossible for one of two contradictories to be identical to the second.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent objects: 'We do not affirm the existence of the now.' The opponent quotes the author's statement that 'the mind judges its actuality and subsistence in itself.' The author responds: Similarly, the mind and intellect can judge a thing as necessary, possible, or impossible. But these judgments are merely concepts with no external subsistence; otherwise they would entail infinite regress. The author then adds: Also, the intellect may judge something to be nonexistent, but that does not make nonexistence a positive attribute, because one of two contradictories cannot be the other. Thus the mental existence of the now is not proof against its real existence.

Key Terms

الوجوب
الإمكان
الامتناع
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental judgments about existence imply real existence.
Correction: The author distinguishes between mental concepts and external reality; modal concepts are valid in the mind without external subsistence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the opponent's analogy between the now and modal concepts?
A) Because modal concepts have no external subsistence.
B) Because the now is a mental concept.
C) Because modal concepts are eternal.
D) Because the now is divisible.

Key Takeaway
The now's mental existence is sufficient for rational discourse, just as modal concepts are valid mental judgments without external subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm something in your heart about Allah—such as His existence or attributes—do you ever assume that your mental conviction alone is insufficient? In what area of your faith do you demand external proof beyond what your intellect can judge, and does this lesson challenge that demand?
?
The author argues that the intellect can judge nonexistence without that judgment being a positive attribute. Consider a time you felt the weight of a sin's nonexistence in your repentance. Did you treat that absence as a real 'thing', or did you allow your mental turning away to suffice? How can you apply this distinction to lighten your heart's burden?
?
If mental judgments about modal concepts (necessary, possible, impossible) are valid without external reality, then your understanding of tawheed—Allah as the only Necessary Being—rests on a rational judgment. Does this reliance on mental existence make your belief weaker or stronger? Confront honestly: have you ever doubted because you couldn't 'see' or 'feel' Allah's necessity? What does this lesson say about the sufficiency of that inner judgment?

The Second Objection: Does the Now's Existence Follow?
The opponent presents the second objection with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the argument from temporal existence, which argues that even if something exists in the now, it does not necessitate the now's existence, and you will see the two proofs offered by the opponent.

Core Idea
Existence in a now does not require the now itself to exist as a substance.

In this passage, the opponent raises a second objection: even if we grant that a thing exists in the now, it does not follow that the now itself exists. The opponent provides two proofs to show the impossibility. This objection challenges a key premise in the author's argument about the nature of time and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the opponent structures the objection: he concedes the thing exists in the now, but questions the conclusion.
The objection is based on a logical inference.
2
Identify the two proofs
The opponent gives two proofs. Find each and note how they both conclude that the now cannot exist in a now without contradiction.
The first proof relates to God's existence, the second to the now itself.
3
Consider the implications
Think about why these proofs are considered impossible. What does the opponent assume about existence and time?
This will be addressed in the author's later response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أن الشيء وجد في الآن . فلم قلتم : ( إن صدق هذا ، يقتضي كون الآن موجوداً ؟ )، والدليل عليه : وجهان : الأول : إنه تعالى يصدق عليه أنه موجود الآن في الآن والزمان . ع أنه يمتنع وقوع ذاته والثاني : إن نفس الآن موجود ، مع أنه يمتنع أن يقال : إن الآن وجد في الآن . إلا لزم كون الشيء : موجوداً في نفسه ، أو يلزم التسلسل . وكلاهما محال . السؤال الثالث : سلّمنا أن الآن موجود في اعيان . فلم قلتم : « إن عدمه . إما أن يكون دفعة أو على التدريج ؟ ، بل ههنا قسم ثالث ، وهو أن يكون عدمه حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده. ولا يقال : ليس كلامنا في عدم الآن ، بل في أول علم الآن. ومعلوم أن أول عدم الآن ، يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده. بل لا بد وأن يكون أول ذلك العدم واقعاً إما دفعة ، وإما على التدريج . لأنا نقول : هذا التقسيم إنما يصح لو كان لعدمه أول ، يكون هو فيه معدوماً ، وهذا ممنوع عندنا . بل عندنا : ظرف زمان عدمه : هو الآن الذي هو غير آن وجوده . فأما فرض آن آخر ، عقيب وجوده ، حتى يكون ظرفاً لزمان عدمه. فهذا إنما يصح لو ثبت القول بجواز تتالي الآنات . وذلك عين المطلوب . هذا غاية ما يمكن أن يقال من جانبهم . والله أعلم والجواب : قوله : « الآن له وجود في الذهن ، وذلك يكفي في صدق قولنا : وجد الآن ، وفي صدق قولنا : وجد الشيء في الآن ، قلنا : ما الذي أردت بقولك : ( وجد الآن في الذهن ؟ ، إن عنيت به أنه وجد العلم به في الذهن . فهذا حق . وإن عنيت به أن حضور الشيء في نفسه ، لا حصول له إلا في الذهن . فهذا جهل عظيم . لأنه لو عدمت الأذهان والأفهام ، فإن وجود الأشياء وحضورها في أنفسها : أمر حاصل متحقق . وأما حديث الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع ، فهو تشكيك في البديهيات . قوله : الباري موجود ، وليس في الآن . وكذلك الآن موجود ، وليس في الآن ، قلنا : نحن ندرك التفرقة بين ما هو موجود على سبيل الحقيقة ، وبين الذي كان موجوداً ، أو سيصير موجوداً . ولا شك أن الباري تعالى موجود على سبيل
The second question: We grant that the thing existed in the now. So why do you say: 'If this is true, it necessitates that the now exists?' And the proof for it is two aspects: First: God Most High is described as existing now in time and space—that His essence cannot occur. Second: The now itself exists, while it is impossible to say that the now existed in the now, unless it would entail that the thing exists in itself, or infinite regress. Both are impossible. The third question: We grant that the now exists in re. So why do you say: 'Its nonexistence is either instantaneous or gradual?' Rather, there is a third division: that its nonexistence occurs in all the time that follows. It is not said: 'Our discussion is not about the nonexistence of the now, but about the first moment of its nonexistence.' And it is known that the first moment of the now's nonexistence cannot occur in all the time that follows. Rather, that first nonexistence must occur either instantaneously or gradually. For we say: This division is only valid if its nonexistence has a first moment in which it is nonexistent, and this is denied by us. Rather, according to us, the time of its nonexistence is the now that is other than the now of its existence. As for positing another now after its existence so that it is the time of its nonexistence, this is only valid if the permissibility of the succession of nows is proven. That is exactly the desired conclusion. This is the utmost that can be said from their side. And God knows best. And the answer: His saying: 'The now has existence in the mind, and that suffices for the truth of our statement: the now existed, and for the truth of our statement: the thing existed in the now.' We say: What did you mean by your statement: 'The now existed in the mind?' If you mean that knowledge of it existed in the mind, that is true. But if you mean that the presence of the thing in itself has no occurrence except in the mind, that is great ignorance. Because if minds and intellects were absent, the existence of things and their presence in themselves is an actual, realized matter. As for the discussion of necessity, possibility, and impossibility, that is a raising of doubts about self-evident truths. His saying: 'The Creator exists, and not in the now. Similarly, the now exists, and not in the now.' We say: We perceive the distinction between what exists in the manner of truth and what existed or will exist. There is no doubt that God Most High exists in the manner...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage contains two objections from the opponent followed by the beginning of the author's comprehensive answer. The second objection argues that even if something exists in the now, that does not necessitate that the now itself exists; the opponent provides two proofs: first, God exists but not in a now, and second, the now cannot itself exist in a now without infinite regress. The third objection presents a third alternative: the now's nonexistence could occur in all subsequent time, not gradually or instantly. The opponent rejects the division into instantaneous or gradual because they deny a first moment of nonexistence. The author then begins his answer, focusing on the opponent's claim that the now's existence in the mind suffices. He distinguishes between knowledge of the now in the mind (which he accepts) and the absurd claim that things only exist in the mind. He also dismisses the analogy to modal concepts as a confusion of self-evident truths. The answer is cut off at the end of the page.

التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that if something exists in a now, then the now itself must be a real entity.
Correction: The opponent argues that this leads to contradictions, so the now need not be an existent thing.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof the opponent gives in the second objection?
A) God cannot exist in a now.
B) The now cannot exist in itself without infinite regress.
C) The thing that exists in the now must be eternal.
D) The now is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that accepting existence in a now does not logically force us to conclude that the now itself exists as a real entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the first proof: God is described as existing now, yet His essence does not occur in time. Does your prayer life reflect a belief that God is present with you in this moment, or do you treat Him as distant? How can you increase your awareness of His immediate presence without confining Him to your concept of 'now'?
?
The second proof shows that treating the 'now' as a substance leads to contradiction. In your own life, do you cling to certain moments as if they define you? How can you let go of holding onto the past or future as real substances and trust that only God is truly constant?
?
The opponent's objection ultimately defends the transcendence of God. When you think about God's attribute of eternity, do you imagine it as endless time or as beyond time altogether? What practical difference would it make in your devotion if you understood eternity as God's own mode of existence, not related to our 'now'?

The Third Objection and the Author's Initial Response
The opponent's third objection and the author's initial response (cut off).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that proposes a third alternative for the nonexistence of the now, and the beginning of the author's response focusing on the mental existence of the now.

Core Idea
The opponent introduces a third division for the now's nonexistence, and the author begins by distinguishing real existence from mental existence.

In this passage, the opponent presents a third objection: even if the now exists, its nonexistence need not be instantaneous or gradual; it could be that it is nonexistent in all subsequent time. The opponent then argues that this third alternative is impossible because it requires a first moment of nonexistence, which they deny. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the now's existence is purely mental and has no reality outside the mind.
Correction: The author argues that the now has an objective reality, though it is a time-point, not a substance.

Checkpoint
What third alternative does the opponent propose for the nonexistence of the now?
A) Gradual nonexistence.
B) Instantaneous nonexistence.
C) Nonexistence in all subsequent time.
D) Nonexistence in another now.

Key Takeaway
The third objection attempts to introduce a third possibility for the now's nonexistence, but the author's response begins by affirming the objective reality of things and distinguishing it from mental concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken your mental understanding of a truth for the truth itself? In what area of your life do you need to stop assuming that your conception of God, yourself, or reality is sufficient, and instead seek the actual reality independent of your thoughts?
?
The author says that if minds were absent, things still exist. Do you live as if God's existence depends on your understanding of Him, or do you truly believe He is real even when you are not thinking of Him? How would your daily awareness change if you remembered that God's reality is not constrained by your mental grasp?
?
The opponent raised doubts about a self-evident truth to avoid an unwelcome conclusion. When have you argued against a clear truth—perhaps about your own faults, your need for repentance, or the reality of the Hereafter—simply because accepting it would require change? Will you today commit to embracing difficult truths with intellectual honesty, no matter how uncomfortable?
