The Objector's Third Division of the Now's Non-Existence
Objector proposes third division; author refutes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's proposed third division of the now's non-existence and why the author rejects it based on the impossibility of a first moment of non-existence.

Core Idea
The non-existence of the now cannot be divided into a first moment without assuming the very point at issue.

The objector attempts to refute the author's division of non-existence into instantaneous or gradual by proposing a third option: the now's non-existence occurs across all subsequent time. The author responds by exposing that this division requires a first moment of non-existence, which is not granted. This debate reveals deep assumptions about the nature of time and the succession of nows.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's challenge to the binary division (instant vs. gradual) by introducing a third possibility: the now's non-existence spans all subsequent time.
Notice the objector first grants that the now exists externally.
2
Identify the author's key move
The author denies that there is a first moment of non-existence. Without a first moment, the third division collapses.
This denial is central to the refutation.
3
Link to the larger argument
Accepting a subsequent now implies the succession of nows, which is exactly what is being debated. Thus the objector's argument begs the question.
The succession of nows (تتالي الآنات) is a key concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلّمنا أن الآن موجود في اعيان . فلم قلتم : « إن عدمه . إما أن يكون دفعة أو على التدريج ؟ ، بل ههنا قسم ثالث ، وهو أن يكون عدمه حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده. ولا يقال : ليس كلامنا في عدم الآن ، بل في أول علم الآن. ومعلوم أن أول عدم الآن ، يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده. بل لا بد وأن يكون أول ذلك العدم واقعاً إما دفعة ، وإما على التدريج . لأنا نقول : هذا التقسيم إنما يصح لو كان لعدمه أول ، يكون هو فيه معدوماً ، وهذا ممنوع عندنا . بل عندنا : ظرف زمان عدمه : هو الآن الذي هو غير آن وجوده . فأما فرض آن آخر ، عقيب وجوده ، حتى يكون ظرفاً لزمان عدمه. فهذا إنما يصح لو ثبت القول بجواز تتالي الآنات . وذلك عين المطلوب .
هذا غاية ما يمكن أن يقال من جانبهم . والله أعلم
والجواب : قوله : « الآن له وجود في الذهن ، وذلك يكفي في صدق قولنا : وجد الآن ، وفي صدق قولنا : وجد الشيء في الآن ، قلنا : ما الذي أردت بقولك : ( وجد الآن في الذهن ؟ ، إن عنيت به أنه وجد العلم به في الذهن . فهذا حق . وإن عنيت به أن حضور الشيء في نفسه ، لا حصول له إلا في الذهن . فهذا جهل عظيم . لأنه لو عدمت الأذهان والأفهام ، فإن وجود الأشياء وحضورها في أنفسها : أمر حاصل متحقق . وأما حديث الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع ، فهو تشكيك في البديهيات .
The third question: We grant that the now exists in reals. So why did you say: 'Its non-existence is either instant or gradual?' Rather, here there is a third division, which is that its non-existence occurs in all of the time after it. It is not said: Our discussion is not about the non-existence of the now, but rather about the first moment of the now? And it is known that the first moment of the non-existence of the now is impossible to occur in all of the time after it. Rather, it is necessary that the first of that non-existence occurs either instantly or gradually. For we say: This division is only valid if there is for its non-existence a first [moment] in which it is non-existent, and this is denied by us. Rather, in our view: The container of the time of its non-existence is the now which is other than the now of its existence. As for supposing another now, subsequent to its existence, such that it is a container for the time of its non-existence—this is only valid if the statement allowing the succession of nows is established. And that is exactly what is sought.
This is the utmost that can be said from their side. And God knows best.
And the answer: His statement: 'The now has existence in the mind, and that suffices for the truth of our saying: The now existed, and for the truth of our saying: The thing existed in the now.' We say: What did you intend by your statement: 'The now existed in the mind?' If you mean by it that knowledge of it existed in the mind, then this is true. And if you mean by it that the presence of the thing in itself has no existence except in the mind, then this is great ignorance. Because if minds and intellects were non-existent, the existence of things and their presence in themselves is an actualized, realized matter. As for the talk of necessity, contingency, and impossibility, it is casting doubt on axioms.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of its answer concerning the nature of the now and its non-existence. The objector first grants that the now exists in external reality. He then challenges the author's division of the non-existence of the now into either instantaneous or gradual. He proposes a third division: that the now's non-existence occurs in all subsequent time. The author responds by pointing out that this division presupposes a first moment of non-existence, which the author denies. In their view, the time-container of the non-existence of the now is simply the now that is other than the now of its existence. To suppose another now immediately after existence requires accepting the succession of nows, which is precisely what the author is trying to prove. The author then begins to answer the objection: the objector claimed that the now has mental existence sufficient for statements like 'the now existed'. The author distinguishes between two meanings: (1) that knowledge of the now exists in the mind—this is true; (2) that the thing itself has no presence except in the mind—this is great ignorance, because things have existence in themselves independent of minds. This distinction protects the reality of time. The final remark about necessity, contingency, and impossibility being casting doubt on axioms suggests that the objector's argument leads to skepticism about self-evident truths.

Key Terms
الآن
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the non-existence of the now can be distributed across all future time without a first moment.
Correction: Any distribution of non-existence over time must have a first moment, otherwise the division is meaningless. The author denies that such a first moment exists for the now.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's proposed third division for the non-existence of the now?
A) It occurs instantaneously.
B) It occurs gradually.
C) It occurs in all time after the now.
D) It does not occur at all.

Key Takeaway
The objector's new division fails because it assumes a first moment of non-existence, which the author does not grant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith have you been assuming a third option without checking whether it really exists? Are you avoiding a clear conclusion by positing an unexamined alternative?
?
The objector's argument fails because it assumes what it tries to prove. Where in your own reasoning about Allah or the unseen do you rely on assumptions that actually need proof? Will you examine them honestly?
?
When you encounter a difficult theological question, do you tend to propose comfortable but unexamined middle positions to avoid the force of the argument? What would it cost you to follow the truth wherever it leads?

Answering the Objection: Mental Existence vs. Reality
Author answers objection about mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's distinction between the now's existence in the mind and its real external existence, and why the objector's claim amounts to skepticism about self-evident truths.

Core Idea
The now has real existence independent of minds; mental existence is merely knowledge of it.

The objector argues that the now's mental existence suffices to speak of its existence. The author clarifies the ambiguity: if by 'existence in the mind' you mean knowledge of the now, that's fine; but if you claim the now has no reality outside the mind, that is a grave error. The author insists that things have presence in themselves regardless of minds. The final remark warns that such confusion leads to doubting basic axioms like necessity, contingency, and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's claim
The objector says the now has mental existence, which is enough to affirm statements like 'the now existed'.
This is a common move: reduce reality to mental representation.
2
Follow the author's disambiguation
The author asks: do you mean knowledge of the now exists in the mind (true) or that the now's own being is only mental (false)? The second is great ignorance.
The key is distinguishing between the act of knowing and the thing known.
3
Recognize the consequence
If the now has no external reality, then all talk of necessity, contingency, and impossibility becomes doubtful. This undermines self-evident axioms.
The author asserts that things have presence in themselves even if no minds exist.

Source Text

?

The Now: Clarifying Existence and Proving Succession
Author clarifies existence in the now and proves succession via first non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's clarification that existence in the 'now' is not container-contained, and his proof that the first moment of non-existence leads to the succession of nows.

Core Idea
The now exists as reality, not as a container, and its non-existence must have a first moment.

The author continues his response to an objection about the meaning of existence in the now. He clarifies that saying 'the now exists in the now' does not imply a container-contained relationship, which would lead to absurdities. Then, he provides a logical proof that the non-existence of the now must have a first moment, which leads to the necessary succession of nows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification about existence in the now
Read the author's clarification that existence in the now is not like a container-contained relationship. This is important to avoid misunderstandings.
The author rejects the idea that the now is a container for existence.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the non-existence of the now occurs in all subsequent time. The author responds by specifying that they meant the first moment of non-existence.
3
Follow the proof of the first moment
The author proves that the non-existence must have a beginning because it occurs after existence. Trace the argument: at the beginning of that non-existence, either the now is non-existent or not.
4
Conclude the succession of nows
If the now is non-existent at the first moment, that moment is a new now adjacent to the previous one, thus establishing the succession of nows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقة، وأن الآن موجود على سبيل الحقيقة . ولا نعني بقولنا : إنه موجود في الآن : كونه مظروفاً حاصلاً في ظرف آخر . حتى يلزمنا المحالات . قوله : ه عدم الآن واقع في جميع الزمان الذي بعده ، قلنا : مرادنا : إن أول عدمه . إما أن يقع دفعه ، أو على التدريج. قوله : ولا نسلم أن لعدم ذلك الآن أول يكون هو فيه معدوماً ، قلنا : هذا مكابرة عظيمة . ثم الدليل عليه : إن عدم هذا الآن ، حصل بعد وجوده ، فيكون لعدمه ابتداء . فعند ابتداء ذلك العدم. إن لم يكن معدوماً ، فذلك العدم لم يبتدىء بعد . وإن كان معدوماً ، فهو المطلوب. وحينئذ يكون الآن الذي يحصل فيه أول عدمه ، بوجوده ، فيلزم تتالي الآنات . وهو المطلوب متصلاً
The reality, and that the now exists in the manner of reality. And we do not mean by our saying: 'It exists in the now' that it is a container contained in another container, such that impossibilities would follow. His statement: 'The non-existence of the now occurs in all the time after it,' we say: What we intend is that the first of its non-existence either occurs instantaneously or gradually. His statement: 'And we do not grant that for the non-existence of that now there is a first [moment] in which it is non-existent,' we say: This is great obstinacy. Then the proof for it: The non-existence of this now occurred after its existence, so its non-existence has a beginning. At the beginning of that non-existence, if it is not non-existent, then that non-existence has not begun. And if it is non-existent, then that is what is sought. And then the now in which the first of its non-existence occurs is connected to its existence, so the succession of nows follows. And that is what is sought, continuously.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage continues the author's answer to the objection. First, he clarifies that when they say the now exists in the now, they do not mean a container-contained relationship, which would lead to absurdities. Then he addresses the objector's claim that the non-existence of the now occurs in all subsequent time. The author insists they meant that the first moment of non-existence is either instantaneous or gradual. When the objector denies that there is a first moment of non-existence, the author calls this obstinacy and provides a proof: since the now's non-existence comes after its existence, there is a beginning of that non-existence. At that beginning, either the now is non-existent or it is not. If it is not non-existent, then the non-existence hasn't started; if it is non-existent, then we have a first moment of non-existence, which leads to a now adjacent to the previous now, entailing the succession of nows. This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
الآن
تتالي الآنات
أول العدم
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence of the now could be spread out over all future time without a distinct first moment.
Correction: Any non-existence that occurs after existence must have a beginning, because it is a temporal event. The author proves that this beginning exists and leads to a new now.

Checkpoint
What is the author's proof that the non-existence of the now has a first moment?
A) Because the now is a container.
B) Because non-existence occurs after existence, so it has a beginning.
C) Because the objector denies it.
D) Because succession is required.

Key Takeaway
The now exists as a reality, not as a container, and its non-existence must have a first moment, which leads to the succession of nows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every 'now' you experience is a distinct, fleeting reality that immediately passes into non-existence, are you truly aware that this very moment—the only moment you have—is slipping away? What will you do in the next now to ensure it is spent in obedience to Allah, before it becomes a non-existence you cannot retrieve?
?
The proof shows that the non-existence of the now has a definite beginning, meaning each now is uniquely countable. How does this reality confront your habit of procrastination? Which obligation have you been delaying, thinking you have many 'nows' ahead, when in fact each now is the only one you will ever have? Write down one specific action you will take in your next waking now.
?
You have just learned that the now is not a container but a direct reality, and that its passing is instantaneous and conclusive. Is there a sin you have been committing in the 'nows' you are given, assuming you can repent later? If this now were your last, what would you change? Make that change now, not as a thought, but as a deed.

Second Proof for Succession of Nows
Second proof: indivisible now must be followed by another present moment to avoid time interruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the succession of nows, which argues that each indivisible present moment must be followed by another present moment to avoid a break in time.

Core Idea
Time cannot stop: every now demands a next now.

In this proof, the author builds on the earlier demonstration that the present 'now' is indivisible. He argues that when this now ceases, another must occur immediately; otherwise time would be interrupted, an impossibility. This argument strengthens the case for the succession of nows by focusing on the necessity of a subsequent present moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage also includes a distinction from the previous proof.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument rests on the indivisibility of the present now. Find where this is stated and understand its role.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: if the now is indivisible, then when it ceases, something must be present immediately after; otherwise time would break, which is impossible.
This is a conditional argument with an absurdity.
4
Note the distinction from the first proof
The author explicitly contrasts this proof with the previous one. In the first proof, adjacency was known; here, the focus is on the necessity of a subsequent moment regardless of how the first now ceases.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The reasoning applies to every now, thus proving the succession of nows. Ask yourself: does this fully establish continuous time?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات تتالي الآنات : أن نقول : قد دللنا على أن الآن الحاضر ، لا يقبل القسمة المذكورة - أعني الانقسام إلى جزءين ، يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر - وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن هذا الآن إذا عدم ، فلا بد وأن يحصل عقيبة شيء آخر ، يكون حاضراً . إذا لو لم يحصل عقيبة ذلك الذي ذكرنا ، لزم أن ينقطع الزمان ، وهو محال . وكذا القول في الثاني والثالث . وذلك يوجب القول بتتالي الآنات . والفرق بين هذا الوجه وبين ما قبله : أنا بينا في الوجه الأول : أنه لا بد وأن يعلم الآن الأول ، في أن آخر ويلاصقه وأما في هذا الوجه ، فإنا لم نلتفت إلى كيفية عدم الآن الأول ، بل قلنا : إن عند عدم الآن الأول لا بد وأن يحدث أن آخر ، يكون هو أيضاً حاضراً ، حال حصوله . وإلا لزم انقطاع الزمان ، وهو محال . والله أعلم .
The second proof for establishing the succession of nows: We say: We have demonstrated that the present now does not admit the aforementioned division—I mean the division into two parts, one of which is prior to the other. And if this is established, we say: When this now becomes non-existent, it is necessary that there occurs after it something else which is present. For if there did not occur after it that which we mentioned, then time would be interrupted, and that is impossible. Similarly for the second and third. And that necessitates the statement of the succession of nows. The difference between this argument and the one before it: In the first argument, we showed that it is necessary that the first now be known to be adjacent to another. But in this argument, we did not pay attention to the manner of the non-existence of the first now; rather, we said: When the first now is non-existent, it is necessary that another now occurs, which is also present at the moment of its occurrence. Otherwise, time would be interrupted, and that is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the succession of nows. The author first recalls that the present now is indivisible (no parts with priority). Given that, when this now ceases to exist, there must be something else present immediately after it; otherwise time would stop, which is impossible. This reasoning applies to each now in succession, thus proving that nows follow one another. The author distinguishes this proof from the earlier one: the first proof required knowing the first now as adjacent to another, whereas this proof simply argues from the necessary occurrence of a subsequent present moment upon the non-existence of the previous one, regardless of how that non-existence occurs.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر
تتالي الآنات
انقطاع الزمان
القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think time could have gaps or pauses.
Correction: This argument shows that gaps are impossible because each moment must be followed by another present moment, otherwise time would be interrupted, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the second proof for the succession of nows?
A) The now is infinitely divisible.
B) The now is indivisible and its non-existence requires a subsequent present moment to avoid time disruption.
C) Time is continuous.
D) The now exists only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Every indivisible present moment must be followed by another, otherwise time would be interrupted, proving the succession of nows.

Third Proof: Time Must Be Discrete
Third proof dichotomy eliminating continuous time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the succession of nows, which argues that time must be discrete because continuous time would consist only of non-existent past and future.

Core Idea
Time cannot be continuous; it must be composed of successive nows.

This lesson presents the third proof from the author's series establishing the discrete nature of time. The argument sets up a dichotomy: time is either continuous or discrete. By showing the impossibility of continuous time, the proof concludes that time is made of successive nows. The reasoning hinges on the observation that past and future are non-existent and the present moment is not a part of time for the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the logical structure.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The passage presents a clear either-or: time is either a continuous quantity or a discrete quantity. This is the starting point of the proof.
3
Understand why continuity is rejected
The author argues that continuous time is false because time only means past and future, both of which are non-existent. The now is not considered a part of time by the opponent.
What does 'non-existent' imply for the continuity of time?
4
Connect to the conclusion
Once the continuous option is eliminated, only the discrete remains, supporting the succession of nows. Reflect on why this conclusion follows logically.
The conclusion is implicit: if not continuous, then discrete.

Source Text

الحجة الثالثة في إثبات تتالي الآنات : أن نقول : الزمان . إما أن يكون كماً متصلاً ، أو كماً منفصلاً . والأول باطل . لأن الزمان لا معنى له إلا الماضي والمستقبل . وهما معدومان . وأما الآن فليس عندكم جزء من أجزاء الزمان .
The third proof for establishing the succession of nows: We say: Time is either a continuous quantity or a discrete quantity. The first is false, because time has no meaning except the past and the future, and both are non-existent. As for the now, in your view it is not a part of the parts of time.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author presents a dichotomy: time is either continuous (a continuum) or discrete (made of indivisible parts). He argues that continuous time is false because time only consists of past and future, which are non-existent. The past no longer exists, the future does not yet exist. The now (present moment) is not considered a part of time according to the opponent's view (perhaps referring to those who deny the now as a part). By eliminating the continuous view, time must be discrete, i.e., composed of nows, thus supporting the succession of nows. The argument is cut off in the window, but the point is made.

But in this now, you can turn to Allah. Are you seizing this now to repent, or are you delaying, thinking there will be another continuous stream of time?
?

The Reality of Now and the Beginning of Non-Existence
Clarifies that 'now' exists truly and answers an objection on the first moment of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'now' is a real entity and that its non-existence must have a first moment, which establishes the succession of instants.

Core Idea
The now is real, and its non-existence begins at a precise instant.

This passage addresses a key objection to the reality of 'now' in kalām. The author first clarifies what is meant by the existence of 'now', then responds to an objection that its non-existence covers all subsequent time. By proving that non-existence must have a beginning, he establishes the continuous succession of instants.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification
Read the opening where the author asserts that 'now' exists truly and clarifies what he does NOT mean by that—namely, he does not mean it is contained in another container.
This distinction prevents impossible consequences.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objector's claim: that the non-existence of a now occurs throughout all the time after it. This would imply that non-existence has no beginning.
The objector is denying that non-existence has a start.
3
Analyze the response and proof
The author distinguishes between instantaneous or gradual non-existence, then proves that non-existence must have a first moment. If at that moment the now were not non-existent, non-existence would not have begun. Hence it must be non-existent, and that moment is contiguous with the now's existence, yielding succession.
The proof relies on the fact that non-existence occurs after existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقة ، وأن الآن موجود على سبيل الحقيقة . ولا نعني بقولنا : إنه موجود في الآن : كونه مظروفاً حاصلاً في ظرف آخر . حتى يلزمنا المحالات . قوله : ه عدم الآن واقع في جميع الزمان الذي بعده ، قلنا : مرادنا : إن أول عدمه . إما أن يقع دفعه ، أو على التدريج. قوله : ولا نسلم أن لعدم ذلك الآن أول يكون هو فيه معدوماً ، قلنا : هذا مكابرة عظيمة . ثم الدليل عليه : إن عدم هذا الآن ، حصل بعد وجوده ، فيكون لعدمه ابتداء . فعند ابتداء ذلك العدم. إن لم يكن معدوماً ، فذلك العدم لم يبتدىء بعد . وإن كان معدوماً ، فهو المطلوب. وحينئذ يكون الآن الذي يحصل فيه أول عدمه ، . بوجوده ، فيلزم تتالي الآنات . وهو المطلوب متصلاً
The reality: that the now exists in a true manner. And we do not mean by our saying 'it exists in the now' that it is contained and occurring in another container, such that impossibilities would follow from us. His statement: 'The non-existence of the now occurs in all the time after it.' We said: Our meaning: its first non-existence either occurs instantaneously or gradually. His statement: 'And we do not concede that that now has a first [moment] in which it is non-existent.' We said: This is great obstinacy. Then the proof for it: The non-existence of this now occurred after its existence, so its non-existence has a beginning. At the beginning of that non-existence, if it was not non-existent, then that non-existence did not begin. And if it was non-existent, then that is what is sought. And then the now in which its first non-existence occurs is [connected] with its existence, so the succession of instants follows. And that is what is sought continuously.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair within the first proof for the succession of instants (تتالي الآنات). It begins with a clarification that the 'now' (الآن) is real, not a container. The objector then claims that the non-existence of a now pervades all subsequent time, implying no instant begins. The answer distinguishes between instantaneous or gradual non-existence, then demonstrates that non-existence must have a first moment, because it begins after existence. If at that first moment the now were not non-existent, then non-existence would not have begun; if it is non-existent, that is the proof. This first moment of non-existence is necessarily contiguous with the instant's existence, leading to a chain of successive instants (تتالي الآنات). The argument follows the author's own order: first clarifying the position, then presenting the objection, then responding by distinguishing and proving the necessary beginning of non-existence.

العدم
تتالي الآنات

Misconception: The non-existence of a now does not have a first moment; it simply pervades all subsequent time.
Correction: The author proves that non-existence must have a beginning because it occurs after existence, and at that beginning the now must be non-existent.

The Second Proof for the Succession of Instants
Proves that when the present instant ceases, another instant must arise immediately to avoid interruption of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the succession of instants and how it differs from the first proof.

Core Idea
Time cannot have a gap; when an instant ends, another must immediately arise.

This lesson presents the second proof that time is composed of successive indivisible instants. The argument is simple but powerful: if one instant ceases and nothing follows, time would be interrupted — and that is impossible. The passage also clarifies how this proof differs from the first one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of indivisibility
Recall that the present instant cannot be divided into earlier and later parts. This is a key starting point for the proof.
2
Follow the reasoning from cessation to necessity
Trace the argument: when the instant ceases, something else must immediately come to be. If not, time would have a break, which is impossible.
Notice the author uses 'we say' to introduce the logical steps.
3
Compare with the first proof
The author explains the difference: the first proof focused on contiguity, while this one focuses on the impossibility of a temporal gap.
This clarifies that the second proof does not rely on how the first instant ceases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات تتالي الآنات : أن نقول : قد دللنا على أن الآن الحاضر ، لا يقبل القسمة المذكورة - أعني الانقسام إلى جزءين ، يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر - وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن هذا الآن إذا عدم ، فلا بد وأن يحصل عقيبه شيء آخر ، يكون حاضراً . إذ لو لم يحصل عقيبه ذلك الذي ذكرنا ، لزم أن ينقطع الزمان ، وهو محال . وكذا القول في الثاني والثالث . وذلك يوجب القول بتتالي الآنات . والفرق بين هذا الوجه وبين ما قبله : أنا بينا في الوجه الأول : أنه لا بد وأن يلم الآن الأول بآن آخر ويلاصقه . وأما في هذا الوجه ، فإنا لم نلتفت إلى كيفية عدم الآن الأول ، بل قلنا : إن عند عدم الآن الأول لا بد وأن يحدث آن آخر ، يكون هو أيضاً حاضراً ، حال حصوله . وإلا لزم انقطاع الزمان ، وهو محال . والله أعلم .
The second proof for the succession of instants: We say: We have demonstrated that the present instant does not admit the mentioned division — that is, division into two parts such that one is prior to the other. And when this is established, we say: When this instant ceases, there must necessarily occur after it another thing which is present. For if that which we mentioned did not occur after it, time would be interrupted, which is impossible. The same applies to the second and the third. That necessitates affirming the succession of instants. The difference between this method and the previous one: We showed in the first method that the first instant must touch and be contiguous with another instant. But in this method, we did not attend to the manner of non-existence of the first instant; rather we said: When the first instant ceases, there must necessarily arise another instant which is also present during its occurrence. Otherwise, time would be interrupted, which is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الثانية) for the succession of instants. It builds on the established fact that the present instant (الآن الحاضر) is indivisible — it cannot be split into earlier and later parts. Once that is accepted, the argument proceeds: when this indivisible instant ceases (يعدم), something else must immediately come into existence that is present (حاضر). If nothing followed, time would be interrupted (انقطاع الزمان), and that is impossible. The same reasoning applies to every subsequent instant, yielding a continuous succession. The author then distinguishes this proof from the first: the first proof required the first instant to touch (يلم) and be contiguous (يلاصق) with another instant. This second proof, however, does not consider the *how* of the first instant's non-existence; it simply states that upon the cessation of the first instant, another present instant must arise. The argument is purely from the impossibility of time having a gap.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر
القسمة
انقطاع الزمان
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that an instant is a part of time and therefore requires a successor.
Correction: The proof does not assume that; it uses the premise that the present instant is indivisible, and the impossibility of time being interrupted.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the second proof?
A) The present instant is divisible into two parts one prior to the other.
B) The present instant does not admit division into earlier and later parts.
C) Time is a continuous quantity.
D) The non-existence of the first instant is gradual.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the present instant must be followed by another to prevent a break in time, regardless of how the first instant ceases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Third Proof: Time as a Discrete Quantity
Demonstrates that time cannot be continuous but is composed of discrete successive instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that time must be composed of discrete instants (atoms of time) rather than being a continuous flow.

Core Idea
Time's continuity is impossible; it is made of successive instants.

This lesson presents the third proof from the kalam tradition demonstrating that time is not a continuous quantity but a discrete series of now-moments. The argument hinges on the nature of the 'now' as a boundary between past and future, not a part of time. Follow along to see why a continuous time leads to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: it begins with the claim, gives a disjunction, refutes one side, and concludes.
The argument is concise; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the two possibilities for time
The passage presents time as either continuous (كم متصل) or discrete (كم منفصل). Understand these terms from kalam physics.
Continuous means infinitely divisible; discrete means composed of indivisible units.
3
Understand the rejection of continuity
Focus on why continuity is impossible: past and future are non-existent, and the now is only a boundary. Connecting non-existents via an existent boundary is impossible.
This is the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إثبات تتالي الآنات : أن نقول : الزمان . إما أن يكون كماً متصلاً ، أو كماً منفصلاً . والأول باطل . لأن الزمان لا معنى له إلا الماضي والمستقبل . وهما معدومان . وأما الآن فليس عندكم جزء من أجزاء الزمان . وإنما هو ظرف مشترك فيه بين الماضي وبين المستقبل . لأنه نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل . وهذا القول يقتضي اتصال معدوم بمعدوم آخر ، بطرف موجود . وإنه محال . فثبت : أنه كم منفصل . فهو مركب من وحدات متعاقبة ، وذلك يوجب تتالي الآنات . وتقرير هذا الوجه : مذكور في باب الآن والزمان على الاستقصاء
The third proof for the succession of instants: We say: Time is either a continuous quantity or a discrete quantity. The first is false, because time has no meaning except past and future, and both are non-existent. As for the now, it is not for you a part of the parts of time; rather it is a common container shared between the past and the future, because it is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. This statement necessitates the connection of a non-existent to another non-existent by an existent extremity. And that is impossible. So it is established that it is a discrete quantity. So it is composed of successive units, and that necessitates the succession of instants. The detailed exposition of this method is mentioned in the chapter on the now and time in full.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (الحجة الثالثة) for the succession of instants. It classifies time into two kinds of quantity: continuous (كم متصل) and discrete (كم منفصل). It argues that time cannot be continuous. Why? Because time reality is only past and future, both non-existent. The 'now' (الآن) is not a part of time but a boundary common to past and future—it is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. If time were continuous, it would require connecting two non-existents (past and future) through an existent extremity (the now), which is impossible. Therefore time must be a discrete quantity composed of successive atomic units (آحاد متعاقبة), i.e., instants. This necessitates the succession of instants (تتالي الآنات). The author notes that a full treatment can be found elsewhere in the chapter on now and time.

Key Terms
كم متصل
كم منفصل
الآن
نهاية الماضي
بداية المستقبل
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The now (instant) is a part of time, like a slice of a continuous timeline.
Correction: The now is not a part of time; it is a boundary between past and future. Time is composed of successive boundaries, not extended parts.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why is time not a continuous quantity?
A) Because time is composed of instants that are divisible.
B) Because past and future are both non-existent, and connecting them via an existent now is impossible.
C) Because the now is a part of time.
D) Because continuous quantities always exist in reality.

Key Takeaway
Time is not a continuous flow but a series of discrete instants; continuous time would require an impossible connection between non-existent past and future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every 'now' is a boundary that separates your past from your future, and you have no guarantee of the next instant, are you living this instant as if it is your last chance to repent and obey Allah? What keeps you from doing so right now?
?
The proof shows that continuous time is impossible—past and future are non-existent, only the now is real. Yet you spend so much energy worrying about the non-existent future and regretting the non-existent past. How can you train yourself to focus your worship and actions solely on the present moment, treating each instant as a distinct gift from Allah?
?
Time is discrete—made of separate instants. Each instant is a point of decision. In the next instant, will you choose to act in obedience to Allah or will you let the opportunity slip into non-existence? What specific action will you take in the next 'now' that will define your eternity?

Initial Objection on Point Contact and Contiguity
Opponent argues sphere-plane contact implies only one point, refutation of contiguity and instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that sphere-plane contact does not force contiguous points, and the author's refutation that the argument is circular because it assumes the very concept in dispute (successive instants).

Core Idea
Proving contiguity of points by assuming successive instants is arguing in circles.

In this passage, Sanusi examines an objection against the notion that moving spheres generate contiguous points. The opponent attempts to show that contact at a point does not lead to a line of points, and even if two points exist, contiguity does not follow. The author then reveals the flaw: the opponent’s own reasoning relies on the possibility of successive instants, which is just as controversial as the succession of points. This lesson unpacks this intricate exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's initial claim that sphere-plane contact is at a point, not a line. Notice the reasoning about the sphere's simplicity and the perishing of points.
Pay attention to the phrase 'النقطة إنما توجد فيها بالفعل'.
2
Identify the opponent's provisos
The opponent concedes contact by point but then argues that only one point exists at a time, so no line of contiguous points forms. Also consider the concession about two points and the line between them.
3
Understand the author's counter
The author points out that the opponent's dilemma about successive instants assumes the possibility of tatālī al-ānāt, which is itself disputed, making the argument circular.
This is the key move: proving a thing by what equals it in definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالنقطة . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها تماسة بخط مستدير ؟ سلمنا : إنها تماس السطح المستوي بالنقطة . لكن الكرة جسم بسيط . فالنقطة إنما توجد فيها بالفعل ، بسبب المماسة . فإذا زالت المماسة الأولى ، وحدثت المماسة الثانية ، فقد فنيت النقطة الأولى ، وحدثت النقطة الثانية. وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون الحاصل في الكرة أبداً ، ليس إلا نقطة واحدة . فلا يلزم تركب الخط المتشافعة . سلمنا : بقاء النقطتين . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : حصل بين النقطتين خط ؟ وبهذا التقدير ، فإنه لا يلزم تشافع النقط . عن النقط لا يقال : الكرة إذا صارت مماسة للسطح بنقطة في آن ، ففي الآن الثاني ، إما أن تبقى تلك المماسة ، وإما أن تحصل مماسة أخرى . والأول يقتضي سكون الكرة ، وقد فرضناها متحركة . والثاني يقتضي مماسة نقطة أخرى . وحينئذ يلزم تشافع النقط . لأنا نقول : قولكم : « إذا حصلت المماسة على نقطة في آن ، ففي الآن الثاني إما أن يكون كذا وكذا ، : بناء على إمكان تتالي الآنات. لكن النزاع في جواز تتالي الآنات ، كالنزاع في جواز تتالي النقط . فجعل أحدهما مقدمة في إثبات الثاني : يكون إثباتاً للشيء بما يساويه في الحد . وإنه باطل
By the point. Why is it not permissible to say: it is contact by a circular line? We concede: it is contact of the plane surface by the point. But the sphere is a simple body. The point exists in it only actually, due to contact. So when the first contact ceases and the second contact occurs, the first point perishes and the second point occurs. On this estimation, what obtains in the sphere forever is only one point. Therefore, the composition of contiguous points into a line does not necessarily follow. We concede: the survival of the two points. Then why is it not permissible to say: a line occurs between the two points? On this estimation, contiguity of points does not necessarily follow. Regarding points, it is not said: if the sphere contacts the plane at a point in an instant, then in the second instant, either that contact remains or another contact occurs. The first necessitates the sphere's rest, but we have assumed it moving. The second necessitates contact at another point. Then contiguity of points follows. We say: your statement: 'If contact occurs at a point in an instant, then in the second instant either so-and-so' is based on the possibility of the succession of instants. But the dispute about the permissibility of the succession of instants is like the dispute about the permissibility of the succession of points. Making one a premise for proving the other is proving a thing by that which equals it in definition, and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-argument exchange concerning sphere-plane contact and its implications for atomic indivisibles. The opponent first argues that contact must be at a point, not a line, and since the sphere is simple, only one point exists at a time, so no line of contiguous points arises. Even if two points survive, a line between them does not force contiguity. The opponent then introduces a dilemma: in successive instants, either the same contact remains (implying rest) or a new contact occurs (implying another point). The opponent claims this leads to contiguity. The author responds that this reasoning assumes the possibility of successive instants (tatālī al-ānāt), but the permissibility of successive instants is itself disputed, just like the permissibility of successive points. Thus, using one to prove the other is circular—proving a thing by what is equivalent to it, which is invalid.

Key Terms
نقطة
مماسة
تشافع النقط
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent denies that contact entails contiguous points or a line; the misconception is that the opponent denies contact altogether.
Correction: The opponent concedes contact at a point but argues that it does not logically force a line of contiguous points, because points perish and are replaced, or a line could exist without contiguity.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author identifies in the opponent's argument about the sphere and instants?
A) The opponent assumes the sphere is at rest.
B) The opponent's argument assumes the existence of successive instants, which is itself disputed.
C) The opponent claims a line must be composed of points.
D) The opponent denies the existence of the sphere.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument against contiguity fails because it relies on a premise (successive instants) that is as controversial as the conclusion it seeks to disprove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever built your understanding of a major theological issue on an assumption you never realized was itself in dispute? What step will you take this week to examine the foundations of your own beliefs?
?
When you find yourself in a debate, do you check whether your argument relies on a premise your opponent does not accept? How can you train yourself to spot circular reasoning in your own thinking?
?
The opponent's argument seemed logically sound at first glance, but its hidden assumption made it invalid. Is there any area of your life where you've accepted a convincing argument without scrutinizing its unstated assumptions? What will you do about it today?

The Possibility of the Sphere and Plane
Author defends possibility of sphere and plane to prove indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the logical possibility of a sphere and plane to establish the necessity of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Possibility of simple shapes leads to necessity of the indivisible atom.

This lesson presents the author's reply to an objection challenging the assumed shapes. He argues from principles of natural philosophy that both the sphere and plane are possible, and this possibility is key to demonstrating the indivisible atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the reply structure beginning with 'والجواب'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Follow the argument for the sphere
The author argues the sphere is possible because it is necessitated by simple natures, the simple exists, and it can be free from preventing accidents.
3
See how the plane argument leads to the atom
The plane is possible because roughness comes from small smooth surfaces (otherwise infinite regress). Once these possibilities are accepted, the indivisible atom becomes necessary.
This connects to the overall proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما قوله : « لم قلتم إنه يمكن وجود كرة وسطح على الوجه الذي فرضتموه ؟ قلنا : أما المنع من وجود هذه الكرة ، فغير مستقيم ، على أصول الفلاسفة. لأن هذا الشكل هو الذي تقتضيه جميع الطبائع البسيطة . ووجود البسيط غير ممتنع . إذ لو امتنع البسيط ، لامتنع المركب . وخلو البسيط عن العوارض المانعة ممكن ، والموقوف على الممكن ممكن . فوجود الكرة شيء ممكن . وأما وجود السطح المستوي، فهو أيضاً ممكن . لأن مسبب الخشونة : لا بد وأن تحصل من سطوح صغار ملس . وإلا ذهبت الزوايا إلى غير النهاية . وإذا جاز وجود سطح صغير مستوى ، فقد حصل إمكان كل المقدمات التي ذكرناها . وثبت : أن بتقدير وقوعها ، فالجوهر الفرد لازم فحينئذ يحصل المقصود
The answer: As for his statement: 'Why do you say that the existence of a sphere and a surface in the manner you assumed is possible?' We say: As for the prevention of the existence of this sphere, it is not sound, according to the principles of the philosophers. Because this shape is what all simple natures necessitate. The existence of the simple is not impossible. For if the simple were impossible, the composite would be impossible. And the freedom of the simple from preventing accidents is possible, and what depends on the possible is possible. So the existence of the sphere is a possible thing. As for the existence of the plane surface, it is also possible. Because the cause of roughness necessarily occurs from small smooth surfaces; otherwise the angles would go to infinity. And if the existence of a small plane surface is permissible, then the possibility of all the premises we mentioned has been obtained. And it is established that, given their occurrence, the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) is necessary, and then the purpose is achieved.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to an objection questioning the possibility of a sphere and plane as assumed. The author argues from the principles of the philosophers (i.e., natural philosophy) that a sphere is possible because it is a simple shape necessitated by simple natures; the simple is not impossible, otherwise composites would be impossible. Moreover, the sphere can be free from preventing accidents, so its existence is possible. The plane surface is also possible, because roughness necessarily results from small smooth surfaces (otherwise angles would regress infinitely). Once a small smooth surface is allowed, all premises become possible. Therefore, given these possibilities, if they occur, the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) necessarily follows, and the proof's purpose is achieved.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الطبائع البسيطة
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is asserting that a perfect sphere and plane actually exist in the world.
Correction: The author is only arguing for their logical possibility based on first principles, not their actual existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the existence of a plane surface is possible?
A) Because smooth surfaces are simple.
B) Because roughness results from small smooth surfaces; otherwise angles regress infinitely.
C) Because philosophers assume it.
D) Because atoms are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a sphere and plane, when taken together, leads to the necessity of the indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Two Arguments for the Point of Contact
Author refutes denial of contact point's existence with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments that establish the existence of the point of contact in the context of bodily contact.

Core Idea
The point of contact must exist; otherwise contact would be with pure non-existence.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that denies the existence of the point of contact between two bodies. He provides two concise arguments rooted in the opponent's own premises and rational necessity. This lesson unpacks those arguments, showing how they defend the reality of the meeting place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the compact style of the refutation.
2
Identify the two arguments
The author states 'from two aspects'. Find the first argument (turning opponent's view against them) and the second argument (reductio ad absurdum).
3
Understand the reductio
Consider why describing non-existence with 'meeting' is impossible. The commentary explains the logical problem.
This is a key concept in kalam: non-existence cannot bear attributes.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself how these two arguments together force the opponent to accept the existence of the point of contact. Note the strategy of using an opponent's own premises.
This connects to broader debates about atoms and accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second, if the point of contact were not existent, then contact would occur with pure non-existence, which is absurd. Thus, the point of contact must exist.

The author flatly denies the objection, claiming his proof already necessitated the atom.

In this short passage, the author addresses a potential objection to his proof of the indivisible atom from geometry. The opponent argues that positing continuous spheres and circles undermines the atomist position. The author responds with a sharp denial, asserting that his earlier demonstration already established the atom's necessity.

This is a direct denial, not a detailed refutation.
3

This shows the atomist position is robust against such objections.
4

This brevity suggests the point is considered settled by the author's previous proofs.

Substituting Sliding for Rolling
Objection denies sphere rolling; author substitutes sliding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the atomic points argument by substituting sliding for rolling when the opponent objects to rolling.

Core Idea
When one argument is blocked, a different motion still supports the conclusion.

In this passage, the opponent challenges the argument by denying that a sphere can roll. The author skillfully concedes this point but shows that even if rolling is impossible, sliding is uncontroversial and still leads to the same conclusion. This demonstrates a key dialectical technique in kalam: if one path is closed, another is available.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to see the structure: the opponent's statement and the author's response. Then read the translation.
2
Notice the concession
The author does not insist that rolling is possible. Instead, he says 'Suppose it cannot roll' (هب أنه لا يصح تدحرجها). This is a strategic concession to move the discussion forward.
Concessions can defuse objections without weakening the overall argument.
3
Understand how sliding works
Sliding still produces a line composed of points of contact. Ask yourself why that line is important for the argument about atomic points. The commentary can help.
The line of contact points is evidence for discrete parts (atoms).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « لا نسلم إمكان تدحرج الكرة ، قلنا : هب أنه لا يصح تدحرجها ، لكن لا نزاع في إمكان انزلاقها، على السطح المستوي. وبتقدير انزلاقها ، فإنه يفترض في البسيط خط مركب من نقط التماس ، ويحصل المقصود
His statement: 'We do not concede the possibility of the sphere rolling.' We say: Suppose that it cannot roll, but there is no dispute about the possibility of it sliding on the plane surface. And assuming it slides, then a line composed of points of contact is supposed in the simple body, and the purpose is achieved.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is another objection-answer. The opponent denies that a sphere can roll (i.e., rotate while moving). The author concedes for the sake of argument that rolling may be impossible, but points out that there is no disagreement about the possibility of the sphere sliding (i.e., moving without rotation). In sliding, the sphere's contact points form a line composed of points of contact on the plane, and this still yields the desired conclusion (the existence of atomic points). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
تدحرج
انزلاق
خط مركب
نقط التماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument requires the sphere to roll.
Correction: The author concedes rolling may be impossible but shows sliding suffices.

Checkpoint

A) Rotation.
B) Sliding.
C) Oscillation.
D) Free fall.

Key Takeaway
The author's dialectical flexibility saves the argument even when a specific physical motion is challenged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author shifts from rolling to sliding without losing the argument. What holds you back from exploring alternative paths to the same truth?

Refutation of Line Contact During Rolling
Author refutes claim that sphere contacts plane by a line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument proving that a sphere rolling on a plane contacts it only at a point, not along a line.

Core Idea
Line contact would force a straight line on a sphere's curved surface, which is impossible.

The author presents a concise refutation of a common misconception about rolling contact. Using basic geometry and the principle of coincidence, he shows that line contact leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for rational judgments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the flow of the argument before analyzing details.
Note the key terms: مماسة (contact), انطباق (coincidence), خط مستقيم (straight line).
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The passage begins by quoting the opposing view: 'the sphere during rolling contacts the plane by a line.' Identify this assumption.
3
Follow the refutation steps
Trace the logical steps: definition of contact, implication of line contact, deduction that the sphere's line must be straight, conclusion that this is impossible.
The argument relies on the fact that a straight line can only coincide with a straight line.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this is a rational judgment (ḥukm 'aqlī). The refutation uses pure reason and geometry, not empirical observation.
This exemplifies how kalam uses rational proofs to establish truths about physical reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الكرة حال تدحرجها تماس السطح المستوي بالخط ، قلنا : هذا باطل قطعاً، لأن المماسة لا تحصل إلا بانطباق أحد المماسين على الآخر، فلو ماست الكرة السطح بالخط، لحصل في الكرة خط منطبق على الخط المستقيم، المرتسم في السطح المستوي. والمنطبق على المستقيم مستقيم. فيلزم أن يحصل على محيط الكرة، خط مستقيم. وذلك باطل
His statement: 'The sphere during its rolling contacts the plane surface by a line.' We say: This is absolutely false, because contact occurs only by one of the two contacting things coinciding with the other. So if the sphere contacted the surface by a line, there would occur in the sphere a line coinciding with the straight line traced on the plane surface. And that which coincides with a straight line is straight. So it would follow that on the circumference of the sphere there occurs a straight line, and that is false.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a refutation of the claim that during rolling the sphere contacts the plane along a line rather than a point. The author argues that contact requires one surface to coincide with the other. If the sphere contacted by a line, that line on the sphere would have to coincide with a straight line on the plane. But anything coinciding with a straight line is itself straight. That would imply a straight line on the sphere's circumference, which is impossible (since the circumference is curved). Therefore, line contact is impossible, and contact must be at a point.

Key Terms
مماسة
انطباق
خط مستقيم
محيط الكرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a sphere rolling on a plane contacts it along a line (like a line of points).
Correction: Geometrically, contact requires coincidence of surfaces; line contact would imply a straight line on the sphere, contradicting its curvature.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea of line contact during rolling?
A) Because the sphere is smooth.
B) Because it would impose a straight line on the sphere's circumference.
C) Because rolling is impossible.
D) Because the line would be curved.

Key Takeaway
A sphere rolling on a plane touches it only at a point, not a line, because line contact would contradict the geometry of a curved surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a rolling ball, your eyes tell you it touches the ground along a line. This lesson shows your eyes are wrong. Where else in your life are you trusting your senses over rational proof? What would it cost you to submit your perception to reason?
?
The opponent held a belief simply because it seemed intuitive. The author did not accept intuition without evidence. Is there an area of your faith where you hold a belief merely because it feels right, without ever examining its logical basis? What would it mean for you to test that belief against proof today?
?
The author declares the opponent's claim 'absolutely false' without softening. Are there falsehoods in your own thinking — beliefs or habits you know are wrong — that you have comforted yourself with words like 'maybe' or 'it's not that bad'? What would it take for you to declare them false with the same certainty?

Two Points from One Contact: The Line with an Endpoint
Objection-answer: author introduces line with endpoint to allow two points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a line with an endpoint to show the coexistence of two points, refuting the opponent's insistence on a single persistent point.

Core Idea
Two points can coexist: one as an endpoint, the other from new contact.

In this final objection of the section, the opponent argues that if only one contact exists at any moment, then only one point can persist. The author responds by introducing a line with an endpoint that allows the first point to remain while a second point arises from a new contact. This response shows the rational possibility of multiple points existing simultaneously, countering the opponent's assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the first line: the opponent says since there is only one contact at any time, only one point exists always. Understand the reasoning.
This is a common assumption in such arguments.
2
Identify the author's key response
Notice the phrase 'we say: It is possible that in the sphere there actually occurs a line having an endpoint.' This is the core idea.
3
Understand the two-point coexistence
The first point persists as the endpoint of that line, while the second point arises from a new contact. Both exist simultaneously.
This is how the author gets two points.
4
Note the concession and further inference
The author concedes that the contacts occur in different instants, leading to succession of instants and contiguity of points.
This strengthens the argument for contiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « الموجود في الكرة أبداً ، مماسة واحدة ، فالموجود فيها أبداً نقطة واحدة ، قلنا : يحتمل أن يحصل في الكرة خط ذو نهاية ، بالفعل ، ثم إن الكرة لقيت ذلك السطح بتلك النقطة ، ثم عند زوال الملاقاة عنها ، تحصل الملاقاة بنقطة أخرى تتلوها . فالنقطة الأولى تكون باقية بسبب كونها نهاية بالفعل لذلك الخط ، والنقطة الثانية تكون موجودة بسبب حصول المماسة عليها ، وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب . سلمنا : أنه لا يتشافع نقطتان ، لكن هاتان المماستان لا تحدثان إلا في آنين . فيلزم منه تتالي الآنات ، ومن تالي الآنات تتشاقع النقط . وأيضاً : فالخط المرتسم على السطح المستوي ، إنما ارتسم من المماسات الحاصلة
His statement: 'What exists in the sphere always is one contact, so what exists in it always is one point.' We say: It is possible that in the sphere there actually occurs a line having an endpoint. Then the sphere meets that surface at that point. Then when contact with it ceases, contact occurs at another point following it. So the first point remains because it is actually the endpoint of that line, and the second point exists due to contact occurring on it. Then the desired conclusion is obtained. We concede that two points are not contiguous, but these two contacts occur only in two instants. Thus, the succession of instants follows, and from the succession of instants, the contiguity of points follows. Also: the line traced on the plane surface is traced only from the contacts that occur.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the final objection-answer in the window. The opponent argues that since only one contact exists at any time, only one point exists always. The author replies by positing a line with an endpoint already existing in the sphere. The sphere contacts the plane at that endpoint. When contact ceases, a new contact occurs at the next point. The first point persists as the endpoint of that line, and the second point exists by contact. Thus, two points exist simultaneously (one as endpoint, one as new contact), satisfying the requirement for contiguity. The author further concedes that the two contacts occur in two different instants, leading to the succession of instants (tatālī al-ānāt), which in turn implies contiguity of points. The passage ends with an additional note that the line on the plane is composed from these contacts (incomplete).

Key Terms
خط ذو نهاية
النقطة الأولى
النقطة الثانية
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only one point can exist at a time because only one contact occurs at a time.
Correction: A point can persist as an endpoint of a line independently of contact, allowing a second point from new contact to coexist.

Checkpoint

A) The sphere has two points inherently.
B) The first point remains as an endpoint of a line, the second arises from new contact.
C) The points are on different surfaces.
D) The instants are simultaneous.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that a point can persist as an endpoint, allowing contiguity of points through the coexistence of an old endpoint and a new contact point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an apparent contradiction between what you observe and what you believe, do you immediately dismiss the belief, or do you, like the author, search for a hidden factor (like the line with an endpoint) that resolves the tension? What hidden factor might you be overlooking in your own spiritual life right now?
?
The author accepts that progress occurs through a succession of discrete instants. In your journey of faith, do you allow yourself to grow step by step, or do you demand instant transformation? How can you embrace the small, successive moments of change that Allah grants you today?
?
The first point persists even when not actively contacting—its existence is not dependent on contact. Similarly, are there truths about your relationship with Allah that persist even when you do not feel them? What 'endpoint' of faith have you neglected because you only value it when it is 'touching' your experience?

First Form of the Proof for the Atom
Razi presents the first form of the proof for the atom using circle tangency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first form of the proof for the atom using point-to-point contact between circles.

Core Idea
The equator's composition of points of contact proves indivisible atoms.

Razi presents a concise proof for the existence of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard). Unlike earlier proofs, this one does not rely on establishing circles and spheres; instead, it uses the observable intersection of celestial circles to argue that the equator must be composed of indivisible points. This lesson unpacks the logic of this elegant argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi contrasts this proof with earlier ones.
2
Identify the key claim
The conclusion is that the equator is composed of points of contact. Trace how Razi arrives at this.
3
Understand the role of point contact
The proof hinges on the idea that a point touches a point, not a line segment. This implies spatial quantization.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why is this proof considered easy and clear? How does it support atomism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك مبني على إثبات الدائرة والكرة . وستعرف : أن إثبات الدائرة والكرة ، يوجب القول بإبطال الجوهر الفرد . وأما هذا البرهان فلا حاجة فيه إلى شيء من المقدمات أصلاً ، وإنه قليل المقدمات ، ظاهر الانتاج . واعلم : أنه يمكن أن نذكر هذا البرهان في صور كثيرة : الأول : أن نقول : لا شك أن دائرة معدل النهار ، ودائرة فلك البروج ، لا تزال ترتفع نقطة بعد نقطة ، حتى تتم الدورة. وإذا كان كذلك ، فتلك النقطة المعينة من دائرة الأفق ، مست جميع الأجزاء المفترضة في معدل النهار على التعاقب والتلاحق . والذي تمسه النقطة نقطة . فيلزم أن يقال : إن معدل النهار مركب من نقط التماس . وذلك يفيد المطلوب
That [proof] is built on establishing the circle and sphere. And you will know that establishing the circle and sphere necessitates the statement of nullifying the atom. As for this proof, it has no need for any of the premises at all, and it has few premises and clear conclusion. And know that we can mention this proof in many forms: The first: that we say: There is no doubt that the circle of the equator and the circle of the zodiac are continuously rising point after point until the rotation is completed. And if that is the case, then that specific point from the horizon circle has touched all the assumed parts of the equator successively and consecutively. And what the point touches is a point. So it necessarily follows that one says: The equator is composed of points of contact. And that yields the required [conclusion].
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage introduces a second proof for the atomic theory. The author first clarifies that while a previous proof relied on establishing circles and spheres, this proof does not require those premises at all; it has few premises and a clear conclusion. Then he states that this proof can be presented in many forms, and he gives the first form: The equator and the zodiac circle intersect continuously point by point. The same point on the horizon touches each successive part of the equator. Since what a point touches is itself a point, the equator must be composed of such points of contact, implying the existence of indivisible atoms (the points). The argument is straightforward: if the equator is made of point contacts, then space is quantized into indivisible units.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد (al-jawhar al-fard)
النقطة (al-nuqṭa)
مماسة (mumāssa)
التشافع (tashāfuʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof requires establishing circles and spheres first.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that this proof has no need for those premises; it is independent.

Checkpoint

A) The equator is composed of points of contact.
B) The circle and sphere must be established first.
C) The proof requires many premises.
D) The point touches an arc.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first form of the proof demonstrates that the equator's composition of point-to-point contacts implies the existence of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that even the most ordinary sight (the sun's path) reveals a hidden structure of indivisible points. When you look at the sky, do you see only the surface, or do you ponder the precise order Allah has placed? How can you train your heart to see His hand in every detail?
?
Razi says this proof is 'clear in its conclusion' with few premises. Yet many people miss it because they do not reflect. What do the things you overlook about Allah's creation say about the state of your contemplation? Will you commit today to stopping once daily to trace a proof of His wisdom in something you see?

Al-Shinti's Objection to Point-to-Point Contact
Al-Shinti enumerates his objection against point-to-point contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shinti's argument that between two intersection points of circles there must be arcs and time, implying continuity and challenging the atomic theory.

Core Idea
Points are imaginary; circles intersect on lines, not points.

Al-Shinti the engineer raised a sophisticated objection against the proof of atoms. He argued that when two circles intersect, they do so on points only in imagination; in reality, there is a continuous line with arcs and time between any two imagined points. This objection seeks to undermine the necessity of point juxtaposition.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Shinti's objection
Read the passage carefully, noting the key claim that between two intersection points there are arcs and time.
The objection is presented as a single statement from al-Shinti.
2
Understand his reasoning
Al-Shinti argues that a circle is a line, and points are only imprinted by imagination. When we imagine a point after another, they come from potentiality to actuality but a line must exist between them.
This challenges the idea of direct point-to-point contact.
3
Identify the challenge
Recognize that this objection, if valid, would refute the necessity of atomic points and support continuity.
4
Prepare for the response
Reflect on how one might counter this argument. The following lesson will present the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن ( الشنتي ) المهندس ، عمل رسالة طويلة في الجواب عن هذا الكلام. وتطويله إنما حصل بسبب أنه ذكر دلائل نفاة الجوهر الفرد . وأما ما يكون جواباً عن هذا الحرف ، فلم يذكر إلا كلاماً واحداً : وهو أنه قال : و لا شك أن هاتين الدائرتين إنما يتلاقيان على نقطة واحدة ، ثم يتلاقيان على نقطة أخرى . إلا أن بين النقطتين : قوسان من تلك الدائرة ، وبين كل أنين : زمان ، قال : والدليل عليه : أن الدائرة في نفسها : خط واحد . والنقطة : إنما ترتسم في الخط ، بسبب التوهم. فليس فيه شيء من النقط [ اصلا ] فإذا توهمنا [ نقطة ] بعد نقطة أخرى ، فقد خرجت هاتان النقطتان من القوة إلى الفعل . إلا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بينهما خط ، والجواب عنه : بالوجهين المذكورين في البرهان المتقدم. ولا بأس بإعادتها . فتقول : أما الوجه الأول : فتقريره : أنه لما تقاطعت هاتان الدائرتان على نقطة . ثم تقاطعتا على النقطة الثانية . فإن فرضنا حصول قوس بينهما . فهل حصلت المماسة على تلك القوس أو لم تحصل ؟ فإن كان الأول ، كان موضع التقاطع بين الدائرتين شيئاً منقسماً . وهذا باطل محال . وإن كان الثاني ، لزم حصول الطفرة . ولما بطل القسمان ، ثبت أنه حصل التقاطع بينهما على نقطة ، ثم على نقطة ثانية . وحينئذ يلزم تشافع النقط . والثاني : إنه لما حصل التقاطع على نقطة ، ففي أول زمان حصول اللاتقاطع على النقطة ، قد حصل التقاطع على نقطة أخرى. وحينئذ يلزم تشافع النقط . وهو المطلوب
And know that al-Shinti the engineer composed a long treatise in response to this speech. And his lengthiness only occurred because he mentioned the proofs of the deniers of the atom. As for what was a response to this point, he only mentioned one statement: which is that he said: There is no doubt that these two circles meet at one point, then they meet at another point. Except that between the two points there are two arcs from that circle, and between each two is time. He said: The evidence for that is that the circle in itself is one line. The point is only imprinted on the line by imagination. So there is no point in it at all. If we imagine a point after another point, then these two points have come from potentiality to actuality. But there must occur a line between them. The response to it is with the two faces mentioned in the previous proof. And there is no harm in repeating them. So we say: As for the first face: its formulation is: When these two circles intersected at a point, then intersected at the second point. If we assume the occurrence of an arc between them, then did contact occur on that arc or not? If the first, then the place of intersection between the two circles would be something divisible, and that is false and impossible. If the second, then a leap (tufra) would occur. And when the two divisions are invalid, it is established that intersection occurred between them at a point, then at a second point. Consequently, juxtaposition (tashāfuʿ) of points becomes necessary. And the second [face]: When intersection occurred at a point, in the first moment of non-intersection at the point, intersection has occurred at another point. Consequently, juxtaposition of points becomes necessary. And that is what is required.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Shinti and the author's response. Al-Shinti argued that between two intersection points there are arcs and time, implying continuity rather than point-to-point contact. He claimed that points are only imagined on a line, so no actual points exist. The author responds using two faces from the previous proof: First, if an arc existed between two intersection points, then either contact occurred on the entire arc (making the intersection divisible) or only on some parts (leading to the absurdity of a leap/skip). Since both options are impossible, the intersections must be at points directly adjacent. Second, at the moment after the first intersection, the second intersection occurs immediately, necessitating contiguous points. This establishes the necessity of point juxtaposition (tashāfuʿ), which supports the atomic theory.

Key Terms
التشافع (tashāfuʿ)
الطفرة (al-ṭufra)
الشنتي (al-Shinti)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Shinti, what is the relationship between the two intersection points of circles?
A) They coincide at the same point.
B) They are separated by arcs and time.
C) They are directly adjacent without anything between.
D) They are only imagined but not actual.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shinti argues that points are imaginary and between any two imagined points on a circle there must be a line, challenging the necessity of point juxtaposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Shinti says that points are only in the imagination. In your own life, what 'points' — things you treat as certain, fixed, or real — might actually be imagined constructs? How does this affect your certainty in your beliefs?
?
If the circle is one continuous line with no actual divisions, and we only imagine points, what does that say about how you perceive the world around you? Are you mistaking your own mental divisions for reality?
?
Between every two imagined points there is a line — a gap you cannot avoid. Are there relationships or responsibilities in your life that you treat as separate 'points' when they are actually continuous with everything else? How can you live more in the reality of that continuity?
