The Two-Face Response to Al-Shinti
The author presents two logical faces to rebut al-Shinti's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical arguments (faces) that refute al-Shinti's objection and prove the necessity of point juxtaposition.

Core Idea
Two logical faces show that only direct point-to-point contact avoids absurdities.

The response to al-Shinti's objection is presented through two faces from a previous proof. The first face examines the assumption of an arc between intersection points and concludes it leads to either divisibility or a leap (tufra). The second face uses immediate temporal succession to show that points must be contiguous. Together, they establish tashāfuʿ.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the entire response, noting the two distinct faces (al-wajh al-awwal and al-thānī).
The response explicitly repeats arguments from a previous proof.
2
Analyze the first face
The first face considers: if we assume an arc exists between the two intersection points, then either contact occurs on the whole arc (making the intersection divisible, impossible) or only on parts (requiring a leap, also impossible). Hence, no arc, so direct point-to-point.
This is a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Analyze the second face
The second face argues: at the first moment after the first intersection, either there is intersection at a second point or not. If not, then no contact, which implies a gap (tufra). But if yes, then two points are simultaneous, necessitating tashāfuʿ.
This uses the concept of time and moment.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both faces lead to the necessity that points are immediately adjacent (tashāfuʿ), which supports the atomic theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Proof of the Atom via Intersecting Circles
Sanusi presents a dilemma for the intersection of two circles to prove the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument for the existence of the indivisible atom using the intersection of two circles and the concept of overlapping points.

Core Idea
Two circles can only intersect at indivisible points, proving the reality of the atom.

This passage presents a geometrical argument from the kalam tradition to establish the existence of the indivisible atom. Using the intersection of two circles, Sanusi shows that the only coherent account of their contact involves points overlapping without division. This lesson unpacks the two horns of the dilemma and the conclusion that necessitates overlapping points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
The passage begins mid-dilemma; focus on the two cases presented.
2
Identify the two rejected possibilities
The argument considers two possibilities for where the circles intersect: a divisible area or a leap. Note why each is rejected.
A divisible area would make the intersection extended; a leap violates continuity.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since both possibilities fail, the intersection must occur at points, and as the circles move, points overlap. This overlapping implies indivisibility.
The second sub-argument reinforces the same conclusion from a temporal perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لم تحصل ؟ فإن كان الأول ، كان موضع التقاطع بين الدائرتين شيئاً منقسماً . وهذا باطل محال . وإن كان الثاني ، لزم حصول الطفرة . ولما بطل القسمان ، ثبت أنه حصل التقاطع بينهما على نقطة ، ثم على نقطة ثانية . وحينئذ يلزم تشافع النقط . والثاني : إنه لما حصل التقاطع على نقطة ، ففي أول زمان حصول اللاتقاطع على النقطة ، قد حصل التقاطع على نقطة أخرى. وحينئذ يلزم تشافع النقط . وهو المطلوب
Or did it not occur? If the first [case], then the place of intersection between the two circles would be a divisible thing. And this is false and impossible. And if the second [case], then a leap would necessarily occur. And when the two divisions are invalidated, it is established that the intersection between them occurred at a point, then at a second point. And then the overlapping of points is necessitated. And the second: when the intersection occurred at a point, then at the first moment of non-intersection on the point, intersection occurred at another point. And then the overlapping of points is necessitated. And that is what is required.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the first face of a proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). It begins by posing a dilemma regarding the intersection of two circles: either the intersection is a divisible area (i.e., a line or surface), or it occurs via a leap (al-tafrah) without contact at any intermediate point. Both possibilities are rejected. If the intersection were a divisible area, then the circles would touch over an extended region, which contradicts the definition of a circle's perfect roundness. If it were a leap, that would violate the principle of continuity. Therefore, the intersection must occur at a point. Furthermore, because the circles move, the intersection occurs first at one point and then at another point, implying that points overlap (tashaffu' al-nuqat) in contact. The second sub-argument adds that at the first moment when the circles cease to intersect at the initial point, they have already intersected at another point, again necessitating overlapping of points. This overlapping proves that points can be adjacent without division, supporting the existence of the indivisible atom. The phrase 'which is required' (wa huwa al-matlab) signals the conclusion.

Key Terms
نقطة (point)
تشافع النقط (overlapping of points)
طفرة (leap)
تقاطع (intersection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Overlapping points implies that points can be divided or that the atom is divisible.
Correction: The proof shows that overlapping points means points are in contact without any gap or division, actually supporting indivisibility.

Checkpoint
What are the two rejected possibilities for the intersection of two circles in this proof?
A) The intersection is a line or a point
B) The intersection is a divisible area or a leap
C) The intersection occurs at two points or not at all
D) The circles do not intersect or intersect at a single point

Key Takeaway
The overlapping of points proves the existence of the indivisible atom by showing that points can be in contact without being divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demands that you accept the reality of indivisible particles based on logical necessity. Do you truly believe that reason alone can establish truths about the physical world, or do you rely on empirical evidence? Where in your own faith have you accepted something without sensory proof? Commit to identifying one such belief today and examining its rational basis.
?
The argument rejects a 'leap' as impossible. Are there areas of your life—your habits, your character—where you expect a sudden leap rather than gradual, continuous effort? What point-by-point progress have you been avoiding? Name one concrete step you will take this week, and hold yourself accountable.
?
The overlapping of points shows that the smallest parts can be in contact without losing their individuality. In your relationships, do you maintain your identity while being close to others, or do you lose yourself? Think of one person with whom you can practice healthy boundaries while remaining connected. What will you do to preserve your own 'indivisible' self?

Third Face: Cube Dragged on a Distance
Sanusi presents the third face using a cube dragged along a distance to prove point overlapping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dragging of a cube along a distance demonstrates the necessity of point overlapping, reinforcing the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Dragging a cube shows that points must overlap to form a line.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concrete physical argument for the indivisible atom. Using the simple act of dragging a cube, he demonstrates that a point, when moved, must come into contact with other points part by part, producing a line. This argument is presented as the third face of the proof, and it leads to the conclusion that points overlap without division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the concise style and the logical progression.
2
Identify the core analogy
Sanusi uses a cube dragged along a distance. Understand that the cube contains an actual point.
The point is already actual, not potential.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the chain: cube has point -> drag -> line formed by point contact -> point contacts point -> overlapping of points.
Each step builds on the previous one.
4
Reflect on the methodological note
Sanusi adds that fewer and clearer premises make a stronger proof. Consider how this applies to the cube argument.
This note shows the author's awareness of argument strength.
5
Connect to atomism
The overlapping of points implies that points are indivisible and that space is composed of them.
This is a key argument for the atom in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في تقرير هذا البرهان : إنا بينا : أن الجسم المكعب ، قد حصلت النقطة فيه بالفعل ، فإذا انجر ذلك المكعب على المسافة ، فقد انجرت تلك النقطة على تلك المسافة ، ويحصل من انجرارها : خط . وذلك الخط إنما ارتسم من مماسة تلك النقطة ، جزءاً فجزءاً . والنقطة لا تماس إلا نقطة فوجب تشافع النقط وهذه الوجوه بأسرها ظاهرة جليه ، وغنية عن إثبات الكرة والدائرة ويجب أن يعلم : أن البرهان كلما كانت مقدماته أقل ، وقوتها أظهر عند العقل ، كان ذلك البرهان أقوى ، وبالقبول أولى . وبالله التوفيق
The third face in establishing this proof: We have shown that in the cubic body, the point actually exists. So when that cube is dragged along the distance, then that point is dragged along that distance, and from its dragging a line is obtained. And that line is traced only from the contact of that point, part by part. And a point contacts only a point, so the overlapping of points is necessitated. And these faces entirely are evident and clear, and need no proof of sphere and circle. And it must be known that whenever the premises of a proof are fewer and their strength is more apparent to the intellect, that proof is stronger and more worthy of acceptance. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the third face of the proof, using a concrete physical example: a cubic body (al-jism al-mukabbab) that contains a point in actuality. When the cube is dragged along a distance, that point is dragged as well, and from its dragging a line is produced. The line is formed by successive contact of the point with points on the distance, part by part. Since a point can only contact another point, the line must consist of overlapping points (tashaffu' al-nuqat). This again supports the existence of the indivisible atom. Then the author remarks that all three faces (wujuh) are self-evident and dispense with the need for proving sphere or circle. He adds a methodological note: the fewer and more evident the premises, the stronger and more acceptable the proof. This highlights the value of clear, concise arguments in kalam.

Key Terms
جسم مكعب
انجر
خط
تشافع النقط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume a point dragged produces a line with length, implying the point itself has size.
Correction: The proof argues that the line is formed by the point contacting other points, not by the point having extension. The overlapping of points shows the line is composed of indivisible atoms without division.

Checkpoint
What does the dragging of a cubic body produce according to this proof?
A) A surface
B) A line
C) Another cube
D) A point

Key Takeaway
The cube-dragging argument shows that physical motion necessarily involves point-to-point contact, proving the reality of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof uses the simplest object—a cube—to uncover a profound truth about creation. What simple object or routine in your life have you neglected to reflect on as a sign of Allah's precise design? Will you take five minutes today to look at one such thing with fresh eyes and ask what it reveals about its Maker?
?
Sanusi notes that the strongest proofs have few and clear premises. Examine your own belief in the Oneness of Allah (Tawheed): is it built on one clear, undeniable premise from the Qur'an or fitrah, or is it buried under doubts? What is that single core reason, and how can you make it so evident that it overrides every whisper of shirk?
?
The passage shows that every line is made of point-to-point contact, implying that no part of creation is accidental or continuous without designed breaks. Your life is similarly made up of discrete moments. Are you treating each moment as a deliberate contact with your Lord, or are you drifting from one to another carelessly? Commit to a specific action to make one moment today consciously obedient.

Defining the Point: Existence and Position
Sanusi gives the point's definition and proves its existence and position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a point is defined as a real existent thing with a position, and why this definition is essential for proving the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
A point must be real and locatable, or lines could not meet.

Sanusi begins his third proof by defining a point. He lists three restrictions, and this lesson focuses on the first two: that the point is a thing (exists) and that it has a position (can be pointed to). These are proved using intuitive arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the initial statement: 'The point is a thing, having position, not divisible.' Notice that Sanusi says if this is established, the indivisible atom follows necessarily.
The definition contains three restrictions.
2
Examine proof for existence
Understand the proof: lines meet at a point; if points weren't real, meeting would be at nothing, which is absurd. This refutes those who say points exist only in imagination.
3
Examine proof for position
The point has position because the extremity of a line can be sensorily pointed to. This is intuitively known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث في إثبات الجوهر الفرد أن نقول : النقطة شيء، ذو وضع لا ينقسم . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فالقول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد : لازم أما بيان الأول : فهو أمر متفق عليه بين جمهور الفلاسفة ، إلا أنا سنقيم البرهان على صحته ، ليصير الكلام برهانياً ، ويخرج عن كونه جدلياً الزاميا . فنقول : قولنا : النقطة شيء ذو وضع ، لا جزء له : كلام مشتمل على قيود ثلاثة : القيد الأول : إنه شيء. والدليل عليه : أن الخط إنما يلقى الخط بالنقطة . فلو لم تكن النقطة موجودة ، لكان الموصوف بالملاقاة عدماً محضاً وإنه باطل في بديهة العقل . وبهذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم . لأنا نقول : الملاقاة حاصلة في الأعيان . والملاقاة إنما حصلت على النقطة ، فوجب كون النقطة موجودة في الأعيان القيد الثاني : قولنا : إنها ذات وضع. والمراد منه : أنه يمكن الإشارة الحسية إليها . والعلم البديهي حاصل بأن طرف الخط ، يمكن الإشارة الحسية إليه القيد الثالث : إنه لا يقبل القسمة والدليل عليه وجوه : الأول : البراهين الأربعة المذكورة في بيان أن موضع الملاقاة من الكرة غير منقسم . والثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن طرف الخط . فنقول : طرف الخط . إن كان فيه طول وعرض ، فهو سطح . فلا يكون طرفاً للخط . وإن حصل فيه الطول فقط ، كان خطاً ، ولا يكون طرفاً للخط . وإن لم يحصل فيه لا طول ولا عرض ، كان غير منقسم البتة . إذ لو كان منقسماً ، لكان أحد قسميه مبايناً عن الآخر ، بحسب الإشارة الحسية. وحينئذ يكون مقدار ذلك المجموع ، أزيد من مقدار أحد نصفيه . وحينئذ يحصل الطول والعرض والثالث : إن النقطة عبارة عن طرف الخط . فهذا الطرف . إن كان منقسماً ، حصل فيه نصفان ، وحينئذ لا يكون كل واحد من هذين النصفين , طرفاً للخط . بل إنما يكون أحد نصفيه طرفاً للخط . وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون كل واحد من النصفين طرفاً ، وليس بطرف ، وذلك محال
The third proof for the establishment of the indivisible atom: We say: the point is a thing, having position, not divisible. And when this is the case, then the statement establishing the indivisible atom is necessary. As for the clarification of the first [attribute]: it is a matter agreed upon among the majority of philosophers, except that we will establish a proof for its correctness, so that the discourse becomes demonstrative and leaves being merely dialectical and compelling. So we say: Our statement: "The point is a thing, having position, no part for it" is speech containing three restrictions: The first restriction: that it is a thing. And the proof for it is that a line meets a line only at a point. So if the point were not existent, then that which is described as meeting would be pure non-existence, and that is invalid in the primary intuition of the intellect. And by this the falsity appears of the statement of those who say: the point has no existence except in the imagination. For we say: meeting occurs in external objects. And meeting occurs only at the point, so it is necessary that the point exists in external objects. The second restriction: our statement: it has position. What is meant by it is that it is possible to point to it by sensory indication. And it is intuitively known that the extremity of a line can be pointed to by sensory indication. The third restriction: it does not accept division. And the proof for it has several aspects: The first: the four proofs mentioned in the clarification that the place of meeting from the sphere is indivisible. The second: the point is the extremity of the line. So we say: the extremity of the line: if it has length and width, then it is a surface, and it would not be an extremity of the line. And if it has length only, then it is a line, and it would not be an extremity of the line. And if it has neither length nor width, then it is completely indivisible. For if it were divisible, then one of its two parts would be distinct from the other with respect to sensory indication. And then the magnitude of that whole would be greater than the magnitude of one of its halves. And then length and width would occur. And the third: the point is the extremity of the line. So this extremity: if it were divisible, then there would be two halves in it, and then each of these two halves would not be an extremity of the line. Rather, only one of its halves would be an extremity of the line. And then it would be necessary that each of the two halves be an extremity and not an extremity, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) based on the definition of a point. It begins by stating the definition: a point is a thing (shay') having position (wad') and not divisible (la yanqasim). The author notes that if this is established, then affirming the indivisible atom follows necessarily. He then breaks down the definition into three restrictions (quyood). The first restriction is that the point is a thing (shay'), i.e., it exists. The proof: a line meets another line only at a point. If points did not exist, then the meeting would occur at nothing, which is absurd. This refutes those who claim points exist only in the imagination. The second restriction is that the point has position, meaning it can be sensorily indicated. This is intuitively known from the extremity of a line. The third restriction is that it is indivisible. The author provides multiple proofs for this. The first proof refers back to the four earlier proofs about the indivisibility of the place of contact on a sphere (presumably from earlier in the text). The second proof: the point is the extremity of a line. The extremity cannot have length and width (otherwise it would be a surface), nor length alone (otherwise it would be a line), so it must have neither, hence it is indivisible. If it were divisible, each half would have a magnitude greater than the other, leading to length and width, which would make it a surface. The third proof: again, the point is the extremity of a line. If the extremity were divisible, it would have two halves, and then each half would both be an extremity (since they are at the end) and not be an extremity (since only the outermost part is the true extremity), which is contradictory. Thus, the point must be indivisible. This entire proof establishes the existence of the indivisible atom by showing that a point, which exists and has position, is necessarily indivisible, and therefore the atom (which is like a point) exists.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
نقطة (point)
وضع (position)
قيد (restriction)
ملاقاة (meeting/contact)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Points have no real existence; they are only in the imagination.
Correction: Lines meet at points in external reality, so points must exist externally.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why must the point exist in external objects?
A) Because philosophers agree on it
B) Because lines meet at a point, and meeting is real
C) Because the point has no length or width
D) Because the point is imaginary

Key Takeaway
The point must be a real existent thing with a position, as shown by the impossibility of lines meeting at nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof relies on the simple intuition that two lines meet at a point—something you have never doubted. But when a philosophical claim denies that obvious intuition (saying the point is not real), do you trust your intellect or the theory? Have you ever dismissed a clear truth because an authority or system told you otherwise? What will you do next time your reason conflicts with common assumptions?
?
If the point were merely imaginary, then the meeting of lines—a real event—would happen at nothing. Think of a belief or habit in your life that has real effects but whose foundation you have dismissed as 'unimportant' or 'imaginary.' How does this lesson urge you to examine the roots of your actions? Will you trace them back to their necessary causes?

Proving the Indivisibility of the Point
Sanusi presents three proofs for the indivisibility of a point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments that demonstrate why a point cannot be divided.

Core Idea
A point has no length or width, so division would create dimension or contradiction.

After establishing that a point exists and has position, Sanusi turns to its indivisibility. He provides multiple proofs, each showing that if a point were divisible, it would cease to be a point.

Guided Path
1
Read the third restriction and first proof
Read that the point does not accept division. The first proof refers to earlier arguments about the sphere's meeting point.
This proof is referenced, not repeated here.
2
Examine the second proof
The second proof argues by elimination: the extremity of a line cannot have length and width (would be surface) nor length alone (would be line), so it must have neither, hence indivisible.
If divisible, parts would have magnitude leading to length/width.
3
Understand the third proof
The third proof is a reductio: if the extremity (point) had two halves, each would both be and not be the extremity, a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third proof for the establishment of the indivisible atom: We say: the point is a thing, having position, not divisible. And when this is the case, then the statement establishing the indivisible atom is necessary. As for the clarification of the first [attribute]: it is a matter agreed upon among the majority of philosophers, except that we will establish a proof for its correctness, so that the discourse becomes demonstrative and leaves being merely dialectical and compelling. So we say: Our statement: "The point is a thing, having position, no part for it" is speech containing three restrictions: The first restriction: that it is a thing. The author provides multiple proofs for this. The first proof refers back to the four earlier proofs about the indivisibility of the place of contact on a sphere (presumably from earlier in the text). The second proof: the point is the extremity of a line. The extremity cannot have length and width (otherwise it would be a surface), nor length alone (otherwise it would be a line), so it must have neither, hence it is indivisible. If it were divisible, each half would have a magnitude greater than the other, leading to length and width, which would make it a surface. The third proof: again, the point is the extremity of a line. If the extremity were divisible, it would have two halves, and then each half would both be an extremity (since they are at the end) and not be an extremity (since only the outermost part is the true extremity), which is contradictory. Thus, the point must be indivisible. This entire proof establishes the existence of the indivisible atom by showing that a point, which exists and has position, is necessarily indivisible, and therefore the atom (which is like a point) exists.

Key Terms
نقطة (point)
طرف الخط (extremity of a line)
قسمة (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A point is a very small dot with some size.
Correction: A point has no length or width; it is indivisible by definition.

Checkpoint
What is the main idea of the second proof?
A) The point is a surface
B) The point has length
C) The point is indivisible because it has no dimension
D) The point is divisible

Key Takeaway
The point is indivisible because any division would introduce dimensions or lead to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a simple point can be proven indivisible through logical reasoning, what does that tell you about the precision needed in defining your own beliefs? Are there areas of your faith where you have accepted vague or contradictory ideas, and will you now examine them with the same rigor?
?
The third proof shows that assuming division leads to a contradiction where each half must be both an extremity and not an extremity. In your own life, are there things you claim to believe but also act as if you don't? Where do you live in such a contradiction, and how will you resolve it today?
?
The second proof eliminates false possibilities to arrive at the truth. What false understandings of God or His attributes have you held or heard from others, and how will you use rational inquiry, as modeled here, to eliminate them and embrace the truth?

Inherence Without Division: Two Types and Four Proofs
Refutation of universal divisibility, classification of inherence, and four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of inherence and why some accidents can be indivisible despite inhering in a divisible subject.

Core Idea
Not all inherence requires division; some attributes are indivisible wholes.

The author begins by rejecting the claim that every accident inhering in a divisible subject must itself be divisible. He then classifies inherence into two types—penetrative and non-penetrative—and provides four clear proofs to show that some attributes (like unity, relations, finiteness, and privations) do not divide with their subjects. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the author's response
The opponent asserts that accidents in a divisible subject must be divisible. The author immediately denies this as a universal rule.
The key phrase is 'هذا الحكم على عمومه ممنوع'.
3
Understand the two types of inherence
The author divides inherence into two types: (1) penetration (سريان) like color in a body, which requires division; (2) non-penetration, where the accident does not spread across parts.
The second type is the focus of the proofs.
4
Examine the four proofs
Each proof demonstrates an attribute that clearly cannot be divided even though its subject is divisible. Study each one carefully.
The proofs cover unity of ten, fatherhood/sonship, finiteness of a line, and negative attributes.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical conclusion
After establishing that inherence without division is possible, the author asks why the case under discussion cannot be of this second type.
This connects the general principle to the specific issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : و الحال في المنقسم يجب أن يكون منقسماً ، قلنا : هذا الحكم على عمومه ممنوع وبيانه : هو أن الحلول على قسمين : حلول بمعنى السريان في المحل . كحلول اللون في الجسم . وهذا يقتضي انقسام الحال ، بسبب انقسام المحل . وذلك لأن أي جزء فرضناه في ذلك الجسم ، فإنه يحصل فيه بعض ذلك اللون . والقسم الثاني : حلول لا بمعنى السريان . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : وصفنا العشرة بأنها عشرة واحدة . فإن الوحدة صفة العشرة . ولا يمكن أن يقال : حصل في كل واحد من أحاد تلك العشرة : جزء من أجزاء تلك الوحدة . فإن الوحدة لا تقبل القسمة . والوجه الثاني في إثبات هذا المطلوب : إنا نصف الشخص المعين ، بأنه أبو زيد ، وابن عمرو . ولا يمكن أن يقال : إنه قام بكل جزء من أجزاء الأب : جزء من أجزاء هذه الأبوة وهذه البنوة. حتى يقال : إنه قام بنصف بدن الأب نصف الأبوة ، وقام بثلثه ثلثها . فإن فساد هذا المعلوم ببديهة العقل . والوجه الثالث : إنا نصف هذا الخط بكونه متناهياً . ولا شك أن الخط منقسم . ولا يمكن أن يقال : قام بنصف هذا الخط نصف تلك النهاية المعينة ، وقام بثلثه ثلثها . لأن تلك النهاية لم تحصل إلا في ذلك المقطع المعين . فإن حصل مقطع آخر ، فذلك نهاية أخرى . الوجه الرابع : الصفات السلبية . كقولنا : ليس له علم ، وليس له مال . فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال : هذه الصفة العدمية ، تنقسم بحسب انقسام ذلك الجسم ، فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف ، قد يكون بحيث لا يلزم من انقسام المحل ، انقسام الحال . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في هذه المسألة واقعاً على هذا الوجه ؟
He said: 'The accident in the divisible must be divisible.' We say: This ruling in its generality is denied. Its clarification: Inherence is of two types: (1) Inherence in the sense of penetration into the subject, like the inherence of color in body. This necessitates division of the accident because of the division of the subject. Because any part we suppose in that body, some of that color occurs in it. (2) The second type: Inherence not in the sense of penetration. There are evidences for it: The first: we describe ten as being one ten. Unity is an attribute of ten. It cannot be said that in each one of the units of that ten a part of the parts of that unity came about. For unity does not admit division. The second evidence for establishing this claim: we describe a specific person as being father of Zayd and son of Amr. It cannot be said that there stands by each part of the father's parts a part of the parts of this fatherhood and this sonship, such that it would be said that half of fatherhood stands by half of the father's body, and one-third of it by one-third. For the corruption of this is known by the intuition of the intellect. The third evidence: we describe this line as being finite. There is no doubt that the line is divisible. It cannot be said that half of that specific finiteness stands by half of this line, and one-third by one-third of it. Because that finiteness occurs only in that specific cut. If another cut occurs, that is another finiteness. The fourth evidence: negative attributes, like our saying: he has no knowledge, he has no property. It cannot be said that this privative attribute divides according to the division of that body. Thus it is established by these evidences that the standing of an attribute with the described can be such that division of the subject does not entail division of the accident. And when this is established, we say: why is it not permissible that the accident in this issue occurs in this manner?
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that whenever an accident (حال) inheres in a divisible subject, the accident must itself be divisible. The author begins by identifying the opponent's assertion: 'the accident in the divisible must be divisible.' He rejects this as universally valid. He then clarifies by dividing inherence into two kinds. The first kind is inherence as penetration (سريان) like color in a body; such inherence does require division of the accident because the color extends throughout the body's parts. The second kind is inherence not as penetration; the accident can be present without being spread out. He provides four evidences (وجوه) to prove this second kind. The first evidence: Unity (وحدة) is an attribute of ten, but unity is indivisible; it is not present in parts. The second: A person can be both father and son; these relations (أبوة and بنوة) do not divide into parts of the body. The third: The finiteness (نهاية) of a line is an attribute of the line as a whole, not of each part; each cut has its own finiteness. The fourth: Negative attributes like 'has no knowledge' are not divisible. These four show that an attribute can inhere in a divisible subject without being divided. The author then poses a rhetorical question: why should the case under discussion not be of this second type?

Key Terms
حلول (hulul - inherence)
سريان (saryan - penetration)
وحدة (wahda - unity)
نهاية (nihaya - finitude)
صفة سلبية (sifa salbiyya - negative attribute)
انقسام (inqisam - division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents that inhere in a divisible subject must themselves be divisible.
Correction: The author shows that some accidents (like unity, relations, finiteness, and negative attributes) are indivisible even though their subjects are divisible, so the universal rule is false.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The second type of inherence shows that an accident can be present without being divided, preserving the possibility of indivisible attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often treat virtues like honesty or patience as something you have in degrees—partially here, partially there. What would it mean to see these virtues as indivisible wholes, like unity in ten? Identify one area where you have been allowing yourself partial honesty, and commit to making it whole.
?
The fourth evidence shows that a negative attribute like 'has no knowledge' is indivisible. Reflect: Are you defining yourself by a lack (e.g., 'I am not good at X') as if it were a divided, partial thing? How can you instead see that lack as a whole condition that you can change decisively, not in fragments?
?
In the second evidence, a person is both father and son without those roles dividing into parts of the body. Are you living some of your roles (parent, spouse, friend) as whole realities or as fragmented, only giving partial attention? What is one role you will treat as indivisible today?

The Trilemma of Accident Division and Its Refutation
The author presents an objection and gives a detailed answer refuting the trilemma and denying the reality of relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that accidents must divide when their subject divides, and why certain attributes like unity and relation are not real existents.

Core Idea
The whole is distinct from its parts; so the objection is a fallacy.

This passage deals with a critical objection to the claim that accidents can inhere indivisibly in divisible subjects. The objector presents a trilemma; the author dissects it and exposes a logical fallacy. The lesson will walk through the objection and the layered refutation, clarifying key concepts about inherence and the nature of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and trilemma
Read the opening objection which presents three possibilities for how an accident relates to the parts of a divisible subject. Notice that the objector considers only one option acceptable—the one that forces division.
The trilemma is: (1) accident not in any part, (2) accident wholly in each part, (3) accident partially in each part.
2
Understand the initial counter-question
The author challenges the necessity of the first option being false. He asks: why must the whole lack the attribute if its parts lack it? This is the key point of contention.
The objector claims it is necessary knowledge; the author questions that claim.
3
Grasp the distinction between whole and parts
The author explains that the aggregate (مجموع) is different from each individual part. Therefore, the absence of an attribute in parts does not automatically entail its absence in the whole.
This distinction reveals the fallacy in the objection.
4
See the final answer on inherence
The author defines inherence as being present in the subject. If the subject is divisible, the accident must be present in each part. He then dismisses unity, relation, and finiteness as non-existent entities, avoiding infinite regress.
This answer upholds the possibility of indivisible inherence for true accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : الدليل على أن الحال ينقسم بانقسام المحل : أنا نقول : إما أن يقال : إنه لم يقم بشيء من أجزاء ذلك المحل . لا تمام ذلك الحال ، ولا شيء من أجزاء ذلك الحال . أو يقال : إنه قام تمام ذلك الحال ، بكل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل . أو يقال : إنه قام بكل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل : جزء من أجزاء ذلك الحال . والأول باطل . لأنه إذا لم يقم بشيء من أجزاء ذلك المحل . لاتمام ذلك الحال ولا شيء من أجزاء ذلك الحال : امتنع وصف ذلك الشيء بتلك الصفة . والعلم به ضروري. والثاني يفيد انقسام الحال ، بسبب انقسام المحل . وهو المطلوب . لأنا نقول : لم قلتم : إنه لما لم يتصف شيء من أجزاء المحل . لا تمام ذلك الحال ، ولا بشيء من أجزائه ، فإنه يمتنع كون ذلك المجموع موصوفاً بتلك الصفة ؟ وتقريره : إن مجموع الشيء ، مغاير لكل واحد . من أجزائه وأبعاضه . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم من خلو كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الموصوف ، عن كل تلك الصفة ، وعن بعض من أبعاضها : خلو مجموع ذلك الموصوف عن تلك الصفة ؟ فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه المغالطة إنما وقعت ، لأجل الغفلة عن الفرق بين مجموع الشيء من حيث إنه ذلك المجموع ، وبين كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع . والجواب : إنه لا معنى لحلول الشيء في الشيء ، إلا كونه حاصلاً فيه. فإذا كان ذلك المحل منقسماً ، فلا معنى لوجود ذلك الشيء ، إلا تلك الأقسام والأبعاض . فإن كان كل واحد من تلك الأقسام والأبعاض ، خالياً عن تلك الصفة . كان العلم الضروري حاصلاً بامتناع كون ذلك المجموع ، موصوفاً بتلك الصفة . وأما الوحدة والإضافة والنهاية ، فلا نسلم أنها أمور موجودة إذ لو كانت الوحدة صفة موجودة لكان كل فرد من أفرادها وحدة. ولزم التسلسل . وكذلك يلزم التسلسل في الإضافات . وأما النهاية فمعناها انقطاع ذلك الشيء وفناؤه . وذلك عدم .
It cannot be said: The proof that the accident divides with the division of the subject is that we say: It must be said either that it stood by no part of that subject - neither entirely nor partially - or that the entirety of that accident stood by each part of that subject, or that by each part of that subject stood a part of that accident. The first is false because if it does not stand by any part of that subject, neither entirely nor partially, then describing that thing with that attribute is impossible, and that knowledge is necessary. The second entails division of the accident due to division of the subject, and that is the desired conclusion. But we say: Why do you say that when not a single part of the subject is characterized by the entire accident nor by any of its parts, then it is impossible for that aggregate to be described with that attribute? Its explanation: The aggregate of a thing is different from each of its parts and portions. So why do you say that the lack of each part of that described thing of the entire attribute and of some of its parts entails the lack of the whole described thing of that attribute? Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that this fallacy only occurred due to negligence of the distinction between the aggregate of a thing inasmuch as it is that aggregate, and each of the parts of that aggregate. The answer: There is no meaning to the inherence of a thing in a thing except its being present in it. So if that subject is divisible, then there is no meaning to the existence of that thing except those divisions and parts. If every one of those divisions and parts is devoid of that attribute, then necessary knowledge obtains in the impossibility of that aggregate being described with that attribute. As for unity, relation, and finiteness, we do not concede that they are existing things. For if unity were an existing attribute, then every individual of it would be a unity, and an infinite regress would follow. Similarly, infinite regress would follow in relations. As for finiteness, its meaning is the cessation of that thing and its annihilation, which is non-existence.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the author's position that accidents can inhere indivisibly. The objector constructs a trilemma: if an accident inheres in a divisible subject, either (a) it does not inhere in any part at all, (b) it inheres wholly in each part, or (c) it inheres partially in each part. He claims (a) is impossible because if no part has the attribute, the whole cannot have it - this is taken as necessary knowledge. (b) entails division of the accident, which the objector uses as proof that division is necessary. The author refutes this by questioning the necessity: why must the whole lack the attribute if its parts lack it? He explains that the whole is distinct from its parts, and it does not automatically follow. He calls this a fallacy arising from ignorance of the difference between the aggregate and each part individually. Then he provides his own answer, asserting a key principle: inherence means the accident is present in the subject. If the subject is divisible, the accident must be present in each part, otherwise it cannot be said to inhere. For negative attributes and certain relational attributes, the objector's examples fail because they are not real existents: unity, relation, and finiteness are not positive attributes; finiteness is just non-existence. Thus the objection collapses.

Key Terms
حلول (hulul - inherence)
انقسام (inqisam - division)
مجموع (majmu' - whole/aggregate)
مغالطة (mughalata - fallacy)
ضروري (daruri - necessary)
تسلسل (tasalsul - infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The assumption that the absence of an attribute in parts necessarily implies absence in the whole.
Correction: The author shows that the whole is distinct from its parts; thus the inference does not hold automatically. Logical necessity requires additional premises.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, what is the meaning of inherence (حلول)?
A) The accident being potentially in the subject
B) The accident being present in the subject
C) The accident being divisible like the subject
D) The accident being an illusion

Key Takeaway
Accidents can inhere in divisible subjects without themselves dividing, because the whole is not reducible to its parts, and only real existents (not unity, relation, or finiteness) are subject to inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged your entire character or faith based on a single failure or weakness in one area? How does the author's distinction between part and whole challenge you to reconsider that judgment? What will you change today about how you assess yourself?
?
The author denies that 'unity' and 'relation' are real things. When you say 'I am a Muslim' or 'I have faith', do you treat that as a real attribute that must be proven in every action, or as a conceptual label? How does that distinction affect the way you pursue sincerity and avoid hypocrisy?
?
The trilemma is labeled a fallacy because of neglecting the difference between the aggregate and its parts. In which relationship, responsibility, or understanding of deen have you made a similar mistake—assuming that if a part fails, the whole is invalid? Will you stop using that flawed logic, and instead examine the whole on its own terms?

The First Proof: Finite Body and Infinite Parts
Razi presents the first proof against infinite divisibility via conditional reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's first proof that a finite body cannot be infinitely divisible, specifically how a conditional argument leads to an absurd consequent.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility would imply infinite actual parts, which is impossible.

Razi begins his demonstration with a clear conditional proof. He assumes that a finite body could be divided infinitely, then shows this would force the body to have infinite actual parts — something all philosophers agree is absurd. By rejecting the consequent, the original assumption is refuted. This lesson walks through each logical step, helping you see why infinite divisibility is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure ('if... then...').
The proof is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional and its parts
Find the antecedent ('if a finite body were capable of infinite divisions') and the consequent ('then it would be possible to have infinite actual parts').
The consequent is declared false by consensus.
3
Understand the justification of the conditional
Razi explains why the conditional holds: each division is individually possible, and they don't conflict. Therefore, their simultaneous occurrence is also possible.
This is the key logical move.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the consequent is impossible (infinite parts in a finite body), the antecedent must be false. Hence, a finite body cannot be infinitely divisible.
This is a classic 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الدلائل الدالة على هذا المطلوب كثيرة: الحجة الأولى: لو كان الجسم المتناهي في المقدار، قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها، لأمكن كون هذا الجسم المتناهي في المقدار مؤلفاً، من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. والثاني باطل، فالمقدم باطل. بيان الشرطية: إنه لما كان كل واحد من تلك الانقسامات ممكناً في نفسه، ولم يكن دخول بعضها في الوجود مانعاً من دخول الباقي في الوجود، فحينئذ يصدق أن كل واحد منها ممكن. ووجود بعضها لا ينافي وجود الباقي. وكل أشياء يكون كل واحد منها ممكناً في نفسه، ولا يكون وجود البعض مانعاً من وجود الباقي، وجب أن يكون حصولها على سبيل الاجتماع: ممكناً. فثبت: أن الجسم المتناهي في المقدار، لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها، لأمكن كونه مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وهذا التالي محال باتفاق الفلاسفة، فوجب أن يكون المقدم باطلاً.
Know that the proofs indicating this claim are many: The first proof: If the finite body were capable of infinite divisions, then it would be possible for this finite body to be composed of actually infinite parts. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. Explanation of the conditional: Since each of those divisions is possible in itself, and the occurrence of some of them in existence does not prevent the occurrence of the rest in existence, then it is true that each of them is possible. And the existence of some does not contradict the existence of the rest. And for any things each of which is possible in itself, and the existence of some does not prevent the existence of the rest, it is necessary that their occurrence together be possible. So it is established: if the finite body were capable of infinite divisions, then it would be possible for it to be composed of actually infinite parts. And this consequent is impossible by agreement of the philosophers, so the antecedent must be false.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by conditional reasoning. The argument is structured as a conditional: if a finite body (جسم متناهي المقدار) were divisible infinitely (قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها), then it would be possible for it to be composed of actually infinite parts (أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل). The consequent is declared false by consensus of the philosophers (باتفاق الفلاسفة), so the antecedent is false. The key step is justifying the conditional: each individual division is possible in itself, and the occurrence of some does not preclude the occurrence of others. For a set of things where each is individually possible and they are mutually non-exclusive, their simultaneous existence is possible. Thus, infinite possible divisions would entail the possibility of their simultaneous actuality, i.e., the body having infinite actual parts. Since that is impossible, the original assumption of infinite divisibility is false. The argument follows a logical form common in kalam: establishing absurdity of a consequent to refute a premise.

Key Terms
جسم متناهي المقدار
انقسامات لا نهاية لها
أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل
باتفاق الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument assumes that infinite divisions actually happen one after another in time, but the argument only considers possibility, not actual occurrence.
Correction: Razi's argument is about possibility: if infinite divisions are individually possible and non-conflicting, then it is possible for them to exist together. He does not claim they actually do occur simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What is the logical flaw Razi identifies in the assumption of infinite divisibility?
A) It requires infinite time to complete.
B) It leads to the absurd consequence of a finite body having infinite actual parts.
C) It contradicts empirical evidence from physics.
D) It is rejected by religious authorities.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof uses conditional reasoning: since infinite divisibility would allow infinite actual parts (impossible), the original claim must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a finite body cannot contain infinite parts. Your own body is finite and limited. Do you live as if you have infinite time or capacity? How does the finitude of your existence call you to prioritize what truly matters before it ends?
?
The argument depends on the principle that if each part is possible and non-conflicting, then together they are possible. In your life, do you try to pursue many goals simultaneously that are actually mutually exclusive? What would it mean for you to accept your limitations and focus on what is truly compatible?
?
If you believed that your body were infinitely divisible—that you could keep dividing your attention, energy, and devotion without end—would that change how you value each finite moment? How does this logical truth press you to make your worship and actions count?

First Proof Against Infinite Divisibility
Presents the first proof that a finite body cannot have infinite divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof against infinite divisibility of a finite body, and why the impossibility of an actual infinite leads to the rejection of potential infinite divisibility.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility implies an actual infinite, which is impossible.

This lesson introduces the first of several proofs that a finite body cannot be divided infinitely. The argument uses a conditional syllogism: if infinite divisibility were possible, then the body would be composed of actually infinite parts. Since philosophers deem an actual infinite impossible, the original assumption must be false. We will unpack each step of this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The proof is a hypothetical syllogism.
2
Identify the conditional and its parts
Identify the antecedent (finite body capable of infinite divisions) and the consequent (body composed of actually infinite parts).
The consequent is stated as 'impossible by agreement of the philosophers'.
3
Examine the justification of the conditional
The author explains why the antecedent leads to the consequent: each division is possible and mutually compatible, so all can exist together.
This uses the principle of possibility aggregation.
4
Understand the impossibility of the consequent
The consequent is impossible because an actual infinite cannot exist in a finite body (philosophers' consensus).
This is the key premise.
5
Conclude the proof
Since the consequent is impossible, the antecedent must be false. Thus, a finite body cannot have infinite divisibility.
The conclusion is the denial of the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الدلائل الدالة على هذا المطلوب كثيرة : الحجة الأولى : لو كان الجسم المتناهي في المقدار ، قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لأمكن كون هذا الجسم المتناهي في المقدار مؤلفاً ، من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل . والثاني باطل ، فالمقدم باطل . بيان الشرطية : إنه لما كان كل واحد من تلك الانقسامات ممكناً في نفسه ، ولم يكن دخول بعضها في الوجود مانعاً من دخول الباقي في الوجود، فحينئذ يصدق أن كل واحد منها ممكن . ووجود بعضها لا ينافي وجود الباقي . وكل أشياء يكون كل واحد منها ممكناً في نفسه ، ولا يكون وجود البعض مانعاً من وجود الباقي ، وجب أن يكون حصولها على سبيل الاجتماع : ممكناً . فثبت : أن الجسم المتناهي في المقدار ، لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لأمكن كونه مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل . وهذا التالي محال باتفاق الفلاسفة ، فوجب أن يكون المقدم باطلاً
Know that the proofs indicating this claim are many: The first proof: If a body finite in magnitude were capable of infinite divisions, then it would be possible for this body finite in magnitude to be composed of actually infinite parts. The second is false, so the antecedent is false. Explanation of the conditional: Since each one of those divisions is possible in itself, and the entry of some of them into existence does not prevent the entry of the rest into existence, then it is true that each one of them is possible. And the existence of some does not contradict the existence of the rest. And for any things each one of which is possible in itself, and the existence of some does not prevent the existence of the rest, it is necessary that their occurring together is possible. So it is established: that a body finite in magnitude, if it were capable of infinite divisions, it would be possible for it to be composed of actually infinite parts. And this consequent is impossible by the agreement of the philosophers, so the antecedent must be false.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the infinite divisibility of a finite body. It is a conditional argument (hypothetical syllogism). The author states that if a body of finite size could be divided infinitely, then it would be possible for that body to be composed of actually infinite parts. This consequent is deemed impossible by consensus of the philosophers (presumably meaning the impossibility of an actual infinite). The proof then explains the conditional: because each division is possible in itself and the existence of some does not preclude the others, therefore it is logically possible for all to exist together. This leads to the possibility of an actual infinite in a finite body, which is impossible. Therefore, the initial assumption (that the body is capable of infinite divisions) is false. The argument relies on the principle that if each individual possibility is not mutually exclusive, then their conjunction is also possible. This is a standard kalam argument against infinite regress in physical bodies.

Key Terms
قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها
أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل
ممكن في نفسه
اجتماع الممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes infinite divisibility is possible, but it actually argues from that assumption to an impossibility.
Correction: The proof does not assume infinite divisibility; it shows that if it were possible, it would lead to an impossible consequence, so it must be false.

Checkpoint

A) A finite body cannot be subject to infinite divisions.
B) A finite body is composed of infinite parts.
C) Infinite divisibility is possible.
D) Philosophers agree that infinite parts exist.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that a finite body cannot be subject to infinite divisions because that would entail the impossible existence of an actual infinite.

?

?
This argument depends on logical reasoning to show physical truth. Have you ever dismissed a logical proof because its conclusion was uncomfortable? Where in your faith are you avoiding following reason to its end? What would change if you did?

The Proof from Exclusive Capabilities for Specific Ratios
A refutation of infinite potential divisions using exclusive capabilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an argument that if a body had infinite potential divisions, it would entail actually infinite parts, which is impossible, based on the exclusivity of capacities for specific ratios.

Core Idea
Infinite potential divisions lead to actual infinite parts, which is impossible.

This passage presents a refutation of the claim that infinite potential divisions in a body do not entail actual infinite parts. The argument uses the exclusivity of each segment's capacity to accept a specific ratio (e.g., half, third) to deduce that these capacities are real states, leading to the conclusion that the body would have actual infinite parts. Understanding this proof strengthens the doctrine of atomism or finite divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional statement, the objection, and the response.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
The passage begins with a conditional: if infinite possible segments, then actual. Then an objection: half, third, etc., only after actual parts. The author rejects the need to concede actual parts.
3
Understand the author's alternative argument
The author argues that each segment has an exclusive capacity for a specific ratio, and these capacities are real states. This leads to the conclusion that a line with infinite potential divisions would have actually infinite parts.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
The conclusion is that actually infinite parts are impossible, so the initial assumption of infinite potential divisions must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توجب قبول نسبة معينة . فلو كانت المقاطع الممكنة غير متناهية ، لزم كونها حاصلة بالفعل . فإن قالوا : هذه الأوصاف . أعني : كونه نصفاً وثلثاً وربعاً ، لا تحصل إلا بعد حصول الأجزاء بالفعل ، ونحن لا نساعد على حصول تلك الأجزاء فيها بالفعل . فنقول في الجواب : لا حاجة بنا إلى إثبات ما ذكرتم . بل نقول قبول نسبة النصف بعينها مخصوص بمقطع معين ، ويستحيل حصول تلك النسبة في مقطع غيره . وكذا القول في الثلث والربع . فإن كل واحد من تلك المقاطع مخصوصاً بقبول نسبة معينة ، وكان قبول تلك النسبة ممتنع الثبوت لعينه ولذاته في سائر المقاطع . فثبت : أن كل واحد منها مختص بقابلية نسبة بعينها ، وهذه القابليات أحوال حاصلة في نفس الأمر . فيلزم : أن الخط المعين ، لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لكان مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل . ومعلوم : أن هذا محال . والله أعلم
It necessitates acceptance of a specific ratio. If the possible segments were infinite, then they would necessarily be actual. If they say: These descriptions, i.e., being half, third, and quarter, only occur after the parts become actual, and we do not concede that those parts are actual in it. We say in reply: We have no need to prove what you mentioned. Rather we say: The acceptance of the ratio of half itself is exclusive to a specific segment, and it is impossible for that ratio to occur in another segment. The same applies to the third and the quarter. For each one of those segments is exclusive in its acceptance of a specific ratio, and the acceptance of that ratio is impossible in essence and in itself in the other segments. So it is established that each one of them is specific to a capability for a specific ratio, and these capabilities are states that are actual in themselves. It follows that if a given line were capable of infinite divisions, it would be composed of actually infinite parts. And it is known that this is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the impossibility of infinite potential divisions in a body. It begins by stating a conditional: if the possible segments were infinite, they would be actual. An objection is then raised: the attributes of being half, third, or quarter only occur after actual division, and the opponent does not grant actual parts. The author responds by shifting the argument: they do not need to prove actual parts; rather, each segment has a unique and exclusive capacity to accept a specific ratio, and this capacity is impossible in other segments. Therefore, these capacities are real states (ahwal) that exist in themselves. As a result, if a line were capable of infinite divisions, it would have to be composed of actually infinite parts, which is absurd.

نسبة (ratio)
مقطع (segment)
قابلية (capability)

انقسام (division)
أجزاء (parts)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite potential divisions in a body do not entail actual infinite parts; they are merely potential.
Correction: The argument shows that each potential division has a unique capacity that is a real state, leading to actual infinite parts if potentials are infinite.

Checkpoint

A) A line can have infinite potential divisions without actual parts.
B) A line capable of infinite divisions must be composed of actually infinite parts, which is impossible.
C) The opponent must concede actual parts from the start.
D) Half, third, and quarter are not real attributes.

Key Takeaway
Infinite potential divisions would require actually infinite parts, which is impossible, so bodies cannot have infinite potential divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Unity is Identical to the Essence: Sanusi's Fifth Proof
Sanusi's fifth argument: unity is the individuated essence, not an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fifth proof that unity is identical to the individuated essence and why this entails that division cannot create new essences, thus precluding actual infinite parts.

Core Idea
Unity is not an extra property; it is the thing's own individuated essence.

This passage presents the fifth proof against the idea that a body could contain actually infinite parts. Sanusi argues that if unity were an accident added to the essence, it would lead to an infinite regress. Therefore, unity must be the essence itself. This has profound implications for how we understand division and composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice that it begins with a definition and then builds a proof by contradiction.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Focus on the conditional: if unity were an accident, then distinguishing each unity would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress. Ask yourself why this regress is impossible.
This is the core logical move.
3
Trace the consequence for body division
After establishing that unity is the essence, Sanusi applies this to a body that becomes two. Understand why the original essence would have to perish if unity were the essence.
Contrast this with what actually happens in division.
4
Grasp the conclusion about infinite parts
The final sentence ties the argument back to the impossibility of actual infinite parts. Understand why infinite divisibility would imply infinite parts in actuality.
This is the target of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن وحدة الشيء ، عبارة عن ذاته المتعينة المتشخصة . لأن الوحدة لو كانت صفة زائدة على تلك الذات المتعينة ، لكانت الوحدات متساوية في ماهية كونها وحدة فيكون امتياز كل واحد من أجزاء هذه الماهية عن الآخر ، بوحدة أخرى. ولزم التسلسل . وهو محال . فثبت : أن وحدة كل شيء، عبارة عن ذاته المتعينة المتشخصة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، لزم أن يقال : إن زوال تلك الوحدة يقتضي فناء تلك الذات المتعينة . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو كان الجسم واحداً في نفسه ، فإذا صار : اثنين ، وجب الحكم بأن تلك الذات التي كانت موجودة قد عدمت وفنيت وحدثت ذاتان متفاوتان متغايرتان للذات الأولى . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً ، علمنا : أن التفريق عبارة عن تبعيد المتجاورين . وذلك يوجب الجزم بأن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها ، لوجب كونه مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل
The Fifth Proof: That the unity of a thing is its specific, individuated essence. Because if unity were an accident superadded to that specific essence, then all unities would be equal in the quiddity of being unity, and thus each part of this quiddity would be distinguished from another by another unity, leading to an infinite regress. This is impossible. So it is established that the unity of every thing is its specific, individuated essence. And when this is established, it follows that the removal of that unity necessitates the annihilation of that specific essence. When this is established, we say: If a body were one in itself, and then becomes two, it would be necessary to judge that that essence which existed has ceased and perished, and two distinct essences different from the first have come into existence. And since that is false, we know that division (tafriq) is merely the separation of contiguous parts. And that necessitates the certainty that if a body were capable of infinite divisions, it would be necessary that it be composed of actually infinite parts.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof against actual infinite parts. The author first argues that the unity (wahda) of a thing is nothing other than its individuated essence (dhat muta'ayyina mutashakhkhisa). If unity were an additional attribute, then all unities would share the same quiddity (mahiyya) of being unity, and the distinction between them would require further unities ad infinitum—an impossible regress. Therefore, unity is identical to the essence. Consequently, if unity disappears, the essence itself perishes. Now consider a body that is one and then becomes two: if unity were the essence, the original essence would have to perish and two new essences arise, which is absurd. Hence, division is merely the separation of contiguous parts, not the destruction of essences. From this, it follows that if a body were divisible infinitely, it would actually contain infinite parts—an impossibility.

Key Terms
وحدة (unity)
ذات (essence)
تعين (individuation)
تشخص (specification)
صفة زائدة (superadded attribute)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
تفريق (division/separating)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is a property that can be added or removed without affecting the essence itself.
Correction: The proof shows that if unity were an accident, it would cause an infinite regress. Therefore, unity must be identical to the essence, so removing unity would destroy the essence.

Checkpoint
Why must unity be identical to the essence according to the fifth proof?
A) Because unity is a simple concept.
B) Because if unity were an accident, it would lead to an infinite regress of unities.
C) Because the opponent concedes it.
D) Because the essence is prior to unity.

Key Takeaway
Unity is identical to the essence; thus, division cannot create new essences, preventing actual infinite parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very identity (your 'self') is not a collection of added traits but your indivisible essence, then what are the false 'add-ons' you have been clinging to as if they define you? Are you willing to let them go and simply be?
?
Sanusi proves that dividing something does not create new essences—it only separates what was already there. In which of your relationships have you artificially created divisions or new labels that hide an underlying unity? How can you work to see that unity today?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible—there must be a stopping point. In your own life, where have you been pursuing an infinite regress of goals, achievements, or validations, each requiring another to feel complete? What would it mean to stop and recognize that your essence is already whole?

The Sixth Proof: Adjacency Forces Actual Division
Sanusi's sixth argument: contact with different things necessitates actual division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's sixth proof that contact with different things forces actual division in a body, and how he responds to the objection that only surfaces differ.

Core Idea
Contact with different things logically forces division in a body's essence.

Sanusi now presents a clever argument that does not rely on physical separation but on logical necessity. By analyzing what happens when a body touches two different things at its ends, he shows that the body must actually be divided. But an opponent might object that only the surfaces differ—Sanusi anticipates this and gives a careful reply. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Let the full argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the logical flow from contact to division.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify how contact with two different things at opposite ends necessitates an actual division between those ends. Trace the recursive implication of infinite divisions.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
Consider the objection that only the surfaces are distinct, not the essence. See how Sanusi responds by examining whether the surfaces are parts or accidents.
The reply forces the conclusion that essence itself must be divided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يماس أو يحاذي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً ، أو بماس أو يحاذي بطرفه الثاني شيئاً آخر . وهذا الاختلاف الحاصل بسبب الملاقاة والمحاذاة ، يوجب حصول الانقسام بالفعل . ثم نقول : إن أحد قسميه يلاقي القسم الثاني بأحد وجهيه دون الثاني فوجب أن ينقسم كل واحد من هذين القسمين . ثم الكلام في نصف النصف كما في الأول . فإن كان قبول القسمة حاصلا إلى غير النهاية وكان المقتضى لقبول الانقسام بالفعل ، حاصلا إلى غير النهاية . فحينئذ يلزم حصول الانقسامات التي لا نهاية لها بالفعل . فإن قالوا : الجسم إذا لقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً ، وبطرفه الثاني شيئاً آخر ، فاختلاف الملاقين يقتضي تغاير الطرفين بالفعل ، فلا جرم قلنا : إن أحد السطحين يغاير السطح الآخر . لكن لم قلتم : إن هذا المعنى يوجب وقوع الانقسام في ذات الجسم ؟ ونقول في الجواب : هذان السطحان . إن كانا جزءين من ماهية الجسم ، لزم من تغايرهما ، وقوع القسمة في ذات الجسم . وإن كانا عرضين حالين فيه ، أوجب تغايرهما حصول التغاير بين كليهما . ولا يمكن حلول كل عرض في عرض آخر ، إلى غير النهاية ، بل يجب الانتهاء إلى . وذلك يوجب وقوع القسمة في الذات . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب .
The Sixth Proof: Every body must either touch or be adjacent by one of its ends to something, or be adjacent by its second end to something else. This difference occurring due to contact and adjacency necessitates actual division. Then we say: One of its two parts contacts the second part by one of its faces and not the other, so it is necessary that each of these two parts be divided. Then the discussion about half of the half is as before. If the acceptance of division occurs infinitely and the efficient cause for accepting actual division occurs infinitely, then it follows that there occur actually infinite divisions. If they say: When a body contacts by one end a thing and by its second end another thing, the difference of the two contacted things necessitates the actual distinction of the two ends, so we indeed say that one surface is different from the other. But why do you say that this meaning entails the occurrence of division in the essence of the body? We say in reply: These two surfaces, if they are two parts of the quiddity of the body, then from their distinction follows the occurrence of division in the essence of the body. If they are two accidents subsisting in it, their distinction necessitates distinction between the two accident-substrates, but it is impossible for an accident to subsist in another accident ad infinitum, rather it must terminate at [the essence]. This necessitates the occurrence of division in the essence. And thereby the desired is obtained.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof. The author argues that every body, when it contacts two different things at its two ends, must actually have a division between those ends. This actual division entails that each of the resulting parts also contact different things, leading recursively to actually infinite divisions. An objection is raised: the difference of the contacted things only proves the surfaces are distinct, not that the body's essence is divided. The author answers by analyzing the nature of the surfaces: if they are parts of the quiddity, then their distinction implies division in the essence. If they are accidents, then the distinction of accidents forces distinction of the underlying substance, and since an accident cannot subsist in another accident infinitely, the regress terminates at the essence, again forcing division in the essence.

Key Terms
مماسة (contact)
محاذاة (adjacency)
قسم (part)
نصف (half)
مقتضى (efficient cause)
عرض (accident)
ماهية (quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division in a body only occurs if it is physically separated.
Correction: Sanusi's proof shows that logical distinction from different contacts forces actual division in the essence, even without physical separation.

Checkpoint

A) The body's own nature.
B) The difference of two things contacting the body at opposite ends.
C) The infinity of possible divisions.
D) The existence of accidents.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's sixth proof uses contact with different things to establish actual division in the body's essence, refuting the idea that only surfaces differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the idea that contact with different things forces a real distinction and division in the body's essence. ?

?
The proof shows that infinite actual divisions are impossible, so there must be a stopping point—the atom. In your spiritual journey, you may feel you are endlessly divided by distractions, desires, and doubts. Where is the 'atom'—the irreducible core of your identity as a servant of Allah—that can never be further divided? How can you anchor your efforts to that singular point and prevent endless fragmentation?

The Sphere's Rotation and Distinct Orbits
Sanusi's seventh argument: the sphere's rotation shows distinct actual orbits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the rotation of a sphere to argue that a continuous magnitude contains actual distinct parts, each with unique properties.

Core Idea
The rotation of a sphere reveals distinct actual orbits, each with a unique speed.

Sanusi presents his seventh proof, using a simple physical example. By considering the rotation of a sphere, he shows that different orbits (circles of latitude) actually exist and have distinct properties. This argument challenges the idea that a continuous magnitude has only potential parts. Let’s examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through.
The passage is a complete argument; note its logical flow.
2
Visualize the rotating sphere
Imagine a sphere rotating on an axis. Identify the equator, the two poles, and the circles (orbits) between them. This physical image is crucial.
The equator and poles are actual points on the sphere.
3
Note the relationship between distance, width, and speed
Sanusi points out that orbits closer to the equator are wider and faster than those near the poles. This is a key observation.
Each orbit has a unique combination of width and speed.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that the motion of each orbit is exclusive to that orbit's magnitude, meaning each orbit is a distinct actual part of the sphere.
This implies that the sphere is not merely continuous but contains actual distinct parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن الكرة إذا استدارت ، فقد حصلت منطقتها ، موجودة بالفعل . وحصل فيها نقطتان بالفعل . هما القطبان للكرة . ثم إنه يحصل بين المنطقة وبين القطبين ، مدارات كثيرة . وكل ما كان منها أقرب إلى المنطقة ، كان أوسع مداراً ، وأسرع حركة من الذي يكون أبعد منها . ولا شك أن قول تلك الحركة المختصة بذلك القدر المعين من السرعة والبطء ، مختص بذلك المقدار المعين ، وممتنع الحصول في سائر المدارات .
The Seventh Proof: When a sphere rotates, its equator (mintaqa) actually exists, and there occur in it two points actually, which are the poles of the sphere. Then between the equator and the poles, there occur many orbits. And whichever of them is closer to the equator is wider in orbit and faster in motion than that which is farther from it. And there is no doubt that the said motion specific to that particular measure of speed and slowness is exclusive to that particular magnitude, and impossible to occur in the other orbits.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof. It uses the example of a rotating sphere. When the sphere rotates, its equator and poles are actual points. Between the equator and the poles, there are many orbits (circles of latitude). The orbits closer to the equator are wider and move faster than those nearer the poles. Each orbit has a unique speed specific to its size, and this speed cannot occur in any other orbit. This demonstrates that these orbits are distinct and actual magnitudes. Although not explicitly stated in the given text, the implication is that if the sphere is continuous, there would be infinitely many such orbits, each with a unique actual property, thus entailing actual infinite parts.
