The Sixth Proof: Contact Necessitates Actual Division
Sixth argument proving bodies have finite parts based on contact and orientation differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the difference in contact between a body's ends proves that bodies have finite, actually divided parts.

Core Idea
When a body touches two different things, its essence must be actually divided.

This lesson presents the sixth proof against the infinite divisibility of bodies. The argument is based on a simple observation: when a body contacts two different objects with its two ends, the difference in contact forces an actual division in the body itself. The author then defends this conclusion against an objection that the division might be merely accidental.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic argument
Read the initial statement: every body that touches two different things must have an actual division. Note how the difference in contact necessitates this.
Focus on the phrases 'ملاقاة' (contact) and 'محاذاة' (alignment).
2
Follow the infinite regress
The argument then extends: if each divided part also has contact differences, then there would be infinite actual divisions, which is impossible.
This shows why infinite divisibility is impossible.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objector claims the surfaces differ only accidentally. The author refutes this by showing that whether surfaces are essential or accidental, division must reach the essence.
The key move: accidents cannot subsist in accidents infinitely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يماس أو يحاذي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً، أو يماس أو يحاذي بطرفه الثاني شيئاً آخر. وهذا الاختلاف الحاصل بسبب الملاقاة والمحاذاة، يوجب حصول الانقسام بالفعل. ثم نقول : إن أحد قسميه يلاقي القسم الثاني بأحد وجهيه دون الثاني [فوجب (١)] أن ينقسم كل واحد من هذين القسمين. ثم الكلام في نصف النصف كما في الأول. فإن كان قبول القسمة حاصلا إلى غير النهاية وكان المقتضى لقبول الانقسام بالفعل، حاصلا إلى غير النهاية. فحينئذ يلزم حصول الانقسامات التي لا نهاية لها بالفعل. فإن قالوا : الجسم إذا لقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً، وبطرفه الثاني شيئاً آخر، فاختلاف الملاقين يقتضي تغاير الطرفين بالفعل، فلا جرم قلنا : إن أحد السطحين يغاير السطح الآخر. لكن لم قلتم : إن هذا المعنى يوجب وقوع الانقسام في ذات الجسم ؟ ونقول في الجواب : هذان السطحان. إن كانا جزءين من ماهية الجسم، لزم من تغايرهما، وقوع القسمة في ذات الجسم. وإن كانا عرضين حالين فيه، أوجب تغايرهما حصول التغاير بين كليهما. ولا يمكن حلول كل عرض في عرض آخر، إلى غير النهاية، بل يجب الانتهاء إلى. وذلك يوجب وقوع القسمة في الذات. وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب.
The sixth proof: Every body must either touch or be aligned with one of its ends to something, or touch or be aligned with its second end to something else. This difference arising due to contact and alignment necessitates actual division. Then we say: One of its two parts meets the second part with one of its faces without the second, so it is necessary that each of these two parts divides. Then the discussion about half of the half is as in the first case. If the acceptance of division occurs ad infinitum and the requirement for acceptance of actual division occurs ad infinitum, then it would necessitate the occurrence of divisions that have no end actually. If they say: When a body meets something with one of its ends and something else with its second end, the difference of the two things met necessitates the actual differentiation of the two ends, so we have said: One of the two surfaces differs from the other surface. But why do you say that this meaning necessitates the occurrence of division in the essence of the body? And we say in answer: These two surfaces. If they are two parts of the essence of the body, their differentiation necessitates the occurrence of division in the essence of the body. And if they are two accidents subsisting in it, their differentiation necessitates the occurrence of differentiation between their two loci. And it is impossible for each accident to subsist in another accident ad infinitum, but it must end at [something]. And that necessitates the occurrence of division in the essence. And then the desired is obtained.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof against the infinite divisibility of bodies, based on contact and orientation. The author argues that when a body touches two different things with its two ends, the difference in contact necessitates an actual division in the body. If the body were infinitely divisible, then each part would also have such contact differences, leading to an infinite number of actual divisions. The passage then addresses an objection: that the differentiation of surfaces might be only accidental, not substantial. The author responds that whether the surfaces are parts of the essence or accidents, their differentiation ultimately forces division in the essence itself, because accidents cannot infinitely subsist in accidents.

Correction: Even if the surfaces are accidents, their differentiation forces a difference in their substrate, leading ultimately to division in the essence because accidents cannot infinitely subsist in accidents.

Key Takeaway

Seventh Proof: Spheres and Orbital Speeds
Seventh proof using rotating sphere orbits to prove finite actual parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh proof uses the actual existence of distinct orbits in a rotating sphere to argue against infinite divisibility.

Core Idea
Each distinct orbit in a rotating sphere must actually exist, proving finite actual parts.

This lesson presents the seventh proof from the text, which uses the rotation of a sphere to demonstrate that a body cannot be infinitely divisible. The argument focuses on the equator, poles, and the orbits between them, each having unique properties that necessitate their actual existence. By the end, you'll see how this leads to the conclusion that actual infinite parts are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation, to grasp the logical flow. Note the key terms: sphere, equator, poles, orbits.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Understand the key claim
Notice that the proof starts by stating that when a sphere rotates, its equator and poles actually exist. Then, between them, many distinct orbits occur, each with its own width and speed.
This establishes actual existence as a starting point.
3
Grasp the argument's logic
The author argues that each orbit has a property unique to it, necessarily established and impossible in others. Therefore, these orbits must exist actually. If orbits could be infinite, then there would be actual infinite orbits, meaning the body has infinite actual parts, which is the conclusion.
The argument moves from unique properties to actual existence to infinite parts if infinite.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this argument is considered a proof against infinite divisibility. How does showing actual existence of distinct orbits lead to the impossibility of infinite divisibility?
The conclusion is that actual infinite parts are impossible, so a body must be composed of finite parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن الكرة إذا استدارت، فقد حصلت منطقتها، موجودة بالفعل. وحصل فيها نقطتان بالفعل. هما القطبان للكرة. ثم إنه يحصل بين المنطقة وبين القطبين، مدارات كثيرة. وكل ما كان منها أقرب إلى المنطقة، كان أوسع مداراً، وأسرع حركة من الذي يكون أبعد منها. ولا شك أن قول تلك الحركة المختصة بذلك القدر المعين من السرعة والبطء، مختص بذلك المقدار المعين، وممتنع الحصول في سائر المدارات. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : كل واحد من المدارات التي يمكن حصولها في تلك الكرة يختص بخاصية واجبة الثبوت فيه، ممتنعة الثبوت في غيره. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، وجب أن تكون تلك المدارات موجودة بالفعل. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لو كانت المدارات الممكنة فيها غير متناهية، لحصل فيها مدارات لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وذلك يفيد صحة ما ذكرنا، من أن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لتقسيمات لا نهاية لها، توجب أن يحصل فيه أجزاء، لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وذلك هو المطلوب [وبالله التوفيق (١)]
The seventh proof: When a sphere rotates, its equator actually exists. And two points actually exist in it, which are the two poles of the sphere. Then between the equator and the two poles, many orbits occur. And whichever of them is closer to the equator is wider in orbit and faster in motion than that which is farther from it. And there is no doubt that that motion specific to that particular amount of speed and slowness is specific to that particular amount, and impossible to obtain in the other orbits. If this is established, we say: Each of the orbits that can occur in that sphere is characterized by a property that is necessarily established in it, impossible to be established in another. And whenever the matter is so, it is necessary that those orbits actually exist. If this is established, we say: If the possible orbits in it were infinite, then orbits that have no end would actually occur in it. And that demonstrates the validity of what we mentioned, that if a body were capable of divisions that have no end, it would necessitate that parts that have no end actually occur in it. And that is the desired [and with Allah is success].
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against infinite divisibility, using the example of a rotating sphere. The author argues that when a sphere rotates, its equator and poles actually exist, and between them there are distinct orbits, each with a unique width and speed. Since each orbit has a specific property that is necessarily established in it and impossible in others, these orbits must actually exist. If it were possible for an infinite number of orbits to exist, then an infinite number of actual orbits would occur, which would mean the sphere has infinitely many actual parts. This supports the conclusion that bodies cannot be infinitely divisible, because that would entail actually infinite parts.

Argument from Orbits for Actual Infinity
Argument from unique properties of orbits to actual infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from orbits attempts to prove that if a body can be divided infinitely, it must actually have infinite parts.

Core Idea
Possibility of infinite orbits implies actual infinite orbits — a key move against infinite potentialities.

In this passage, the author builds on a premise about unique properties to argue for actual infinity. The example of orbits in a sphere is used to show that what is possible must be actual, and if possibilities are infinite, actualities are infinite too.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical connectors: 'If this is established...' indicating a conditional proof.
2
Identify the key premise
The author claims each possible orbit has a property necessarily established in it. Ask why that leads to actual existence.
This is the crux of the argument.
3
Trace the implication
From the premise, the author concludes that if possible orbits are infinite, actual infinite orbits must exist. See how this supports the overall claim about infinite divisions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author says this proves that if a body can be divided infinitely, it actually has infinite parts. Consider if you find this convincing.
This is a standard kalam position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : كل واحد من المدارات التي يمكن حصولها في تلك الكرة يختص بخاصية واجبة الثبوت فيه ، ممتنعة الثبوت في غيره . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن تكون تلك المدارات موجودة بالفعل . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو كانت المدارات الممكنة فيها غير متناهية ، لحصل فيها مدارات لا نهاية لها بالفعل . وذلك يفيد صحة ما ذكرنا ، من أن الجسم لو كان قابلاً لتقسيمات لا نهاية لها، توجب أن يحصل فيه أجزاء ، لا نهاية لها بالفعل . وذلك هو المطلوب وبالله التوفيق (١) (١) سقط ( ط ) .
If this is established, we say: Each of the orbits that can occur in that sphere is characterized by a property necessarily established in it, impossible of establishment in another. And whenever this is the case, it is necessary that those orbits exist in actuality. Commentary
This passage is a proof by way of orbits. The author argues that each possible orbit in a sphere has a unique property, so they must all actually exist. Then, if possible orbits were infinite, actual infinite orbits would result, supporting the claim that infinite divisions of a body lead to actual infinite parts. This is a standard kalam argument against infinite regress in potentialities.

First proof: the half-distance argument against infinite parts
Al-Razi presents the first proof using the half-distance argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the half-distance argument proves that any finite distance must consist of a finite number of parts.

Core Idea
If a distance had infinite parts, traversing it would take infinite time.

Imam al-Razi begins his demonstration against infinite parts in a finite body with a classical argument: if a distance were composed of infinitely many parts, then moving from one end to the other would require crossing an infinite number of halves, which would take infinite time. Since we observe finite traversal times, the parts must be finite. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'
2
Identify the key premises
The argument rests on two premises: (1) to reach the end, one must first reach the halfway point; (2) to reach the halfway point, one must reach its halfway point, and so on. If the number of these halves were infinite, one would need infinite time to complete the journey.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since we do actually traverse finite distances in finite time, the number of parts cannot be infinite. Therefore the distance must be composed of finitely many parts.
This conclusion applies to the body occupying the distance as well.
4
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Some might think the argument assumes motion is discrete (jumping from point to point), but it works for continuous motion too, because the conceptual necessity of halves remains regardless of how the motion is performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في القامة الدلالة على أن الجسم المتناهي في المقار يمتنع أن يكون مؤلفاً من أجزاء لانهاية لها بالفعل

اعلم : أن الذي يدل على أن الأمر كما ذكرناه ، وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إنه من المحال أن ينتهي المتحرك من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، إلا إذا وصل إلى نصفها. ومن المحال أن يصل إلى نصفها ، إلا إذا وصل إلى نصف نصفها . فلو كانت المسافة مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية ، لوجب أن يمتنع الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها، إلا في زمان غير متناه . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا : أن المسافة مركبة من أجزاء متناهية .
Chapter Seven: On the complete proof that a finite body in magnitude cannot be composed of infinite parts in actuality.

Know: That which indicates that the matter is as we have mentioned, are several aspects:
The first proof: It is impossible for the mover to reach from the beginning of the distance to its end unless it reaches its half. And it is impossible for it to reach its half unless it reaches the half of its half. So if the distance were composed of infinite parts, it would be necessary that reaching from its beginning to its end be impossible except in an infinite time. And since this is not the case, we know that the distance is composed of finite parts.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is the first proof in a series. The author uses the well-known half-distance argument: to traverse any distance, one must first traverse half of it, then half of the remaining half, and so on. If the number of these halves were infinite, one would need an infinite amount of time to complete the traversal. Since we do complete finite traversals in finite time, the distance must consist of a finite number of parts. This is a classic kalam argument against the infinite divisibility of space.

Key Terms
الحجة الأولى
مسافة
أجزاء غير متناهية
زمان غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if motion is assumed to be discrete (jumping from point to point).
Correction: The argument does not require motion to be discrete. It relies on the logical necessity of traversing halves, which holds even for continuous motion: to cover any interval, you must first cover its first half, and so on.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that distance is composed of finite parts?
A) Because we cannot imagine infinite halves.
B) Because traversing a distance would require infinite time if parts were infinite.
C) Because the mover cannot reach the halfway point.
D) Because distance is not real.

Key Takeaway
The half-distance argument shows that because we traverse distances in finite time, the distance cannot be composed of infinitely many parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a finite distance cannot contain infinite parts, what does that say about your own finite life and the countless distractions you allow into it? Are you treating your finite time as if it were infinite? How will you change your priorities today?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that traversing infinite halves would require infinite time. In your spiritual journey, have you been avoiding the 'first half' of repentance or change, thinking you have unlimited time? What is the half you have been putting off reaching?
?
This proof relies on the necessity of reaching the half before the end. In your relationship with Allah, what 'half' (like sincere intention or repentance) are you neglecting, hoping to jump straight to the end without passing through it?

Al-Nazzam's Leap Theory: Objection and Response
Abu al-Hudhayl's half-distance proof and al-Nazzam's tuffar response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's tuffar response to Abu al-Hudhayl's half-distance argument and the rhetorical strategy used to defend it.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam avoids infinite regress by proposing motion can 'leap' over intermediate points.

In this exchange, Abu al-Hudhayl uses the half-distance argument to challenge atomism. Al-Nazzam replies that the mover does not need to traverse every part of the distance—it can leap. He then turns the tables, arguing that the atomist's own view of the indivisible atom leads to absurdities, so they cannot reject his leap theory as too implausible. This passage captures a key moment in the debate over atomism and infinite divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the exchange. Note the structure: objection, response, and counter-argument.
The passage contains a dialectical exchange between two early Mu'tazilites.
2
Identify the objection and response
Understand Abu al-Hudhayl's argument that motion requires traversing all parts leads to infinity, and al-Nazzam's denial of this traversal.
Al-Nazzam's key move is the concept of 'tuffar' (leap).
3
Understand the definition of 'tuffar'
Al-Nazzam defines 'tuffar' as movement directly to a distant part without passing through intermediate parts. Consider why this is considered 'remote'.
The definition is given explicitly in the passage.
4
Analyze the counter-argument
Notice how al-Nazzam uses the atomist's own theory (the millstone crumbling) to defend his leap theory. This is a tu quoque strategy.
This shows the debate is about consistency as much as empirical truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن : أبا الهذيل العلاف، لما احتج بهذا الدليل على النظام ، أجاب النظام عنه : بأن المحال إنما يلزم لو كان المتحرك : تحرك على جميع أجزاء المسافة . وليس الأمر كذلك ، بل إنه تحرك على بعضها ، وطفر على الباقي والمراد من الطفر : انتقال المتحرك إلى الجزء البعيد عنه ، من غير أن يمر بما [؟]. قال : ( والقول بالطفر ، وإن كان بعيداً جداً، إلا أن القول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد ، يلزم عليه تفكك حجر الرحى . فإذا جاز لكم التزام ذلك المستبعد ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً التزام مثل هذا المستبعد ؟ ) .
And know: That Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, when he argued with this proof against al-Nazzam, al-Nazzam answered him: That the impossibility would only follow if the mover traversed all the parts of the distance. But this is not the case, rather it moves over some of them and 'tuffar' (leaps) over the rest. And the meaning of 'tuffar' is: the movement of the mover to the part distant from it without passing through what [؟]. He said: 'The theory of tuffar, although it is very remote, yet the theory of affirming the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) necessitates the crumbling of the millstone. So if you permit accepting that unlikely thing, why do you not also permit accepting such an unlikely thing?'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. Abu al-Hudhayl uses the half-distance proof against al-Nazzam. Al-Nazzam responds by denying that the mover must pass through every intermediate part; instead, he claims the mover can 'leap' (tuffar) over some parts. He then argues that the concept of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) is also problematic, and since atomists accept it, they should accept the leap theory as well. This is a key exchange in the debate over atomism and infinite divisibility.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل العلاف
النظام
طفر
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Nazzam is denying the validity of the half-distance argument entirely.
Correction: He is actually offering an alternative that avoids the conclusion, not denying the argument's logical consistency.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nazzam respond to the half-distance argument?
A) He denies the existence of distance.
B) He claims the mover can leap over parts.
C) He says the mover is non-physical.
D) He accepts the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's leap theory is a defense against the half-distance argument, using the claim that motion can skip parts and a tu quoque against atomist assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nazzam was willing to accept a 'very remote' theory to maintain consistency in his worldview. When confronted with a difficulty in your own beliefs, do you tend to adjust the belief or embrace a creative, even uncomfortable, alternative? What might this reveal about your intellectual honesty?
?
The debate here is not just about atoms and motion — it's about what you are willing to accept as plausible. Are there areas in your life where you cling to a view despite obvious absurdities, simply because it is familiar? Will you, like al-Nazzam's challenge to the atomist, examine your own assumptions with the same scrutiny?
?
Al-Nazzam used a tu quoque argument: 'If you permit that, why not permit this?' Have you ever dismissed someone's idea simply because it seemed strange, without realizing your own position has its own difficulties? How can you cultivate fairness in evaluating opposing views, even when they are as 'remote' as the leap theory?

The First Proof Against Infinite Composition
Razi presents the first proof against infinite composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that a finite body cannot have infinite parts, and why the argument leads to atomism.

Core Idea
Traversing infinite parts would require infinite time; finite motion proves finite parts.

Razi presents a crisp logical proof that a finite body cannot be composed of actually infinite parts. The argument uses a simple observation about motion across a distance. By following the chain of necessary conditions, Razi shows that infinite composition leads to a contradiction with our experience. This proof is foundational for the Islamic atomist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that the proof has a clear conditional structure: if infinite parts, then infinite time; but finite time, so finite parts.
2
Analyze the logical chain
Identify the key premises: to reach the end, one must reach the half; to reach the half, one must reach its half, ad infinitum. This creates an infinite regress of necessary conditions.
This is a classic 'dichotomy' paradox, similar to Zeno's.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Observe that Razi concludes the distance must have finite parts since we observe finite traversal times. Connect this to the broader kalām argument for atomism (al-jawhar al-fard).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إنه من المحال أن ينتهي المتحرك من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، إلا إذا وصل إلى نصفها. ومن المحال أن يصل إلى نصفها ، إلا إذا وصل إلى نصف نصفها . فلو كانت المسافة مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية ، لوجب أن يمتنع الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها، إلا في زمان غير متناه . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا : أن المسافة مركبة من أجزاء متناهية .
The first proof: It is impossible for a moving body to reach from the beginning of a distance to its end unless it reaches its half. And it is impossible for it to reach its half unless it reaches the half of its half. So if the distance were composed of infinite parts, it would be necessary that reaching from its beginning to its end would be impossible except in infinite time. And since the matter is not so, we know that the distance is composed of finite parts.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) that a finite body cannot be composed of parts that are actually infinite. The argument is based on the impossibility of traversing an infinite distance in finite time. The author, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, presents the reasoning: to traverse a distance, one must first reach its midpoint, and before that, the midpoint of that midpoint, ad infinitum. If the number of parts were infinite, then traversing them would require passing through infinitely many points, which would take infinite time. Since we observe that bodies do traverse finite distances in finite time, it follows that the distance must be composed of a finite number of parts. This proof is a classic argument in kalām against infinite regress in spatial extension, often used to defend atomism (al-jawhar al-fard). The conclusion is that the distance is composed of finite parts, and by implication, the body itself is ultimately composed of finite indivisible parts.

Key Terms
حجة
متحرك
مسافة
أجزاء غير متناهية
زمان غير متناه
أجزاء متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof assumes that the distance is actually divided into infinitely many parts before motion, but Razi is not assuming actual division; he is considering potential division.
Correction: The proof only requires that the distance is potentially divisible infinitely, not that it is actually divided. The argument shows that if there were actually infinite parts, traversal would be impossible, but since traversal occurs, there cannot be actual infinite parts.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first proof?
A) The moving body cannot reach its destination.
B) The distance must be composed of finite parts.
C) Infinite time is needed for any motion.
D) The parts of a body are actually infinite.

Key Takeaway
Finite motion in finite time implies that the distance traversed has only finitely many parts, supporting the atomist position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that every finite achievement is possible only because the path is composed of finite steps. In what area of your life have you been paralyzed by the thought of infinite obligations? How does this proof free you to take the very next finite step today?
?
Razi builds his argument on an undeniable observation: motion happens. What observable realities about your own limits (time, energy, mortality) are you denying? If you truly accepted them, what would you start or stop doing right now?
?
The proof forces you to admit that you cannot leap to the end without passing through the halves. Where are you trying to skip essential spiritual halves—repentance, patience, or small acts of obedience—in pursuit of a distant goal? What half will you commit to reaching this week?

Second Objection: Infinite Parts in Time and Motion
An objector asks why not allow infinite parts in both body and time simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection raised against the first proof, which challenges the assumption that finite bodies cannot have infinite parts by extending the same reasoning to time and motion.

Core Idea
If time has infinite instants, motion through infinite spatial parts becomes possible.

In this passage, an objector presents a clever parallel to the first proof. The first proof argued that a finite body cannot be composed of infinite spatial parts because it cannot be traversed in finite time. The objector turns the argument around: what if both space and time are infinitely divisible? This objection challenges the claim of necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) and forces the defender to explain why the same logic does not apply to time and motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: body and time are both finite but possibly composed of infinite parts.
2
Identify the parallel reasoning
The objector says: if body can have infinite parts and time can have infinite moments, then motion can occur. Find the key phrases that draw this parallel.
3
Understand the challenge to necessary knowledge
The objector challenges the claim that it is self-evident that between two ends there cannot be infinite actual parts. Ask yourself: why does the objector insist that this claim must apply equally to body, time, and motion?
The answer to this objection is not present in this lesson; it appears later.
4
Summarize the objection
Try to restate the objection in your own words. What is its main point?
This helps ensure you have grasped the logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الجسم المتناهي في المقدار ، مركب من أجزاء لا نهاية لها . والزمان المتناهي في المقدار ، مركب أيضاً من آنات متتالية لا نهاية لها . فلا جرم تحرك المتحرك على الأجزاء التي لا نهاية لها في تلك الآنات ، التي لا نهاية لها ؟ فإن ادعيتم : العلم الضروري بأن الشيء الذي له ، طرفان ، يمتنع أن يحصل فيها بين ذينك الطرفين أجزاء ، لا نهاية لها بالفعل . فنقول : إن صح هذا الكلام فاذكروه في الجسم ، ولا تتعرضوا للزمان ولا للحركة البتة، وإن لم يمتنع ذلك الاحتمال في الجسم ، فكذا لا يمتنع مثله في الزمان وفي الحركة
The second question: Why is it not permitted to say: This body finite in magnitude is composed of infinite parts, and time finite in magnitude is also composed of successive moments (ānāt) infinite in number. Therefore, the moving body traverses the infinite parts in those infinite moments. If you claim necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) that a thing which has two ends, it is impossible for there to be between those two ends actually infinite parts, then we say: If this statement is correct, then mention it regarding the body, and do not speak at all about time or motion. And if that possibility is not impossible in the body, then likewise it is not impossible in time and motion.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection (suʾāl thānī) against the first proof. The objector raises a parallel: the first proof assumed that an infinite number of spatial parts cannot be traversed in finite time. But what if both time and space are composed of an infinite number of parts? Specifically, if the body has infinitely many spatial parts, and time also has infinitely many instants (aanaat), then the mover could traverse the infinite spatial parts in the infinite instants. The objector challenges the claim that it is self-evident (ḍarūrī) that between two ends there cannot be an infinite number of actual parts. They argue: if you insist that this is self-evident, then apply it only to body (space), not to time or motion. If you accept it for body because of some special reason, then you must also apply the same reasoning to time and motion—but you cannot, because time and motion are also finite yet seemingly have infinite potential divisibility. Thus, the objection seeks to undermine the proof by showing that it either leads to an infinite regress in time as well, or the claim of self-evidence is not uniformly applicable. Note: The answer to this objection is not present in the current page window; it presumably follows later.

جسم متناهي
آنات متتالية
علم ضروري
طرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection rejects the first proof entirely by proving that motion is possible over infinite parts in finite time.
Correction: The objection does not prove the possibility; it merely points out an inconsistency in the application of the claim of necessary knowledge. It challenges the defender to explain why the same logic does not apply to time and motion.

Checkpoint
What parallel does the second objection draw to challenge the first proof?
A) Body and time are both finite but have infinite parts.
B) Motion can occur in jumps regardless of infinite parts.
C) The first proof relies on empirical evidence.
D) The concept of necessary knowledge is flawed.

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges the first proof by drawing a parallel between spatial and temporal infinite divisibility, arguing that the claim of necessary knowledge must apply consistently to body, time, and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief as self-evident without ever examining its logical consistency across different areas of your life? Identify one such belief and test it today against the teachings of this lesson. What would it mean if your certainty were misplaced?

This objection challenges applying a rule to one domain but not another. In your relationship with Allah, are you consistent in applying His commands to all aspects of your life? Is there an area where you accept an exception for yourself that you would not accept from others? Write down one inconsistency and make a concrete plan to remove it.

The Intuitive Impossibility of Leaping
Razi gives the first answer: leaping is intuitively known to be false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument against leaping (ṭafra) and the role of intuitive knowledge (badīha) in rejecting it.

Core Idea
Leaping is intuitively impossible: a body cannot skip intervening space.

Razi begins his response to al-Nazzam's concept of leaping by appealing to the most basic form of knowledge: intuition. He asserts that the very idea of a body moving from one place to a distant one without passing through the intermediate space is self-evidently false. This lesson explores that argument and what it means to claim something is known by badīha.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how concise and direct Razi's statement is. He presents his first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) in a single sentence.
2
Understand 'intuitive impossibility'
Razi claims that the impossibility of leaping is known by badīha (intuition/self-evidence). Reflect on what that means: it is a truth that anyone can recognize immediately, without needing proof. Can you think of other self-evident truths?
Intuitive knowledge is considered certain and foundational in Islamic theology.
3
Analyze the key elements of leaping
Identify the three components in Razi's description: the moving body (mutaharrik), transferring to a distant place, without passing through the intermediate space, while remaining the same body throughout. Ask yourself why each element is necessary for the argument.
The phrase 'while it remains the same in all states' rules out the idea of disappearance and reappearance.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Razi's first response is an appeal to common sense. Consider why al-Nazzam might have proposed leaping and why this intuitive argument is a strong counter. This sets up further responses (to be covered in later lessons).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن كون المتحرك منتقلاً من مكان إلى مكان بعيد عنه ، من غير أن يمر بما بينهما ، مع كونه باقياً في الأحوال كلها : معلوم الفساد بالبديهة
The first: That the moving body is transferring from one place to a distant place, without passing through what is between them, while it remains the same in all states, is known to be false by intuition (badīha).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is the first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) from Razi against al-Nazzam's objection of leaping. It asserts that the idea of a body moving from one point to a non-adjacent point without traversing the intervening space is self-evidently false. This is an appeal to necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) or intuition (badīha): every person intuitively knows that to go from point A to point C, you must pass through point B if it lies between them. The phrase 'while it remains the same in all states' (مع كونه باقياً في الأحوال كلها) emphasizes that this is a single continuous motion, not a disappearance and reappearance. Thus, the concept of ṭafra is rejected as contrary to immediate rational evidence.

Key Terms
متحرك
طفر
بديهة
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that intuitive impossibility relies on empirical observation or scientific experiment.
Correction: Razi's argument is not empirical but based on immediate rational intuition. It is a self-evident truth, not a conclusion from empirical data.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider leaping impossible in the first answer?
A) Because it violates the laws of physics.
B) Because it is intuitively self-evident that a body must pass through intermediate space.
C) Because al-Nazzam's argument is weak.
D) Because leaping requires infinite speed.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first refutation of leaping is an appeal to intuitive knowledge: the impossibility of skipping intermediate space is self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, where are you most tempted to 'leap' over the necessary steps—skipping prayer, patience, or learning—hoping to arrive at a destination without passing through the intermediate space? What is one step you have been avoiding that you can take today?
?
Razi says the impossibility of leaping is 'known by intuition'—you already know it inside. But is there an area of your life where you are acting as if you can bypass the process and still reach the goal? What would it cost you to continue pretending?
?
Think of one relationship, one habit, or one skill you’ve tried to improve. Have you ever looked for a shortcut that didn’t exist? Write down the steps you skipped and commit to one concrete action today to begin walking the path you’ve been trying to jump over.

Motion Requires Traversing All Intermediate Points
First aspect: motion without intermediate points is self-evidently absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of passing through intermediate points in motion is considered rationally impossible (self-evidently false) in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Leaping across space without traversing the middle is self-evidently absurd.

This passage presents the first argument against the concept of 'ṭafrah'—the idea that a moving object can skip parts of space. The author claims this is known to be false by intuition (badīha), meaning it requires no proof. We will examine this claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice the compact style: the author states a proposition and immediately declares it self-evidently false.
The passage is a single, complete argument.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify three key terms: mutaḥarrik (moving thing), ṭafrah (leaping), badīha (intuition). Understand that badīha refers to knowledge that is immediate and requires no reasoning.
In kalam, 'self-evident' is a strong claim—it means the contrary is inconceivable.
3
Reflect on why this is considered self-evident
Ask yourself: Can you imagine a ball moving from point A to point C without ever being at any point between A and C? The author says this is absurd by intuition. Try to think of a counterexample.
This reveals the deep connection between motion and continuity in classical physics.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This passage is the first of several responses to a question about motion. It directly refutes the theory of ṭafrah (leaping), which claimed motion could be discrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن كون المتحرك منتقلاً من مكان إلى مكان بعيد عنه، من غير أن يمر بما بينهما، مع كونه باقياً في الأحوال كلها : معلوم الفساد بالبديهة
The first: that the mover moving from a place to a place far from it, without passing through what is between them, while it remains in all states, is known to be false by intuition.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect of the answer to the first question. It states that it is self-evident (badīha) that a moving object cannot travel from one place to another without traversing the intermediate distance, as long as it remains continuous in its states. This directly refutes the concept of ṭafrah (leaping) which would allow skipping parts of space.

Key Terms
متحرك
طفرة
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A moving object can jump from one position to another without passing through the space in between.
Correction: The author asserts this is self-evidently false; reason itself rejects it because the object would have to disappear and reappear, which contradicts its continuous existence.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is self-evidently absurd
B) Because it is not observed
C) Because the mover would disappear
D) Because it requires infinite time

Key Takeaway
The first aspect confirms that motion across a distance requires passing through all intermediate points; this is a self-evident rational truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Your life is a continuous journey from birth to death, with every moment connected. Are you treating your spiritual journey as a series of disconnected leaps, or are you mindful of each intermediate state and what Allah requires of you in it? What will you change to ensure you do not skip the necessary stages of faith?

The Ink Test: Empirical Refutation of Leaping
Second response: leaping possible in principle but refuted by empirical test.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an empirical test with ink demonstrates that motion must touch every part of the distance, refuting the possibility of actual leaping.

Core Idea
Leaping may be possible in theory, but the ink test proves it never happens.

This passage presents a concrete test to show that leaping does not occur in the real world, even if it were theoretically possible. The argument uses an everyday observation—a finger dipped in ink moving across a surface—to demonstrate that motion must contact every part of the distance. An objection is anticipated and refuted, strengthening the case against actual leaping.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the empirical reasoning and the back-and-forth of objection and response.
2
Understand the ink test
Identify the key observation: moving an inked finger across a distance leaves a continuous black line. What does this imply about motion?
This implies that every part of the distance was touched.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
The opponent suggests that the ink only sticks to touched parts, and the skipped parts mix in, giving the illusion of a full line. Follow the refutation that uses the finite vs infinite ratio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون الطفر ممكناً في الجملة، لكنه غير واقع. لأنا إذا لطخنا الإصبع بالمداد، ثم أمررناه على المسافة من أولها إلى آخرها. فإنه يحصل هناك خط مستقيم أسود. وذلك يقتضي أن المتحرك قد مس جميع أجزاء المسافة. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه حصل السواد في بعض أجزاء المسافة، بسبب حصول المماسة، ولم يحصل في الباقي بسبب الطفرة. ثم إن تلك الأجزاء اختلط بعضها بالبعض، ولا يميز الحس بينها. فلا جرم يرى الكل على لون السواد ؟ . فنقول : إن بتقدير أن يصح القول بالطفرة، فإن الأجزاء التي صارت ممسوسة متناهية، والأجزاء التي وقعت الطفرة عليها غير متناهية . فالنوع الأول من الأجزاء قليلة جداً بالنسبة إلى النوع الثاني منها . فإذا كان المتلطخ بالسواد هو النوع الأول فقط، وجب أن تبقى تلك الأجزاء القليلة في تلك الأجزاء الكثيرة . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا : أن كل الأجزاء صارت ممسوسة عند الحركة . وحينئذ يعود الإلزام
The second: that assuming that leaping is possible in general, yet it does not actually occur. Because if we smear the finger with ink, then pass it over the distance from its beginning to its end, there results a straight black line. And that necessitates that the mover has touched all parts of the distance. If they say: Why may it not be said that blackness occurred in some parts of the distance due to contact, and did not occur in the rest due to leaping; then those parts are mixed with each other and the sense cannot distinguish between them, so the whole is seen as the color of blackness? We say: Assuming that leaping is valid, the parts that are touched are finite, and the parts over which leaping occurred are infinite. So the first type of parts is very few compared to the second type. If the only part smeared with blackness is the first type, then those few parts would be lost among those many parts. Since this is not the case, we know that all parts become touched during motion. Then the objection returns.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect of the answer to the first question. It argues that even if leaping (ṭafrah) were possible in principle, it does not actually occur. The proof is empirical: dip a finger in ink and move it across a distance, resulting in a continuous black line, which shows that every part of the distance was touched. An objection is raised: perhaps the ink only deposited on some parts (those touched) and the skipped parts (due to leaping) are not inked, but the touched and skipped parts mix, so the whole appears black. The answer refutes this: touched parts are finite, skipped parts are infinite, so the few black parts would disappear among the many, and we would not see a uniform black line. Since we do see a uniform line, all parts must have been touched, refuting the possibility of actual leaping.

إصبع
مداد
مماسة
غير متناهي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Leaping actually occurs in motion.
Correction: The ink test shows that motion touches every part, so leaping does not happen.

Checkpoint

A) That leaping always occurs
B) That motion touches every part of the distance
C) That ink only sticks to certain parts
D) That the distance is infinite

Key Takeaway
The ink test provides simple empirical evidence that motion touches every part of the distance, refuting the actual occurrence of leaping.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The finite black specks from touched parts are lost among the infinite untouched parts—meaning that if something is rare enough, it can be overwhelmed by the abundant. What rare moments of truth are you allowing to be drowned by the infinite noise of your daily distractions?
?
The argument uses a simple sensory experiment to overturn an abstract philosophical possibility. When was the last time you allowed a simple, undeniable observation to challenge a deeply held belief? Are you willing to do so today?

Third Response: Even with Leaping, a Finite Body Exists
Third response: even with leaping, the part actually moved over is finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second response to the argument from leaping, which shows that even if leaping occurs, the part actually traversed must be a finite body.

Core Idea
Leaping doesn't avoid finitude: the part actually moved is finite.

In this third response (called 'second aspect' in the text), the author addresses a different angle of the objection about leaping (ṭafrah). Rather than examining the nature of leaping itself, the argument simply points out that whatever is actually moved over must be finite. This direct observation cuts through the objection and reaffirms the existence of a finite body.

3
Reflect on the significance

This is a direct rebuttal: even granting leaping, finitude is inescapable.

This conclusion supports the point that a finite body exists, which is what the opponent (who posits infinite divisibility) denies. The argument is straightforward: even if leaping is granted, the part actually traversed is a finite body.

The Impossibility of Infinite Instants in Finite Time
Answer to second question: infinite actual parts in finite time are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why finite time cannot contain an infinite number of actual instants, and how this proof supports the finitude of parts in temporal magnitudes.

Core Idea
Infinite actual parts cannot coexist within a finite time interval.

This passage presents a logical refutation of the idea that a finite time could contain an infinite number of actual instants. Using a dependency argument, it shows that if such infinity existed, reaching the last instant would require traversing an infinite sequence—a manifest impossibility. Since we observe that motion does reach a final instant, the infinite premise is false, proving that the instants are finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'. The argument is a proof by contradiction.
The author uses very precise logical language.
2
Identify the conditional assumption
Find where the author states what is being assumed: that a finite time contains infinite actual instants.
3
Trace the consequence
See what follows from that assumption: reaching the last instant would depend on passing an infinite number of states.
This dependency is crucial.
4
Recognize the impossibility
The author asserts that passing an infinite series in this way is necessarily impossible. The impossible cannot be a condition for something else.
This is a self-evident impossibility.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since the last instant is reached in reality, the initial assumption must be false. Therefore, the parts are finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني فالجواب عنه : أن نقول : إنه لو حصل في الزمان المتناهي آنات غير متناهية بالفعل، لكان دخول الجزء الأخير منها في الوجود ، موقوفاً على أن يبتدىء الشيء، ويمضي بعده أحوال لا نهاية لها . ومن المعلوم بالضرورة : أن انقضاء غير المتناهي على هذا الوجه : محال . والموقوف على المحال : محال . فوجب أن لا يحصل الوصول إلى الجزء الأخير من المسافة وحيث حصل، علمنا : أن تلك الأجزاء متناهية . وهو المطلوب
As for the second question, the answer concerning it: that we say: if in a finite time there were actual infinite instants, then the entry of the last of them into existence would depend on the thing beginning and passing after it an infinite succession of states. And it is known necessarily that the passing of the infinite in this manner is impossible. And that which depends on the impossible is impossible. Therefore, reaching the last part of the distance would be impossible. But since it is reached, we know that those parts are finite. That is what was required.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage answers the second question, which likely concerns whether a finite time can contain an infinite number of instants. The argument states: if a finite time actually contained infinite instants, then arriving at the last instant would require passing through an infinite number of preceding instants. Passing through an infinite series is necessarily impossible. Since reaching the last instant is something that does occur (e.g., the end of the motion), the premise that there are infinite instants must be false. Therefore, the instants are finite, supporting the finitude of parts in time and, by extension, in space.

Key Terms

آنات
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A finite interval can contain an infinite number of simultaneous or nested actual instants without contradiction.
Correction: The argument shows that if these instants are actual and sequential (as parts of time), reaching the last would require traversing an infinite series, which is impossible. Therefore, finite time cannot host infinite actual discrete parts.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Finite time cannot contain an infinite number of actual instants because the last instant would require traversing an infinite series, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Proof of the Mutakallimūn: Separation of Parts Proves Indivisible Atoms
Second proof: assuming separation of parts leads to indivisible atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn argued that if a body's parts could fully separate, each part must be indivisible, thus proving atomism.

Core Idea
Full separation of parts forces each part to be indivisible.

The mutakallimūn's second proof for atomism uses a thought experiment: if a body's parts can separate completely, each separated part cannot be further divided without contradiction. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: premise, possibility, supposition, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument relies on the principle that anything possible can be assumed without contradiction. Locate this premise in the text.
The phrase 'وكل ما كان ممكناً، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال' is crucial.
3
Trace the conclusion
Understand why after full separation, each part must be indivisible. The commentary will help clarify.
Ask yourself: why can't a separated part be further divided?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للمتكلمين : أن قالوا : لو كان الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء لا نهاية لها ، لكانت تلك الأجزاء مجتمعة . ولا شك أن تلك الأجزاء، كما أنها قابلة للاجتماع، فكذلك قابلة للافتراق . وكل ما كان ممكناً، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . فلنفرض زوال تلك الاجتماعات بأسرها، وحصول تلك الافترقات بأسرها. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يبقى كل واحد منها جزءاً لا يتجزأ . وهو المطلوب
The second proof of the mutakallimūn: that they said: if a body were composed of parts without end, those parts would be collected. And there is no doubt that those parts, just as they are capable of being gathered, so they are capable of separation. And everything that is possible, its hypothetical occurrence does not necessitate an impossibility. So let us suppose the removal of all those gatherings and the occurrence of all those separations. On this supposition, each of them remains an indivisible part. That is what was required.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for atomism (the existence of indivisible parts). The argument: if a body had infinitely many parts, those parts are capable of being gathered together and also of separating. Since separation is possible, we can imagine it happening completely: all gathered parts separate from each other. After full separation, each remaining part must be indivisible (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ), because if it were further divisible, it would not be a fully separated unit. Thus, the original body is composed of indivisible atoms. This proof relies on the principle that what is possible can be assumed without contradiction.

Key Terms

أجزاء
اجتماع
افتراق
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires actual physical separation to occur.
Correction: The proof only assumes the possibility of separation, not actual separation. It uses a thought experiment based on logical possibility.

Checkpoint

B) That they can be separated into indivisible atoms
C) That separation is impossible
D) That they are infinite in number

Key Takeaway
The possibility of complete separation of a body's parts implies that each part must be indivisible, establishing atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mountain and Mustard Seed Proof
Third proof: comparing mountain and mustard seed refutes infinite parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the comparison between a mountain and a mustard seed proves that the number of parts in a physical body must be finite, supporting the doctrine of atomism.

Core Idea
If infinite parts were possible, a mountain could equal a mustard seed in size—an absurdity.

This passage presents a clever argument known as the mountain and mustard seed proof. It aims to demonstrate that physical bodies cannot have an infinite number of parts, because that would lead to logical contradictions. This argument is a key piece of evidence for the Islamic theological position that matter is composed of finite, indivisible atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the clear structure of a dilemma.
2
Understand the dilemma
The argument sets up two possibilities about the number of parts in a mountain vs. a mustard seed. Identify what each horn of the dilemma claims.
3
Examine the first horn
The first possibility is that they have the same number of parts. Why is that impossible? What contradiction follows?
The author shows that if more parts cause larger size, equal parts would mean equal size, which is absurd.
4
Examine the second horn
The second possibility is that they have different numbers of parts. Although the text is cut off, the author argues from common experience that magnitude comes from aggregation of parts.
The second horn is also refuted, but the text ends before the full conclusion.
5
Connect to atomism
How does this proof lead to the conclusion that parts must be finite? This supports the doctrine that matter is made of finite atoms.
The impossibility of infinite parts implies a finite number of parts, which aligns with atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قالوا : أجزاء الجبل . إما أن تكون مساوية لأجزاء الخردلة في العدد، أو لا تكون كذلك. والأول باطل . لأن ازدياد تلك الأجزاء، إن كان موجباً لازدياد المقدار، وجب أن يكون عند الاستواء في العدد، يحصل الاستواء في المقدار . فيلزم كون الجبل مساوياً للخردلة في المقدار . وهو محال . وإن لم يكن ازدياد تلك الأجزاء موجباً ازدياد المقدار فحينئذ لا يحصل من تألفها : المقدار والعظم. فهذه المقادير والأعظام، تكون مولدة من تألفها . وقد فرضنا : أن الأمر كذلك
The third proof: they said: the parts of the mountain are either equal to the parts of the mustard seed in number, or not. The first is false. Because if the increase of those parts entails an increase in magnitude, then when the number is equal, magnitude must be equal. So it would follow that the mountain is equal to the mustard seed in magnitude, which is absurd. And if the increase of those parts does not entail an increase in magnitude, then magnitude and size do not result from their composition. But these magnitudes and sizes are generated from their composition. And we have assumed that the matter is so.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for finite atomic parts, though it is incomplete in the source. It sets up a dilemma: either the number of parts in a mountain equals that in a mustard seed, or not. If equal, since more parts increase magnitude, they would be equal in size, which is absurd. If not equal, then the increase in parts does not cause an increase in magnitude, so magnitude does not come from aggregation of parts. But we observe that magnitude does come from aggregation (e.g., piling up grains increases size). Therefore, the number of parts cannot be infinite; it must be finite. The text cuts off before discussing the second horn in full detail, but the argument is logically concluded: the first horn is refuted, and the second horn is also refuted by common experience, leaving the conclusion that the parts are finite.

Key Terms

خردلة
عدد
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for a physical object to have an infinite number of parts without contradiction.
Correction: This proof shows that infinite parts would lead to absurd consequences, such as a mountain being equal in size to a mustard seed or magnitude not arising from composition, both of which are impossible.

Checkpoint

A) They are infinite.
B) They must be equal to those of a mustard seed.
C) They must be finite.
D) They are not related to magnitude.

Key Takeaway
The mountain and mustard seed argument demonstrates that infinite parts are impossible, thus supporting the finite atomism of the mutakallimun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the universe cannot bear infinite parts, then your time is finite. The parts of your life—minutes, days—are limited. When you realize each part adds to your final magnitude before Allah, how does that change what you do with the next ten minutes?

The Mustard Seed and Mountain Argument: Proving Finitude of Parts
This passage proves that a body composed of a finite number of parts must be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple mathematical argument demonstrates that any body composed of a finite number of parts must itself be finite in size.

Core Idea
A finite double of a finite number remains finite.

This lesson examines a classical kalam argument that uses the relationship between a small body (mustard seed) and a large body (mountain) to prove that all physical bodies have a finite number of parts. The argument is based on a self-evident mathematical principle. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: principle, application, conclusion.
2
Identify the general principle
The argument begins with a general statement about numbers: any number less than another is finite.
This is the foundational premise.
3
Apply to the mustard seed
The mustard seed's parts are fewer than the mountain's, so they are finite.
4
Understand the doubling logic
The mountain's parts are a double of the mustard seed's parts by finite degrees. Doubling a finite number a finite number of times yields a finite result.
This is the key inference.
5
Reach the conclusion
Therefore, the mountain's parts are finite. This proves the desired point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدد (۱) أجزاء الخردلة . فنقول : كل عدد يكون أقل من غيره ، كان متناهياً . فعدد أجزاء الخردلة متناه ، وعدد أجزاء الجبل ضعف عدد أجزاء الخردلة بمراتب متناهية . وضعف المتناهي مرات متناهية يكون متناهياً ، فعدد أجزاء الجبل : متناه . وهو المطلوب .
Number (1) [The parts of the mustard seed]. We say: Every number that is less than another is finite. So the number of parts of the mustard seed is finite, and the number of parts of the mountain is double the number of parts of the mustard seed by finite degrees. And the double of a finite thing [multiplied] a finite number of times is finite, so the number of parts of the mountain is finite. That is what was required.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (ḥujja) that the number of parts in a finite body, such as a mountain, must be finite. It begins by establishing a general principle: any number that is less than another number is itself finite. The proof then applies this to the parts of a mustard seed (khardala), which are assumed to be finite. The mountain's parts are double (or a multiple) of the mustard seed's parts by a finite number of degrees (i.e., a finite multiplication factor). Since multiplying a finite number by a finite number yields a finite result, the mountain's parts are finite. The conclusion (al-maṭlūb) is that the number of parts in a body like a mountain is finite.

Key Terms
أجزاء
متناه
خردلة
ضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the finitude of the mustard seed's parts without proof.
Correction: The finitude of the mustard seed's parts is derived from the principle that a number smaller than a finite number (the mountain's parts) is itself finite; it is not an arbitrary assumption.

Checkpoint
In the mustard seed argument, what is the premise regarding the number of parts of the mustard seed?
A) It is infinite
B) It is finite
C) It is unknown
D) It is equal to the mountain

Key Takeaway
The mustard seed argument demonstrates that a finite number multiplied by a finite factor remains finite, proving that any finite body has a finite number of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the number of parts in a mountain is finite, how much more finite are the moments of your life? Are you living as if each moment is a countable unit that you will be held accountable for? What would change if you truly internalized that your time is limited and numbered?
?
The argument uses a mustard seed as the smallest reference. What is the 'mustard seed' in your life—the smallest action, thought, or habit you treat as insignificant? How would your daily practice shift if you recognized that everything finite is accounted for and every small deed matters?

The Fourth Proof: Ratio of Magnitudes Implies Finite Parts
Fourth proof that bodies have finite parts via ratio argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ratio between magnitudes of bodies leads to the conclusion that they are composed of a finite number of parts.

Core Idea
The ratio of magnitudes forces the number of parts to be finite.

This passage presents the fourth proof that bodies cannot be composed of an infinite number of parts. The argument uses a crucial insight: the ratio of two finite magnitudes is finite, which compels the ratio of their part-numbers to also be finite. This yields the conclusion that each body has a finite number of parts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: multiplicity implies units
Read the opening lines: every multiplicity, finite or infinite, contains units. This is foundational for the argument.
The term 'units' (al-wāḥid) refers to the individual components of the multiplicity.
2
Consider the two possibilities when parts are joined
When two parts are joined, either the combined magnitude is greater than each alone, or it is not. The proof explores both scenarios.
Only the scenario where magnitude increases leads to the conclusion.
3
See how ratio of magnitudes tracks ratio of part numbers
If magnitude increases with more parts, then the ratio of magnitudes equals the ratio of the numbers of parts. This is a key step.
This connection relies on the idea that parts are homogeneous.
4
Grasp the conclusion: finite magnitude ratio implies finite part numbers
Since any two finite bodies have a finite ratio of magnitudes, the ratio of their part numbers must be finite, so each body has a finite number of parts.
This refutes the idea of infinite divisibility of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : كل كثرة سواء كانت متناهية أو غير متناهية - فإن الواحد فيها موجود . لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات ، ويمتنع حصول مجموع الوحدات ، إلا عند حصول كل واحدة من تلك الوحدات . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الجزء الواحد من تلك الأجزاء ، إذا انضم إلى جزء آخر ، فإن لم يكن مقدار الجزءين أزيد من مقدار الجزء الواحد ، لم يكن التأليف سبباً لحصول المقدار . فوجب أن لا تكون المقادير والأعظام متألفة من هذه الأجزاء . وإن كان مقادر الجزئين أزيد من مقدار الجزء الواحد ، فحينئذ يكون تألف تلك الأجزاء سبباً لازدياد الحجم والقدر . وكلما كانت تلك الأجزاء أكثر ، وجب أن يكون الحجم والمقدار أعظم . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب أن تكون نسبة أحد المقدارين إلى الثاني ، كنسبة العدد الحاصل في أحد المقدارين إلى العدد الحاصل في المقدار الثاني . ولما كانت نسبة أحد المقدارين إلى الآخر : نسبة متناهي القدر إلى متناهي القدر وجب أن تكون نسبة أحد العددين إلى الثاني : نسبة متناهي العدد إلى متناهي العدد وذلك يوجب كون عدد الأجزاء الحاصلة في هذه الأجسام متناهياً . وهو المطلوب
The fourth proof: Every multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, nevertheless contains units (al-wāḥid). Because multiplicity is an expression of a sum of units, and it is impossible for a sum of units to exist unless each one of those units exists. When this is established, we say: One part from among those parts, when it is joined to another part, if the magnitude of the two parts is not greater than the magnitude of the single part, then combination is not a cause for the acquisition of magnitude. So necessarily, magnitudes and sizes would not be composed of these parts. But if the magnitude of the two parts is greater than the magnitude of the single part, then the combination of those parts is a cause for the increase of volume and size. And whenever those parts are more numerous, the volume and magnitude must be greater. If this is so, then the ratio of one magnitude to another must be as the ratio of the number present in one magnitude to the number present in the second magnitude. And since the ratio of one magnitude to another is the ratio of finite capacity to finite capacity, it is necessary that the ratio of one number to the other is the ratio of finite number to finite number. That necessitates that the number of parts present in these bodies is finite. That is what was required.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-ḥujja al-rābi‘a) that bodies cannot be composed of an infinite number of parts. The argument proceeds as follows: First, it establishes that in any multiplicity (kathra), whether finite or infinite, there must exist individual units (al-wāḥid) because multiplicity is the sum of units. Then it considers two parts being joined. If the combined magnitude is not greater than each singly, then composition does not produce magnitude, so bodies would not be composed of these parts. But if the combined magnitude is greater, then more parts yield greater magnitude. Therefore, the ratio of two magnitudes equals the ratio of the numbers of their parts. Since any two finite bodies have a finite ratio of magnitudes, the ratio of their part-numbers must also be finite, implying that each body has a finite number of parts. The conclusion is that the number of parts is finite (al-maṭlūb).

Key Terms
كثرة
واحد
مقدار
عدد
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes all bodies are finite to conclude part-numbers are finite.
Correction: The argument does not assume all bodies are finite; it uses the ratio of any two finite magnitudes to deduce that the number of parts in each must be finite because the ratio of part numbers is finite.

Checkpoint

A) It is infinite
B) It is finite
C) It depends on the size
D) It is unknowable

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that the finite ratio between any two bodies implies the number of their parts must also be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument proceeds from the ratio of finite magnitudes. In your own life, what finite measures are you using to judge your worth or success? Are you willing to realign your standards with the infinite worth of the Creator, rather than the limited numbers this world offers?
?
If every body has a finite number of parts, then no body can be infinite. Does your daily behavior reflect the belief that you are limited and accountable? How will you live differently today knowing that your own physical existence is bounded by a finite number of parts – each created by Allah?

The Faster and Slower Bodies Proof
Sanusi presents the fifth proof against infinite parts using the faster/slower motion paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the paradox of a fast and slow moving body is used to disprove infinite parts in a body.

Core Idea
If space were infinitely divisible, a fast body could never catch a slow one.

In this lesson, we encounter a proof that uses an absurd consequence to reject the existence of infinite parts in a body. By imagining a race between a slow and a fast body, the argument shows that infinite divisibility leads to a logical impossibility. This is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: 'If A, then B; but B is false, so A is false.'
The passage is a compact proof, so read it twice.
2
Understand the paradox
Visualize a slow body moving, then a fast body starting later. The fast body must reach each previous position of the slow body, but the slow body keeps moving. With infinite parts (infinite intermediate positions), the fast body would never catch up.
This is similar to Zeno's paradox of Achilles and the tortoise.
3
Identify the conclusion
The consequent (the fast never catches) is false because in reality fast bodies do catch slow ones. Therefore, the antecedent (infinite parts) must also be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this proof is important: it establishes that bodies have finite parts, which is a foundational premise in Islamic atomistic cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو حصل في الجسم أجزاء غير متناهية ، لكان إذا شرع البطيء في الحركة ، ثم شرع السريع بعده ، أن لا يصل السريع إلى البطيء البتة . لأن السريع إذا ابتدأ بالحركة بعد البطيء ، وإلى أن يصل السريع إلى موضع البطيء : يكون البطيء قد تحرك عن ذلك الموضع ثم إذا وصل السريع ، إلى الموضع الثاني : يكون البطيء قد انتقل عنه إلى موضع ثالث . فلو حصل في الجسم أبعاض غير متناهية ، لزم أن لا يصل السريع إلى البطيء البتة . ولما كان هذا التالي كاذباً ، كان المقدم أيضاً باطلاً.
The fifth proof: If a body had infinite parts, then when a slow-moving body begins motion and then a fast-moving body begins after it, the fast-moving body would never reach the slow-moving body. Because when the fast-moving body begins motion after the slow-moving body, by the time the fast-moving body reaches the position of the slow-moving body, the slow-moving body will have moved from that position. Then when the fast-moving body reaches the second position, the slow-moving body will have moved to a third position. So if a body had infinite parts, it would necessarily follow that the fast-moving body would never reach the slow-moving body. Since this consequent is false, the antecedent is also false.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisah) against infinite parts in a body. It is an aporia similar to Zeno's paradox: if a body has infinite parts (i.e., space is infinitely divisible), then a faster moving body can never catch a slower one, because the slower always moves ahead while the faster is covering the previous distance. The argument states that this consequent (al-tālī) is false (kādhib), because in reality fast bodies do catch slow ones. Therefore, the antecedent (al-muqaddam) – that the body has infinite parts – must also be false. Thus, bodies cannot have infinitely many parts.

Key Terms
بطيء
سريع
أجزاء غير متناهية
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that motion is possible.
Correction: No, it assumes motion is possible and uses the fact that fast bodies do catch slow ones to disprove infinite divisibility.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof assume would happen if a body had infinite parts?
A) The fast body would eventually catch the slow
B) The fast body would never catch the slow
C) The slow body would catch the fast
D) Motion would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof uses the impossibility of a fast body catching a slow one to argue against infinite parts in a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even what seems logically possible (infinite parts) leads to absurdity in reality. Where in your life are you holding onto beliefs that, if truly followed, would lead to absurd consequences? Will you honestly examine them today?
?
The fast body catching the slow is a daily reality you experience. Do you live as if the world has clear, divine limits, or do you float in vague possibilities, ignoring the boundaries Allah has placed? Pick one area where you will accept a limit as a divine appointment.
?
If the universe is finite in its very structure, what does that say about the fleeting nature of your own life? Are you living as if you have infinite time, or are you acting with the urgency that finite moments demand? What will you change today?
