Three Arguments for Divisibility
Sanusi presents three concise rational arguments for the divisibility of any extended entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three concise rational arguments that every extended thing must be divisible, and how each argument demonstrates the necessity of division.

Core Idea
Any extended thing necessarily has distinct sides, implying divisibility.

Sanusi presents three alternative proofs for the divisibility of extended entities. Each proof uses a different angle: direct knowledge, contact between substances, and the impossibility of contradictory properties. Together, they establish a foundational principle in kalam physics. Let's examine each argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation to understand the meaning. Note the three distinct proofs introduced with 'al-awwal', 'al-thani', 'al-thalith'.
The passage is concise; each argument is only a few lines.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the three proofs: first uses immediate knowledge of sides, second uses contact with different faces, third uses contradictory alignments. Understand the logical structure of each.
Each proof concludes that the extended thing must be divisible.
3
Analyze the first argument
The first argument states we know by necessity that the right is not left and the upper is not lower. This distinction of sides forces division.
This argument relies on direct intuitive knowledge.
4
Analyze the second and third arguments
The second argument assumes a middle substance touching two neighbors; it must have two distinct faces. The third argument uses contradictory alignments; if sides were identical, contradictories would hold.
Both arguments show that distinct parts must exist.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
All three arguments lead to the same conclusion: every extended thing is divisible. This is a key principle in kalam physics.
These are not empirical but rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذا الدليل يمكن ذكره على وجوه أخرى ، سوى الوجه الذي ذكرناه : فالأول : أن يقال : إن كل متحيز ، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن يمينه غير يساره ، وأن الوجه الذي منه يلي السماء ، غير الوجه الذي منه يلي الأرض وذلك يوجب كونها منقسمة . وهذا الوجه على اختصاره يفيد المطلوب . الثاني : إنا إذا فرضنا جوهراً بين جوهرين . فإن المتوسط يماس ما على يمينه ، بوجه غير الوجه الذي به يماس ما على يساره . وذلك يوجب الانقسام . الثالث : إن جانب اليمين من ذلك المتوسط ، محكوم عليه بأنه يحاذي ما على اليمين ، ولا يحاذي ما على اليسار . وجانب اليسار منه محكوم عليه بضد ذلك . فلو كان الجانب الأيمن منه ، غير الجانب الأيسر ، لزم أن يصدق النقيضان على الشيء الواحد . وإنه محال . فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير هذا الوجه
And know: that this proof can be mentioned in other ways besides the manner we mentioned: The first: that it is said: Every extended thing, we know by necessity that its right is not its left, and that the face from which it faces the sky is not the face from which it faces the earth, and that necessitates its being divisible. And this way, despite its brevity, yields the desired conclusion. The second: that if we assume a substance between two substances, the intermediate substance touches what is on its right with a face other than the face with which it touches what is on its left, and that necessitates division. The third: that the right side of that intermediate substance is judged to be aligned with what is on the right and not aligned with what is on the left, and the left side of it is judged opposite to that. So if the right side of it is other than the left side, then two contradictories would be true of the same thing, and that is impossible. So this is the summary of the talk in presenting this way.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents three alternative arguments for the divisibility of any extended entity (mutahayyiz). The first argument is from immediate knowledge: we know necessarily that the right side of an extended thing is not its left side, and the upper face is not the lower face. This distinction of sides implies that the thing has parts, hence divisibility. The second argument assumes a substance (jawhar) between two others. The middle substance touches the right one with one face and the left one with a different face. If the substance were indivisible, it would have only one face, but it must have two distinct faces for contact, so it must be divisible. The third argument uses the concept of alignment (muhadhat). The right side aligns with the right neighbor and not with the left, while the left side aligns with the left and not the right. If the right side were identical to the left side, then both contradictory alignments would apply to the same thing, which is impossible; therefore the sides are distinct, implying division. All three arguments are presented as concise alternatives to the longer proof previously given.

Key Terms
متحيز (extended)
جوهر (substance)
انقسام (division)
نقيضان (contradictories)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments assume that atoms are physical objects that can be split.
Correction: They are purely logical demonstrations based on the concept of extension. Any extended thing, whether physical or mental, must have parts.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what does the fact that a middle substance touches its neighbors with different faces prove?
A) That the substance is divisible.
B) That the substance is indivisible.
C) That the neighbors are divisible.
D) That contact is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments demonstrate that any extended entity must be divisible because it contains distinct parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read three rational proofs that any extended thing must have parts. Reflect on your own body: do you truly live as if you are a composite being, utterly dependent on the One who composes you? What would change in your daily awareness if you internalized this truth?
?
The third argument shows impossible contradictions if you treat your right and left sides as identical. In your life, are there contradictions you hold onto—beliefs or actions that cannot both be true? Will you resolve them today?
?
These proofs are logical, but do they stir your heart to greater awe of Allah? If not, what is blocking that response? Ask Allah to give you a mind that sees His signs in every logical necessity.

Second Proof of Possibility: Circles on a Sphere
Author uses decreasing circles on a sphere to argue that an indivisible part must contact two others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a geometric argument from spherical geometry supports the possibility of an indivisible part contacting two others.

Core Idea
A sphere's curved surface forces atoms to touch multiple neighbors.

In this lesson, we examine the second proof of possibility from the author. He uses a geometric example involving circles on a sphere. By assuming magnitudes are made of indivisible parts, he shows that the only way to form a sphere is if a single part touches two others. This argument relies on simple geometry and logical disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometry of circles on a sphere
Visualize a sphere: the largest circle is the equator, and circles decrease in size toward the poles. The author refers to this familiar fact.
This sets up the spatial arrangement.
2
Assume magnitudes are composed of indivisible parts
The argument is conditional: if atoms exist, each circle (equator, second, etc.) consists of indivisible parts.
This is a hypothetical, not a claim of actuality.
3
Consider the two possibilities for contact
Either each part of the equator connects directly to a part of the next circle, or a part of the second circle lies at the junction between two parts of the equator.
These are the only logical alternatives.
4
See why the first possibility fails
If each part connects directly, the second circle would have the same circumference as the equator, producing a cylinder, not a sphere.
This contradicts the spherical shape.
5
Accept the conclusion
Therefore, the second possibility must hold: a single part contacts two parts, proving that an indivisible part can contact two others.
This is the required result for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أن المعنى الذي ذكرناه ممكن : أن نقول : لا شك أن أعظم الدوائر في الكرة : المنطقة . ثم لا تزال الدوائر تتصاغر مرتبة فمرتبة ، حتى تنتهي إلى القطبين . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إن بتقدير أن تكون المقادير مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، كانت المنطقة مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . والدائرة الثانية الملتصقة بالمنطقة تكون أيضاً مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . وهكذا القول في جميع الدوائر . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إما أن يقال : إن كل جزء من أجزاء المنطقة ، فإنه يتصل به جزء من أجزاء الدائرة الثانية ، أو يقال : إن الجزء المعين من أجزاء الدائرة الثانية ، لا يتصل بالجزء المعين من المنطقة ، وإنما يقع على موضع اتصال جزء بجزء آخر من المنطقة . والأول باطل . وإلا لزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة الثانية ، مساوياً لمدار الدائرة الأولى العظيمة ، التي المنطقة . وحينئذ لا تتولد الكرة من مثل هذا التركيب ، وإنما تتولد الأسطوانة . ولما بطل هذا القسم أعني القسم الثاني . فذلك يوجب وقوع الجوهر الواحد ، على متصل الجوهرين . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second aspect in demonstrating that the meaning we mentioned is possible: we say: There is no doubt that the greatest circles on the sphere is the equator. Then the circles continuously diminish rank by rank until they terminate at the poles. If you know this, we say: On the assumption that magnitudes are composed of indivisible parts, the equator is composed of indivisible parts. And the second circle adjacent to the equator is also composed of indivisible parts. And so on for all circles. If this is established, we say: Either it is said that each part of the parts of the equator connects with a part of the parts of the second circle, or it is said that the specific part of the parts of the second circle does not connect with the specific part of the equator, but rather lies at the point of connection of one part with another part of the equator. The first is false; otherwise it would necessitate that the circumference of the second circle be equal to the circumference of the first great circle, which is the equator. And then the sphere would not be generated from such a composite; instead a cylinder would be generated. And since this division has been invalidated, that is, the second division, it necessitates the occurrence of a single substance upon the contact of two substances. And that is what is required.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the second proof of possibility. The author uses the geometry of spheres. The largest circle on a sphere is the equator; circles decrease in size until the poles. If magnitudes are composed of indivisible parts, each circle is made of parts. Then there are two possibilities: either each part of the equator connects directly with a part of the next circle, or the part of the second circle lies at the junction between two parts of the equator. The first possibility is rejected because it would make all circles equal in circumference, producing a cylinder rather than a sphere. Therefore, the second possibility must hold: a single part (of the second circle) contacts two parts (of the equator). This proves that a single indivisible part can be in contact with two others, which is the required conclusion.

Key Terms
المنطقة
الكرة
الأسطوانة
متصل الجوهرين
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that indivisible parts actually exist.
Correction: The argument is conditional: if magnitudes are composed of atoms, then such contact must occur. It does not prove atomism itself.

Checkpoint
In the sphere argument, why is the first option (each part connects directly) rejected?
A) Because it would make the sphere into a cylinder.
B) Because it would require infinite circles.
C) Because it contradicts the definition of a sphere.
D) Because parts cannot connect directly.

Key Takeaway
The geometric argument shows that on the assumption of indivisible parts, a single part must contact two others to form a sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof eliminates possibilities until only one remains. Have you ever honestly eliminated comfortable but false beliefs about yourself to arrive at an uncomfortable truth? What did you find?
?
The author assumes atoms to see what logically follows. What assumption do you hold about your own nature that you have never examined? Are you willing to follow its logical conclusions wherever they lead?
?
The geometry shows that unity (a sphere) emerges from necessary contact between parts. In your relationship with Allah, do you view your good deeds as isolated contacts with reward, or as part of a greater whole that requires connections you might not have considered?

Third Proof on Body Composition: From Surfaces to Sensible Bodies
Author presents a third version of the body composition proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third formulation of the proof on body composition demonstrates that the argument holds for all sensible bodies without relying on the assumption of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
The proof works even without indivisible parts; it applies directly to everyday bodies.

The author offers a third way of presenting the classical proof that bodies are composed of surfaces. This approach cleverly shows that the argument does not depend on the existence of indivisible parts—it is valid for all perceptible bodies. In this lesson, we will walk through the logical steps of this formulation and see how it strengthens the overall case for body composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The passage uses a hypothetical assumption to make its point.
2
Identify the three logical phases
Break down the passage into: (1) the assumption of indivisible parts, (2) the consequence of stacked surfaces, (3) the example of sunlight, and (4) the key conclusion.
The conclusion is the most important part: the proof does not need indivisible parts.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why the author emphasizes that the proof applies to all sensible bodies. How does this strengthen the argument compared to other formulations?
This shows the argument's universality.
4
Engage with the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the proof requires indivisible parts. How does this passage correct that?
The author explicitly states the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن ذكره على طريق ثالث : فيقال : إن بتقدير كون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، يكون الجسم مركباً من سطوح موضوعة بعضها على البعض . فإذا أشرقت الشمس على الجسم ، فقد استنار وجهه . وذلك الوجه عبارة عن أحد وجهي السطح الأعلى منه . وأما الوجه الثاني من ذلك السطح ، فإنه متصل بالسطح الأسفل منه ، وواقع في عمق ذلك الجسم. فيثبت : أن هذا البرهان لا حاجة في تقريره إلى وجود سطح مؤلف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، بل هذا البرهان حاصل في جميع هذه الأجسام المحسوسة
And it can be mentioned in a third way: it is said: on the assumption that the body is composed of indivisible parts, the body is composed of surfaces placed one upon another. So when the sun shines on the body, its face is illuminated. That face is one of the two faces of the uppermost surface. As for the second face of that surface, it is connected to the lower surface and lies in the depth of that body. Thus it is established that this proof does not need in its formulation the existence of a surface composed of indivisible parts; rather this proof holds for all these sensible bodies.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a third formulation of a proof concerning the composition of bodies. The author begins by assuming, for the sake of argument, that a body is composed of indivisible parts. On that assumption, he considers the body as made of stacked surfaces. He then uses the example of sunlight: when the sun shines on the body, the topmost surface's upper face is illuminated, while its lower face is connected to the surface below and lies inside the body. The key conclusion is that this argument does not rely on the existence of surfaces made of indivisible parts; instead, it applies directly to all perceptible bodies. In other words, the proof is valid even without postulating atomism, and it works on observable physical bodies.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
سطوح (surfaces)
أجزاء لا تتجزأ (indivisible parts)
برهان (proof)
محسوسة (sensible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for body composition from surfaces requires the existence of indivisible parts to be valid.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this proof does not need the existence of surfaces composed of indivisible parts; it applies to all sensible bodies.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the proof presented in the third way?
A) It requires the existence of indivisible parts.
B) It only applies to bodies composed of indivisible parts.
C) It does not require indivisible parts and applies to all sensible bodies.
D) It is invalid because it assumes indivisible parts.

Key Takeaway
The third formulation shows that the proof of body composition from surfaces is independent of atomism and valid for all perceptible bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author appeals to a simple everyday observation—sunlight on a body—to build a proof. When was the last time you let a common physical experience deepen your understanding of a spiritual truth? Commit to noticing one such moment tomorrow and reflecting on it.

The Proof Yields a False Conclusion: Infinite Parts
Author shows proof leads to infinite parts absurdity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the proof for composition entails actually infinite parts, which is false, making the proof sophistical.

Core Idea
A valid proof cannot lead to an absurdity; this one does.

The author begins by noting that no scholar has raised a convincing objection to the proofs, but he finds room for critique. He then demonstrates that the proof, if accepted, forces the conclusion that a body is composed of actually infinite parts—a result rejected by all philosophers. This establishes the proof as sophistical rather than real.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Identify the author's statement that the proof yields a false result by consensus.
The author is not rejecting the proof outright; he is showing its logical consequence.
2
Follow the division argument
Understand how the proof forces a body to be divided into halves, each half further divisible, leading to infinite actual parts.
The key is that the meeting of different ends forces distinct loci, leading to infinite regress.
3
Recognize the false conclusion
Note that infinite actual parts is deemed false by all philosophers (including the author's opponents).
This contradiction invalidates the proof.
4
Understand the verdict
The author concludes that the proof is sophistical because it produces an unacceptable result.
This sets up the further critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا هو الكلام في تقرير هذه البراهين الأربعة على أقصى الوجوه . واعلم : أن أحداً من المتكلمين ما أورد على شيء من هذه البراهين : كلاماً ، يصلح لأن يلتفت إليه العاقل . إلا أنا نقول : للبحث فيه مجال . وتقريره : أنا نبين أن هذه الحجة : تنتج نتيجة باطلة بالاتفاق . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر لنا : أنها حجة مغالطية ، وليست حجة حقيقية. أما بيان أنها تنتج نتيجة باطلة بالاتفاق فهو أنا نقول : إن هذه الحجة لو صحت ، لأنتجت كون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. ومعلوم : أن هذه النتيجة باطلة. أما بيان الأول . فلأن الجسم إذا لقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً ، وبالطرف الثاني منه شيئاً آخر، فهاتان الملاقاتان متغايرتان بالفعل ، فوجب أن يكون محلاهما متغايرين بالفعل . ثم نقول : هذان المحلان إن كانا عرضين عاد التقسيم فيه ، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي آخر الأمر إلى وقوع الكثرة في ذات الجسم ، فحينئذ ينتصف ذلك الجسم بالفعل . ثم إن كل واحد من ذينك النصفين يلقى النصف الثاني بأحد وجهيه دون الثاني . فوجب أن ينتصف ذلك النصف أيضاً بالفعل فإن كان ذلك الجسم قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها لوجب أن يحصل فيه أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل على الوجه الذي بيناه . إلا أن هذا القول باطل باتفاق الحكماء . فثبت : أن النتيجة التي يجب لزومها من هذه الحجة : قول باطل باتفاق الحكماء . والذي يريد الحكماء إثباته ، فإن هذه الحجة لا تفيده ولا توجيه فثبت أن هذه الحجة حجة مغالطية باطلة . فإن قال قائل : هب أن مقصود الحكيم لا يحصل من هذه الحجة ، إلا أن أقواماً آخرين لو تمسكوا بها في إثبات أن الجسم مؤلف من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل ، فكيف الجواب ؟ فنقول : إن هذه الحجة لا تفيد أيضاً هذا المطلوب وذلك لأن من قال بالكثرة ، فسواء كانت تلك الكثرة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإنه لا بد وأن يعترف بوجود الواحد فيها . لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات ، بدون حصول الوحدة . فالقائلون يكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية ، لا بد وأن يعترفوا بوجود أشياء ، يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه واحداً . إلا أن الحجة المذكورة تبطل وجود الواحد لأن أي شيء فرض كونه واحداً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يلقى يمينه غير ما يلقاه يساره فيكون منقسماً فلا يكون الواحد واحداً . فثبت أن هذه الحجة توجب حصول الكثرة بالفعل ولما أوجبت حصول الكثرة بالفعل ، فقد أوجبت حصول الواحد بالفعل . ولكنها تمنع حصول الواحد بالفعل بالطريق الذي ذكرناه . وإذا منعت من حصول الواحد بالفعل ، فقد منعت من حصول الكثرة. فثبت : أن هذه الحجة توجب القول بحصول الكثرة ، وتمنع من القول بحصول الكثرة فهي حجة تفيد نتائج متناقضة . فهي حجة مغالطية ، لا حجة يقينية . هذا القدر كفى لبيان أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها : وجوه باطلة ، غير حقيقية ، ولا يقينية . وهذا القدر كاف لبيان ضعفها وسقوطها
This is the discussion concerning the formulation of these four proofs in the most complete manner. And know: none of the mutakallimun has raised against any of these proofs a statement worthy of a rational person's attention. However, we say: there is room for investigation in it. Its formulation: we show that this proof yields a result that is false by consensus. And when this is established, it becomes clear to us that it is a sophistical argument, not a real proof. As for showing that it yields a false result by consensus, we say: this proof, if valid, would entail that the body is composed of actually infinite parts. And it is known that this result is false. As for showing the first: because if a body meets one thing with one of its ends and another thing with its second end, these two meetins are actually distinct, so their loci must be actually distinct. Then we say: these two loci, if they are accidents, the division recurs, and it does not go on infinitely, but finally leads to the occurrence of multiplicity in the substance of the body; then that body is actually divided. Then each of those two halves meets the second half with one of its faces and not the other. So that half must also be actually divided. So if that body were capable of infinite divisions, then there must actually occur in it infinite parts in the manner we have explained. However, this statement is false by consensus of the philosophers. So it is established that the result necessarily following from this proof is a false statement by consensus of the philosophers. And what the philosophers want to prove, this proof does not afford it nor does it give it. So it is established that this proof is a sophistical false argument. And if someone says: Granted that the object of the philosopher is not obtained from this proof, but if other people were to cling to it to prove that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, what is the response? We say: This proof also does not serve that desired end. That is because whoever affirms multiplicity, whether that multiplicity is finite or infinite, must acknowledge the existence of the one within it. For multiplicity is the sum of units, without the occurrence of unity. So those who say that the body is composed of infinite parts must acknowledge the existence of things each of which is in itself one. But the proof in question destroys the existence of the one, because any thing assumed to be one must meet with its right something other than what it meets with its left, so it becomes divided, so the one is not one. Thus it is established that this proof entails the actual existence of multiplicity; and since it entails the actual existence of multiplicity, it entails the actual existence of the one. But it prevents the actual existence of the one in the manner we mentioned. And if it prevents the actual existence of the one, it prevents the actual existence of multiplicity. So it is established that this proof necessitates the assertion of multiplicity and prevents the assertion of multiplicity. So it is a proof that yields contradictory results. So it is a sophistical argument, not a certain proof. This suffices to show that all these indications are false, not real, and not certain. And this suffices to show their weakness and collapse.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed critique of the proof discussed earlier. The author begins by stating that no scholar of kalam has raised a valid objection to these proofs, but he finds room for critique. He aims to show that the proof leads to a false conclusion by consensus, making it sophistical. He demonstrates that if the proof were valid, it would imply that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, a conclusion rejected by all philosophers (and presumably by the author's school). The argument proceeds by considering a body meeting two different things at its ends, forcing a division, then each half again dividing, and so on ad infinitum, leading to actual infinite parts, which is absurd. The author then addresses an objection: what if someone uses this proof to claim infinite parts? He responds that the proof also fails because it destroys the concept of 'the one' (al-wahid). Multiplicity requires units that are each one, but the proof shows that any supposed 'one' is divisible, thus no true one exists. Therefore the proof both entails multiplicity (by forcing divisions) and prevents the one, leading to contradiction. Hence the proof is self-refuting and useless for establishing any position. The author concludes that all such proofs are false and unreliable.

Key Terms
حجة
نتيجة باطلة
أجزاء لا نهاية لها
مغالطية
الحكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof successfully demonstrates composition without any flaws.
Correction: The author shows that the proof leads to the unacceptable conclusion of actual infinite parts, making it invalid.

Checkpoint
What false conclusion does the author claim the proof leads to?
A) The body is composed of a finite number of parts.
B) The body is composed of actually infinite parts.
C) The body is not composed at all.
D) The proof is valid.

Key Takeaway
The proof for composition fails because it implies actually infinite parts, an absurdity that renders the proof sophistical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage shows that a proof must be tested to its logical end. Is there a belief you hold that you have never examined for logical consistency? Are you willing to follow it to its conclusion, even if it leads to something you consider absurd?
?
The author uses the consensus of philosophers to reject the conclusion. In matters of faith, do you trust the consensus of scholars over your own reasoning? Where do you draw the line between accepting authority and testing arguments yourself?

The Proof Self-Destructs: Contradiction on Unity
Author shows proof leads to contradiction on unity and multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that the proof leads to a contradiction by both affirming and denying the existence of the one, making it self-refuting.

Core Idea
A proof that both necessitates and negates multiplicity is internally contradictory.

Having shown the proof leads to an absurd conclusion, the author now addresses an objection: could the proof at least prove the existence of infinite parts? He responds by revealing a deeper flaw: the proof destroys the very concept of 'the one' required for multiplicity. Hence the proof simultaneously entails and negates multiplicity, a clear contradiction that confirms its sophistical nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
A hypothetical objector suggests that while the proof fails for the philosophers, it might still prove infinite parts for others.
The author takes this as an opportunity to show the proof is even more flawed.
2
Grasp the necessity of the one
Any multiplicity (finite or infinite) requires units that are each 'one'.
This is a conceptual point: multiplicity is a collection of ones.
3
See how the proof destroys the one
The proof's logic forces any supposed 'one' to be divisible, so no true one exists.
This follows from the meeting argument applied to any unit.
4
Identify the contradiction
The proof both entails multiplicity (by forcing division) and prevents it (by denying the one), thus yielding contradictory results.
This makes the proof self-refuting and completely unreliable.

أن هذه الحجة حجة مغالطية باطلة . فإن قال قائل : هب أن مقصود الحكيم لا يحصل من هذه الحجة ، إلا أن أقواماً آخرين لو تمسكوا بها في إثبات أن الجسم مؤلف من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل ، فكيف الجواب ؟ فنقول : إن هذه الحجة لا تفيد أيضاً هذا المطلوب وذلك لأن من قال بالكثرة ، فسواء كانت تلك الكثرة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإنه لا بد وأن يعترف بوجود الواحد فيها . لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات ، بدون حصول الوحدة . فالقائلون يكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية ، لا بد وأن يعترفوا بوجود أشياء ، يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه واحداً . إلا أن الحجة المذكورة تبطل وجود الواحد لأن أي شيء فرض كونه واحداً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يلقى يمينه غير ما يلقاه يساره فيكون منقسماً فلا يكون الواحد واحداً . فثبت أن هذه الحجة توجب حصول الكثرة بالفعل ولما أوجبت حصول الكثرة بالفعل ، فقد أوجبت حصول الواحد بالفعل . ولكنها تمنع حصول الواحد بالفعل بالطريق الذي ذكرناه . وإذا منعت من حصول الواحد بالفعل ، فقد منعت من حصول الكثرة. فثبت : أن هذه الحجة توجب القول بحصول الكثرة ، وتمنع من القول بحصول الكثرة فهي حجة تفيد نتائج متناقضة . فهي حجة مغالطية ، لا حجة يقينية . هذا القدر كفى لبيان أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها : وجوه باطلة ، غير حقيقية ، ولا يقينية . وهذا القدر كاف لبيان ضعفها وسقوطها
This is the discussion concerning the formulation of these four proofs in the most complete manner. And know: none of the mutakallimun has raised against any of these proofs a statement worthy of a rational person's attention. However, we say: there is room for investigation in it. Its formulation: we show that this proof yields a result that is false by consensus. And when this is established, it becomes clear to us that it is a sophistical argument, not a real proof. As for showing that it yields a false result by consensus, we say: this proof, if valid, would entail that the body is composed of actually infinite parts. And it is known that this result is false. As for showing the first: because if a body meets one thing with one of its ends and another thing with its second end, these two meetins are actually distinct, so their loci must be actually distinct. Then we say: these two loci, if they are accidents, the division recurs, and it does not go on infinitely, but finally leads to the occurrence of multiplicity in the substance of the body; then that body is actually divided. Then each of those two halves meets the second half with one of its faces and not the other. So that half must also be actually divided. So if that body were capable of infinite divisions, then there must actually occur in it infinite parts in the manner we have explained. However, this statement is false by consensus of the philosophers. So it is established that the result necessarily following from this proof is a false statement by consensus of the philosophers. And what the philosophers want to prove, this proof does not afford it nor does it give it. So it is established that this proof is a sophistical false argument. And if someone says: Granted that the object of the philosopher is not obtained from this proof, but if other people were to cling to it to prove that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, what is the response? We say: This proof also does not serve that desired end. That is because whoever affirms multiplicity, whether that multiplicity is finite or infinite, must acknowledge the existence of the one within it. For multiplicity is the sum of units, without the occurrence of unity. So those who say that the body is composed of infinite parts must acknowledge the existence of things each of which is in itself one. But the proof in question destroys the existence of the one, because any thing assumed to be one must meet with its right something other than what it meets with its left, so it becomes divided, so the one is not one. Thus it is established that this proof entails the actual existence of multiplicity; and since it entails the actual existence of multiplicity, it entails the actual existence of the one. But it prevents the actual existence of the one in the manner we mentioned. And if it prevents the actual existence of the one, it prevents the actual existence of multiplicity. So it is established that this proof necessitates the assertion of multiplicity and prevents the assertion of multiplicity. So it is a proof that yields contradictory results. So it is a sophistical argument, not a certain proof. This suffices to show that all these indications are false, not real, and not certain. And this suffices to show their weakness and collapse.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed critique of the proof discussed earlier. The author begins by stating that no scholar of kalam has raised a valid objection to these proofs, but he finds room for critique. He aims to show that the proof leads to a false conclusion by consensus, making it sophistical. He demonstrates that if the proof were valid, it would imply that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, a conclusion rejected by all philosophers (and presumably by the author's school). The argument proceeds by considering a body meeting two different things at its ends, forcing a division, then each half again dividing, and so on ad infinitum, leading to actual infinite parts, which is absurd. The author then addresses an objection: what if someone uses this proof to claim infinite parts? He responds that the proof also fails because it destroys the concept of 'the one' (al-wahid). Multiplicity requires units that are each one, but the proof shows that any supposed 'one' is divisible, thus no true one exists. Therefore the proof both entails multiplicity (by forcing divisions) and prevents the one, leading to contradiction. Hence the proof is self-refuting and useless for establishing any position. The author concludes that all such proofs are false and unreliable.

Key Terms
واحد
كثرة
مغالطية
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof can be used to argue for infinite parts without contradiction.
Correction: The author shows that the proof leads to a contradiction because it both requires and negates the existence of the one, which is necessary for multiplicity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the proof yields contradictory results?
A) It proves infinite parts but also finite parts.
B) It affirms multiplicity but denies the existence of the one that multiplicity requires.
C) It relies on an assumption that is not accepted by all.
D) It leads to an infinite regress without resolution.

Key Takeaway
A proof that leads to contradictory conclusions about the same thing is self-refuting and cannot be considered valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold onto an opinion that seems rational but leads to contradictions in your life—like excusing a sin while knowing it's wrong—are you willing to abandon that opinion as the author discards this proof? What contradiction might you be ignoring today?
?
This passage shows that a proof which both affirms and denies the same thing is worthless. Examine your own heart: where do you simultaneously affirm your duty to Allah and deny it by your actions? How will you resolve that contradiction before tomorrow?
?
The author calls the proof 'sophistical' because it appears useful but is actually self-refuting. Are there justifications you use to cover a wrong habit that, if honestly examined, fall apart? Will you drop that justification and repent now?

Refutation of a Proof for Infinite Parts
The author refutes a proof claiming infinite parts by showing it self-destructs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a proof for infinite parts in a body is shown to be self-contradictory and fallacious.

Core Idea
A self-contradictory proof cannot prove anything, not even what it claims.

In this passage, the author demonstrates the fallacy in a proof that claims to show a body is composed of actually infinite parts. He first shows the proof's conclusion is false by consensus, then addresses an objection by showing the proof inherently contradicts itself. This lesson reveals the logical criterion for identifying a fallacious argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the author's argumentative flow and the two main sections.
The passage begins with a refutation and then handles an objection.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Look for the initial refutation (lines 1-6) and the objection-response (lines 7-18). Mark where the author transitions.
3
Understand the contradiction
The author shows the proof simultaneously necessitates multiplicity (hence units) and destroys the unit. Find the exact reasoning for this contradiction.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the final verdict: the proof is fallacious and not certain. Ask yourself why a proof that leads to contradictory results cannot be accepted.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني بأحد وجهيه دون الثاني . فوجب أن ينتصف ذلك النصف أيضاً بالفعل فإن كان ذلك الجسم قابلاً لانقسامات لا نهاية لها لوجب أن يحصل فيه أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل على الوجه الذي بيناه . إلا أن هذا القول باطل باتفاق الحكماء . فثبت : أن النتيجة التي يجب لزومها من هذه الحجة : قول باطل باتفاق الحكماء . والذي يريد الحكماء إثباته ، فإن هذه الحجة لا تفيده ولا توجيه فثبت أن هذه الحجة حجة مغالطية باطلة . فإن قال قائل : هب أن مقصود الحكيم لا يحصل من هذه الحجة ، إلا أن أقواماً آخرين لو تمسكوا بها في إثبات أن الجسم مؤلف من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل ، فكيف الجواب ؟ فنقول : إن هذه الحجة لا تفيد أيضاً هذا المطلوب وذلك لأن من قال بالكثرة ، فسواء كانت تلك الكثرة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإنه لا بد وأن يعترف بوجود الواحد فيها . لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات ، بدون حصول الوحدة . فالقائلون يكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية ، لا بد وأن يعترفوا بوجود أشياء ، يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه واحداً . إلا أن الحجة المذكورة تبطل وجود الواحد لأن أي شيء فرض كونه واحداً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يلقى يمينه غير ما يلقاه يساره فيكون منقسماً فلا يكون الواحد واحداً . فثبت أن هذه الحجة توجب حصول الكثرة بالفعل ولما أوجبت حصول الكثرة بالفعل ، فقد أوجبت حصول الواحد بالفعل . ولكنها تمنع حصول الواحد بالفعل بالطريق الذي ذكرناه . وإذا منعت من حصول الواحد بالفعل ، فقد منعت من حصول الكثرة . فثبت : أن هذه الحجة توجب القول بحصول الكثرة ، وتمنع من القول بحصول الكثرة فهي حجة تفيد نتائج متناقضة . فهي حجة مغالطية ، لا حجة يقينية . هذا القدر كاف لبيان أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها : وجوه باطلة ، غير حقيقية ، ولا يقينية . وهذا القدر كاف لبيان ضعفها وسقوطها
The second by one of its two faces without the second. So it must be that that half is also actually divided. For if that body is susceptible to infinite divisions, then there must occur in it actually infinite parts in the manner we have explained. But this statement is false by agreement of the philosophers. So it is established that the conclusion that must follow from this proof is a statement false by agreement of the philosophers. And what the philosophers intend to prove, this proof does not benefit it nor give it direction. So it is established that this proof is a fallacious, false proof. Then if someone says: Grant that the philosopher's purpose is not achieved by this proof, but other people might adhere to it in proving that the body is composed of actually infinite parts, so what is the answer? We say: This proof also does not benefit this desired conclusion, because whoever affirms multiplicity, whether that multiplicity is finite or infinite, must acknowledge the existence of the one within it, because multiplicity is an expression of the sum of units, without the occurrence of unity. So those who say the body is composed of infinite parts must acknowledge the existence of things each of which is in itself one. Yet the aforementioned proof destroys the existence of the one, because any thing that is assumed to be one must meet with its right something other than what it meets with its left, so it becomes divided, so the one is not one. So it is established that this proof necessitates the occurrence of actual multiplicity, and when it necessitated the occurrence of actual multiplicity, it has necessitated the occurrence of actual one. But it prevents the occurrence of actual one by the way we mentioned. And if it prevents the occurrence of actual one, then it has prevented the occurrence of multiplicity. So it is established that this proof necessitates affirming multiplicity and prevents affirming multiplicity; so it is a proof that yields contradictory results. So it is a fallacious proof, not a certain proof. This much is sufficient to show that all these indications are false, not real, and not certain. And this much is sufficient to show their weakness and collapse.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific proof (hujja) concerning the division of bodies. The author begins by stating that a certain proof—referred to as 'the second'—leads to a conclusion that is false by consensus of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), so the proof itself is fallacious. Then an objector raises a concern: even if this proof does not achieve the philosopher's intended aim, others might use it to prove that a body is composed of actually infinite parts. The author responds that the proof does not even achieve that goal. The reasoning is that any multiplicity (kathra) necessarily includes units (āḥād), because multiplicity is the aggregate of units. However, the same proof that posits infinite parts also undermines the existence of any unit, because any unit would meet something different on its right and left, thus becoming divided and losing its unity. Therefore, the proof simultaneously requires the existence of actual multiplicity (and thus units) and denies the existence of units (and thus multiplicity). This contradiction shows the proof is self-destructive and cannot be a certain demonstration. The author concludes that all such proofs are false and unreliable.

D) Because the proof only applies to finite division.

Key Takeaway
A proof that leads to contradictory conclusions is fallacious and cannot be accepted as a certain demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The scholars rejected this proof despite its apparent cleverness. Is there a comfortable falsehood you have allowed to persist because it supports a desired conclusion? What would it cost you to abandon it, and are you ready to pay that price for truth?

Reinforcement from Euclidean Geometry on Points
Sanusi reinforces his argument using Euclid's postulate about points and lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single point can relate to many points without division, using Euclid's postulate as evidence.

Core Idea
A single point can be opposite to all points without being divided.

The author strengthens his previous argument by appealing to geometry. He shows that a central point in a circle is opposite to every point on the circumference, and even to all points in the cosmos. This is supported by Euclid's postulate that a straight line can be drawn between any two points, illustrating that one point can have multiple relations without being split.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with 'والذي يؤكد ما ذكرناه' indicating reinforcement.
The passage is incomplete but the argument is clear.
2
Understand the geometry example
The central point is opposite to all points that can be supposed in the circle. Appreciate how this shows a single point relating to many.
3
Connect to Euclid's postulate
Euclid's postulate states we can connect any two points with a straight line. This implies that from the central point, lines can be drawn to every point on the circumference, so the central point is indeed opposite to them all.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off. What do you think the next part would say? Likely concluding that just as a point is not divided by many opposite points, an atom is not divided by having many directions.

Commentary
This passage provides additional reinforcement (والذي يؤكد ما ذكرناه) for the previous argument. This is supported by Euclid's postulate that a straight line can be drawn between any two points. This implies that one point can have multiple relations (being opposite to many points) without itself being divided. The argument is used to show that multiplicity of relations or directions does not necessitate division in the substance. The passage is incomplete, as it cuts off mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is clear: just as a central point remains one while relating to many, an atom can have multiple directions without being divided.

The Third Proof: Weight Necessitates Motion, Not Intermittent Rest
Razi argues that weight's constancy prevents intermittent rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a constant cause like weight cannot produce motion intermittently, and how this refutes the idea that slow motion is due to rests.

Core Idea
A constant cause produces a constant effect; therefore weight cannot rest during fall.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi puts forward a direct philosophical argument against those who claim that the difference between fast and slow motion is due to intercalated rests. He focuses on the nature of weight: it is a continuous cause present at every moment. Using this, he shows that such a cause cannot produce motion at one time and rest at another without changing itself. The argument is simple but powerful, and it shifts the burden back to the opponents to explain slowness without rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'innama', 'fa', and 'thumma'.
This passage is a single, tightly reasoned argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into premise, reasoning, and conclusion. Notice how Razi uses the constancy of weight to reject the idea of rest.
The key is that weight remains the same in all moments.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument undermines the hypothesis that slow motion is due to rests. What alternative explanation for slowness might remain?
This shows the strength of Razi's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث : إن الثقل يوجب النزول . فإذا كان الثقل موجباً للنزول، وأنه باق في جميع الأوقات، امتنع أن يوجب الحركة في أجزاء المسافة . ثم إنه بعينه يوجب السكون في جزء آخر ، من غير تفاوت بين الجزءين البتة . فثبت : أن القول بسكونه باطل . وإذا ثبت هذا، ظهر أن التفاوت بين الحركة البطيئة والسريعة ، لا يجوز أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات
The third aspect: Indeed weight necessitates descent. Since weight is a necessitator of descent, and it remains in all times, it is impossible that it necessitate motion in some parts of the distance and then the very same thing necessitate rest in another part, without any difference between the two parts at all. So it is established that the claim of its rest is false. And when this is established, it becomes apparent that the difference between slow and fast motion cannot be due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is the third proof against the idea that slowness in motion is caused by intermittent rests. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues from the nature of weight (thiqal). He states that weight necessitates downward motion. Since weight is a continuous cause existing in all times, it cannot possibly produce motion in one part of the distance and then rest in another part without any change in the weight itself. The conclusion follows: the notion that a heavy body rests during its fall is false. Consequently, the difference between slow and fast motion cannot be attributed to the insertion of periods of rest.

Key Terms
الثقل
العجلة
البطء
السكون
الحركة
تخلل السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that weight only acts intermittently, causing motion sometimes and rest other times.
Correction: Razi shows that weight is a constant cause (baqin fi jami' al-awqat); therefore it cannot act intermittently without changing itself.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why can weight not cause motion in one part and rest in another?
A) Because weight is not a real cause.
B) Because weight is present and unchanged in all moments.
C) Because rest is caused by a different factor.
D) Because motion is only apparent.

Key Takeaway
A continuous cause like weight must produce a continuous effect; therefore, a falling body cannot experience rest between movements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you find that your core commitments (e.g., to prayer, learning, justice) are constant like weight, yet your actions towards them fluctuate wildly? If the cause is the same, what does that reveal about the 'other factors' you've allowed to intrude? Are you willing to name them today and begin removing them?
?
Razi proves that a constant cause cannot produce rest. Consider your spiritual state: you believe your iman (faith) is a constant gift from Allah. But you experience periods of 'rest'—neglect of obligations, laziness in worship. If iman is constant, what do those rests actually indicate about your own role in maintaining that constancy? What will you do this week to ensure no rest is introduced?
?
The proof concludes that slow motion cannot be due to rests. In your journey toward Allah, when you move slowly, what is your excuse? Is it a 'temporary rest' you allow yourself? This lesson declares such rests impossible for a constant cause. Will you keep the cause constant by eliminating the resting times, or will you admit that the cause itself is not as constant as you claim? Make a concrete change today.

The Millstone Argument: Proving Continuous Motion
Razi uses the example of a millstone to show that different speeds occur without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the geometry of a rotating millstone to demonstrate that variations in speed do not require intermittent rests.

Core Idea
Slowness is not absence of motion, but slower motion along a smaller circle.

Razi was a master of using everyday objects to make abstract points. In this argument, a millstone becomes a laboratory for proving that the difference between fast and slow movement is not a matter of stopping and starting. The geometry of concentric circles forced his opponents to choose between impossible outcomes—or accept his conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometric setup
Visualize a line from the center to the edge of a millstone. This line contains many points, each of which traces a circle of a different size when the stone rotates.
The closer to the center, the smaller the circle.
2
Consider the three possibilities
Razi examines three options for how the large outer circle and small inner circle could move together. Work through each one logically.
The first two lead to contradictions.
3
Identify the correct scenario
Only the third option—that the small circle moves more slowly—is coherent. See why this refutes the idea of intermittent rests.
Slowness here is due to a shorter path, not stops.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إنا إذا أخرجنا من مركز الرحى ، إلى محيطه خطاً واحداً . فإنه يجب أن يفرض في ذلك الخط نقط كثيرة. فإذا استدارت الرحى ، ارتسم من كل واحدة من تلك النقط : دائرة. وكل نقطة كانت أقرب إلى المركز . فإن الدائرة المرتسمة منها أصغر ، وكل نقطة كانت أقرب إلى المحيط، كانت الدائرة المرتسمة منها أعظم. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : إذا استدارت الرحى. فقد استدارت الدائرة، التي هي طرفي الرحى، واستدارت أيضاً الدائرة القريبة من القطب . فإما أن يقال : كلما تحركت الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً، فقد تحركت الدائرة الصغيرة جزءاً وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة الصغيرة مساوياً لمدار الدائرة العظيمة . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : إن الدائرة العظيمة تتحرك جزءاً مع أن الدائرة الصغيرة لا تتحرك البتة. وهذا محال أيضاً . لأنه يوجب وقوع التفكك بين أجزاء الرحى . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : كلما تحركت الدائرة العظيمة ، فإن الدائرة الصغيرة تتحرك حركة أبطا من حركة الدائرة العظيمة . وهذا هو الحق . وإنه يوجب القطع بأن التفاوت بين البطيء والسريع، ليس لأجل تخلل السكنات . وهو المطلوب .
The fourth aspect: If we draw a single line from the center of the millstone to its circumference, then it is necessary to posit many points on that line. When the millstone rotates, from each of those points a circle is traced: the closer a point is to the center, the smaller the circle traced from it; the closer to the circumference, the larger the circle traced from it. This being established, we say: When the millstone rotates, the circle at its edge rotates, and the circle near the axis also rotates. Either it is said: Whenever the large circle moves a part, the small circle moves a part—that is impossible, because it would necessitate that the orbit of the small circle equals the orbit of the large circle, which is impossible. Or it is said: The large circle moves a part while the small circle does not move at all—this also is impossible, because it would cause the parts of the millstone to disintegrate, which is impossible. Or it is said: Whenever the large circle moves, the small circle moves with a slower motion than the large circle. This is the truth. And it necessitates the definite conclusion that the difference between slow and fast is not due to the intercalation of rests. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof using the analogy of a rotating millstone (al-raha). The author sets up a geometric scenario: a line from the center to the rim has many points; each point traces a circle of a different size when the millstone rotates. The outer circle is larger. The author then considers three possibilities for the relationship between the motion of the large and small circles. (1) If the small circle also moves one part each time the large moves one part, then the path of the small circle would equal that of the large—impossible since the circumferences differ. (2) If the small circle does not move at all while the large moves, the millstone would break apart—impossible. (3) The correct scenario: the small circle moves more slowly than the large, without any rests. This proves that slowness is not caused by intermittent rests (takhallul al-sakanat). Thus the proof is complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slower motion involves intermittent rests (sakanat) between atomic parts.
Correction: The millstone proof shows that slowness can be continuous, with smaller paths, without any rests.

Checkpoint
In the millstone example, why is it impossible for the large and small circles to both move one part at the same time?
A) Because the millstone would break.
B) Because their orbits would then be equal in length, but they are different.
C) Because rest is necessary for slowness.
D) Because motion is only apparent.

Key Takeaway
The millstone proof demonstrates that slowness is simply motion along a shorter path, not a sequence of stops and starts, thereby refuting the atomistic theory of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a specific situation where you felt frustrated by your own slowness—perhaps in memorizing Qur'an or performing night prayers. Are you willing to accept that this slowness is not a failure of rest but a different path? What would it mean for your self-criticism if you truly believed that your small circle is still a circle of worship?
?

The Fifth Proof: Celestial Sphere and Millstone Analogy
Razi extends the millstone analogy to celestial sphere rotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rotation of the celestial sphere provides evidence that speed variation does not require intermittent stillness.

Core Idea
Speed in the heavens is continuous, not staccato.

In this lesson, we examine a simple but powerful analogy. Razi takes the familiar observation of a millstone—where parts near the center move slower than those at the rim—and applies it to the rotating celestial sphere. The conclusion is the same: differences in speed do not imply pauses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the argument.
2
Understand the analogy
Notice how the millstone's motion (rim fast, center slow) parallels the equatorial region moving faster than circles near the pole.
3
Identify the conclusion
What does this analogy prove? That slowness is not caused by stops.
The conclusion is directly stated: 'That is what was sought.'
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter for kalam? It challenges the Ash'ari occasionalist view that all motion involves discrete atomic moments.

Source Text

الوجه الخامس : وهو أن الذي ذكرناه في حركة الرحى ، نذكره في استدارة الفلك . ويلزم أنه كلما تحركت المنطقة ، فإن الدائرة القريبة من القطب ، قد تحركت بحركة أبطأ منها وذلك هو المطلوب
The fifth aspect: That which we mentioned regarding the motion of the millstone, we mention it regarding the rotation of the celestial sphere. And it follows that whenever the equator moves, the circle near the pole moves with a motion slower than it. That is what was sought.

This passage is a brief extension of the previous argument. The author states that the same reasoning used for the millstone applies to the rotation of the celestial sphere (astidarat al-falak). In the rotating heavens, the equatorial region (al-mintaqa) moves faster than the circles near the pole. This difference in speed is not due to rests inserted along the path, but is a natural consequence of the geometry. The conclusion is the same: slowness is not caused by intermittent stillness.

The Compass Argument for Continuous Motion
Razi uses a three-legged compass to demonstrate proportional speeds without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a three-legged compass to demonstrate that proportional speeds can exist without any intervening rests.

Core Idea
Proportional motion can occur continuously without any stops.

Razi now introduces a geometric example to illustrate a key point about motion: slowness does not require a body to pause. By imagining a compass with three legs at different distances from the center, he shows that the outer leg moves faster simply because it travels a longer path in the same time. This argument reinforces the earlier millstone and celestial sphere analogies, grounding the discussion in a simple mechanical model.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Try to visualize the three-legged compass.
2
Understand the compass setup
Identify the three legs: one at the center, one on a circle of 50 parts, and one on a circle of 100 parts. This is a single rigid compass rotating about the center.
3
Grasp the proportional motion
When the outer leg moves two parts, the middle leg moves one part. This is because their distances from the center are in a 2:1 ratio. The motion is continuous – no rests happen.
This directly refutes the idea that slowness requires stopping.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Razi mentions this is analogous to the millstone and celestial sphere examples. Understand that all these proofs aim to show that varying speeds can occur without any periods of rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : وهو أنا إذا فرضنا فرجاراً، له شعب ثلاثة . فوضعنا شعبة منه على مركز الدائرة، والشعبة الثانية على دائرة مدارها خمسون جزءاً، والشعبة الثالثة على دائرة مدارها مائة جزء . فعندما تتحرك الشعبة الثالثة المحيطة جزءين، وجب أن تتحرك الشعبة المتوسطة جزءاً واحداً . على قياس ما ذكرنا في الرحى وفي الفلك . وذلك يوجب حصول البطء . لا بسبب تخلل السكنات
The sixth aspect: If we posit a compass having three legs, and we place one leg on the center of the circle, the second leg on a circle whose orbit is fifty parts, and the third leg on a circle whose orbit is one hundred parts, then when the third outer leg moves two parts, it is necessary that the middle leg moves one part, in the same way as we mentioned regarding the millstone and the celestial sphere. That necessitates the occurrence of slowness, not due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents a geometric proof using a compass (firjar) with three legs. The legs are placed at different distances from the center: one at the center, one on a circle of radius 50, and one on a circle of radius 100. As the third (outer) leg moves a certain distance, say two parts, the middle leg moves exactly one part. The ratio is proportional to their radii, consistent with continuous rotation. This demonstrates that slowness results from a smaller path length per unit time, not from stopping. The author explicitly refers back to the millstone and celestial sphere examples, showing the unifying principle.

Correction: Razi's compass shows that slowness can be a result of covering a lesser distance in the same time while motion remains continuous, as seen in the proportional arcs of the compass legs.

The Shadow Argument: Continuous Motion Without Rests
Razi analyzes three possibilities for shadow shortening to prove continuous motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the changing length of a shadow proves that motion is continuous and that differences in speed are not due to intermittent rests.

Core Idea
Shadows never jump — continuous change proves continuous motion.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a clever empirical argument using a simple phenomenon: the shortening of a shadow as the sun rises. By analyzing the possible relationships between the sun's rise and the shadow's decrease, he eliminates two absurd options and establishes that motion is continuous, without any pauses or rests.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Imagine a vertical stick planted in the ground at sunrise. Its shadow is longest at dawn and shortens as the sun rises. This is the physical situation Razi asks us to consider.
The shadow length decreases continuously as the sun climbs.
2
Consider three possibilities
Razi lists three options for how the shadow's length changes relative to the sun's rise. Read the Arabic passage and identify each option in order.
The options are: (1) equal decrease, (2) no decrease, (3) decrease by less than the sun's rise.
3
Refute the first two
The first option (equal units) leads to an absurd consequence—the shadow would be as long as the celestial sphere. The second option (no change) is contradicted by observation. Understand why each is impossible.
Option 1 is absurd because it implies the shadow's length equals the celestial orbit; option 2 contradicts what we see.
4
Accept the third and draw the conclusion
The third option (shadow decreases by less than a unit) is the only plausible one. From this, Razi deduces that speed differences are not due to 'intercalation of rests'—meaning motion is continuous.
This conclusion refutes the idea that motion occurs in discrete jumps with rests in between.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : إنا إذا غرزنا خشبة في الأرض . فإذا طلعت الشمس وقع لها ظل على الأرض، ثم كلما ازداد ارتفاع الشمس، انتقص طول الظل . فإما أن يقال : كلما ارتفعت الشمس جزءاً، انتقص من الظل جزء . وهو محال . لأنه يلزم أن يكون طول الظل، مساوياً لمدار الفلك . وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : قد ترتفع الشمس جزءاً، مع أن الظل يبقى بحاله، ولا ينتقص منه شيء . وذلك محال . وإما أن يقال : كلما ارتفعت الشمس جزءاً، انتقص من الظل أقل من جزء . وذلك يوجب القطع بأن التفاوت بين السريع والبطيء : لا يكون بسبب تخلل السكنات
The seventh aspect: If we plant a stick in the ground, when the sun rises it casts a shadow on the ground, and then whenever the sun's altitude increases, the shadow's length decreases. Either it is said: Whenever the sun rises by a part, the shadow decreases by a part—that is impossible, because it would necessitate that the shadow's length equal the celestial sphere's orbit, which is absurd. Or it is said: The sun may rise by a part while the shadow remains as it was, with no decrease—that is also impossible. Or it is said: Whenever the sun rises by a part, the shadow decreases by less than a part. And that necessitates the definite conclusion that the difference between fast and slow is not due to the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage offers a seventh proof based on the lengthening and shortening of shadows. A stick (khashaba) is planted in the ground. As the sun rises, its shadow shortens. The author considers three logical possibilities. (1) If for every unit increase in the sun's altitude, the shadow decreases by an equal amount, then the total shadow length would equal the celestial orbit—impossible. (2) If the shadow does not decrease at all when the sun rises, that is also impossible because we observe it shortening. (3) The correct option is that the shadow decreases by less than one unit per unit of solar altitude. This shows continuous change without instantaneous jumps or rests. Therefore, speed differences are not caused by intermittent stillness.

Key Terms
خشبة
ظل
ارتفاع الشمس
نقصان
تخلل السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that shadows shorten in discrete jumps or that motion occurs with intermittent pauses.
Correction: Razi shows that the shadow's shortening is continuous; if it paused, the second option would be true, but it is not. Thus, motion is smooth without rests.

Checkpoint
Why is the first option in the shadow argument (shadow decreases by a full part per part of sun rise) impossible?
A) Because the shadow would not exist.
B) Because it would imply the shadow is as long as the celestial orbit.
C) Because the sun does not rise continuously.
D) Because the shadow would increase instead.

Key Takeaway
The shadow argument demonstrates that motion is continuous and that differences in speed cannot be attributed to intermittent pauses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every change in your life—every decrease in bad habits and increase in good deeds—is continuous without pause, how do you justify moments where you feel spiritually 'stuck'? Are those rests real, or are you deceiving yourself into complacency?

Razi's argument shows that the shadow never halts in its shortening. Where in your journey toward Allah have you allowed yourself to believe that 'stopping' is natural, when in fact the universe operates on constant motion? Commit today to one area where you will stop allowing rests.
?
The proof forces you to see that your movement toward good or away from evil is never at a standstill. Is your soul's shadow lengthening or shortening right now? Do you dare to examine the rate of change, or are you comfortable with the illusion of stability?

The Eighth Proof: Slow Walking and Continuous Motion
Razi presents a proof from the experience of slow walking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the experience of slow walking to argue that slowness is continuous motion of lesser speed, not a mixture of motion and rest, and why this refutes the atomistic view.

Core Idea
Slow walking proves slowness is continuous, not a series of stops.

This lesson examines Razi's eighth proof against the Ash'ari atomistic theory of motion. The proof is simple but profound: when you walk slowly, do you consciously decide to stop and start repeatedly? No. Yet if slowness were made of rests between motions, you would need to intend each stop. Since you don't, slowness must be a single continuous motion of reduced speed. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice Razi's conditional structure: 'if slowness were... then... but...'
The argument is compact; understanding the conditional is key.
2
Identify the conditional assumption
Find the sentence starting with 'falaw kana al-but'u...' (if slowness were). Understand what Razi assumes about the definition of slowness and what consequence would follow.
The consequence is that the walker would be voluntarily doing both motion and rest.
3
Reflect on the key insight
Razi states that voluntary acts require intention and choice (qasd wa ikhtiyar). When you walk slowly, do you form a conscious intention to pause at each point? The argument says no. How does this break the assumption?
This is the crux: the absence of intention for rests disproves the definition.
4
Formulate the conclusion
Based on the argument, what must be the correct understanding of slowness? Write your own one-sentence conclusion.
The conclusion should be that slowness is a single continuous motion of lesser speed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : إن الإنسان العاقل، قد يمشي مشياً بطيئاً . فلو كان البطء عبارة عن كونه ساكناً في بعض الأحياز، ومتحركاً في بعضها . فمن المعلوم أن تلك الحركة البطيئة عبارة عن الحركات المختلطة بالسكونات، لكان ذلك الإنسان قد فعل باختياره في بعض الأحياز حركة، وفي بعضها سكوناً . لكن من المعلوم : أن الفعل الاختياري لا يحصل إلا بالقصد والاختيار .
The eighth aspect: A rational person may walk with a slow walk. If slowness were defined as him being at rest in some places and moving in others, then it would be known that such slow motion consists of movements mixed with rests. Then that person would have done by his choice motion in some places and rest in others. But it is known that a voluntary act occurs only with intention and choice.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof from human voluntary action. A rational person can walk slowly. If slowness were essentially the insertion of rests between movements, then the walker would be deliberately choosing to intersperse periods of rest with motion. However, voluntary acts require conscious intention (qasd) and choice (ikhtiyar). When someone walks slowly, they do not consciously decide to stop and start repeatedly; the motion feels continuous, albeit slower. Thus slowness is a continuous motion of lesser speed, not a sequence of moves and pauses. The proof appeals to the psychological experience of slow walking.

Key Terms
الإنسان العاقل
المشي البطيء
السكون
الحركة
الفعل الاختياري
القصد والاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think slow walking involves micro-pauses (rests) that are too small to notice.
Correction: Razi argues that if those pauses existed, they would be voluntary acts requiring intention, but the walker does not intend such pauses, so they do not occur.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, why can't slow walking be defined as motion mixed with rests?
A) Because walking is always fast.
B) Because voluntary acts need intention, and slow walkers do not consciously intend to rest.
C) Because rest is impossible during walking.
D) Because only animals walk slowly.

Key Takeaway
Slowness is continuous motion, not an alternation of motion and rest, because voluntary rests would require intention which is absent in slow walking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something slowly—like walking, reading, or memorizing—do you ever become aware of moments where you assume you have ‘stopped’ and then restarted? How does Razi’s proof challenge that assumption about the continuity of your actions? Are you willing to see your own acts as a single, continuous movement rather than a series of fragments?
?
Consider an area of your spiritual life where you feel you are progressing slowly (e.g., gaining patience, controlling anger, or drawing closer to God). Have you been treating that slowness as a series of starts and stops—successful moments followed by failures? What would change if you believed that God’s creation of your progress is continuous, not punctuated by pauses? How would that affect your hope and perseverance?
?
Razi argues that voluntary acts require intention and choice. Think of a habit you do automatically, like a way of speaking or reacting. Is it truly voluntary? If not, what would it take for you to bring that habit under your conscious intention and choice—and is it worth the effort?

Infinite Divisibility of Space and Time
Concludes the argument that space and time are infinitely divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationship between fast and slow motion demonstrates the infinite divisibility of space and time.

Core Idea
Speed differences reveal that both distance and time can be divided endlessly.

This passage concludes the argument for the infinite divisibility of space and time. It uses a simple but powerful observation: when a fast and a slow object cover the same distance, the fast one takes less time. This difference implies that time can be divided, and by a similar logic, distance can be divided as well. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The passage is the conclusion of a longer argument, so it assumes some prior context.
2
Identify the key relationship
The argument uses the scenario where two objects of different speeds cover the same distance. The faster object takes less time, which shows that time can be divided.
This is the first explicit inference: from equality of distance and difference in speed, we deduce time division.
3
Symmetrical reasoning for distance
The author states 'by this way' that distance is also always divisible. This implies a symmetrical argument: if two objects travel for the same time, the faster covers more distance, so distance divides.
The passage does not spell out the symmetrical case explicitly, but it is implied.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that both space and time are infinitely divisible ('always divisible'). This establishes the desired point of the argument.
The phrase 'وهو المطلوب' indicates that this is the intended conclusion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it refutes the idea of indivisible atoms of space or time. If division were finite, speed differences couldn't exist in the way described.
This connects to broader theological and philosophical discussions about the nature of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطيء أقل ، فتنقسم المسافة . وأيضاً : السريع والبطيء إذا تساويا في المسافة ، كان زمان السريع أقل ، فينقسم الزمان . وبهذا الطريق يظهر أن المسافة قابلة للقسمة أبداً وأن الزمان قابل للقسمة أبداً وهو المطلوب فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الحجة
The slow is less, so the distance divides. Also: the fast and slow when equal in distance, the time of the fast is less, so the time divides. And by this way it appears that distance is always divisible and that time is always divisible, which is what is sought. So this completes the discussion in establishing this argument.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of a proof for the infinite divisibility of space and time. The author uses the relationship between fast and slow motion: when two objects cover the same distance, the faster one takes less time. This shows that both distance and time can be divided repeatedly. The argument is presented as a concise syllogism: if speed differs, the slower takes more time, implying division of time; conversely, if time differs, the distance must also be divisible. The author ends by stating that this completes the exposition of this particular proof.

The infinite divisibility of space and time is demonstrated by the difference in time when two objects cover the same distance at different speeds.
