Slowness Caused by Intervening Rests: The Mutakallimun's Proof
Proof that slowness requires intervening rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that slowness in motion is necessarily caused by intervening rests, and why they reject gradual entry into existence.

Core Idea
Slowness is caused by rests between instantaneous atomic motions.

The mutakallimun, the classical Islamic theologians, developed a rigorous atomistic physics to explain motion. In this passage, they present their proof that slowness in motion cannot be a gradual slowing down but must arise from intervals of rest between discrete atomic movements. Understanding this argument is key to grasping their broader metaphysical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the clear structure: claim, definition, dichotomy, proof, cases.
2
Understand the definition of motion
The mutakallimun define motion as a series of discrete 'occurrences' (حصولات) in contiguous spaces. This atomistic view is crucial.
There is no continuous motion; it is a succession of instantaneous states.
3
Follow the dichotomy
Slowness either occurs at the moment a thing enters existence or after. The first is ruled out because entry is instantaneous, not gradual.
The key point: a single thing cannot enter existence gradually.
4
Identify the two after-entry cases
After entry, if the thing remains, that is rest (سكون), causing slowness. If it perishes instantly and is replaced, that is maximal speed, no slowness.
Thus any variation from maximal speed implies rests.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The proof establishes that any motion slower than the fastest must involve intervening rests. The alternative is a gradualist view which is rejected.
The author then promises to answer objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون : البطء لا يمكن حصوله إلا لأجل تخلل السكنات. والدليل عليه : إنا بينا بالدلائل الكثيرة القاهرة : أنه لا معنى للحركة إلا حصولات متعاقبة ، في أحياز متلاصقة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : البطء . إما أن يحصل حال دخول ذلك الشيء في الوجود ، وإما أن يحصل بعد دخوله في الوجود . والأول باطل . لأنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة في بيان أن الشيء الواحد ، يمتنع أن يدخل في الوجود ، على سبيل المهلة والتدريج . بل إنما يدخل في الوجود دفعة واحدة. وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع حصول البطء والسرعة في هذه الحالة ، وإذا بطل هذا ، بقي أن البطء والسرعة إنما يحصل بعد دخول الشيء في الوجود . فإنه إن بقي ذلك الشيء بعد دخوله في الوجود ، فذلك هو السكون . فيكون حصول البطء لأجل حصول ذلك السكون . وإن كان ذلك الشيء بعد دخوله في الوجود : لا يبقى ، بل يغني دفعة واحدة ، ويحدث بعده شيء آخر ، بشرط أن يكون حدوثه أيضاً دفعة . فذلك هو السريع ، الذي لا يعقل وجود سريع أسرع منه ، فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قول من يقول : إنه توجد حركة أسرع من حركة ، لا لاجل تخلل السكنات : مفرع على قول من يقول : إن الحركة عبارة عن الحدوث على سبيل التدريج ولما ثبت فساد هذا ، يجب أن نجيب عن الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها
The mutakallimun said: Slowness cannot occur except due to the interposition of rests. The proof of it: We have explained with many overwhelming proofs that there is no meaning of motion except successive occurrences in contiguous spaces. And when this is established, we say: Slowness either occurs at the moment of that thing's entry into existence, or after its entry into existence. The first is invalid, for we have mentioned many proofs in the explanation that a single thing cannot enter into existence gradually and by degree, but rather it enters into existence all at once. And if this is so, it is impossible for slowness and speed to occur in this state. And when this is invalidated, it remains that slowness and speed only occur after the thing enters into existence. For if that thing remains after its entry into existence, that is rest. Thus slowness occurs due to the occurrence of that rest. And if that thing after its entry into existence does not remain, but perishes all at once, and another thing occurs after it, with the condition that its occurrence is also all at once, then that is the fast, than which no faster fast can be conceived. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement of whoever says that there exists a motion faster than another motion not due to the interposition of rests is based upon the statement of whoever says that motion is expression of occurrence gradually. And when the corruption of this has been established, we must answer the arguments they have clung to.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a proof by the mutakallimun (theologians) that slowness in motion is always due to intervening rests (sakanat). They begin with their definition of motion as a series of discrete occurrences in contiguous spaces (atoms of motion). Then they consider slowness: it can only occur either during a thing's coming into existence or after. The first is impossible because a thing comes into existence instantaneously, not gradually. Therefore slowness must occur after existence. If the thing persists after coming into existence, that persistence is rest, and slowness arises from that rest. If it does not persist but is immediately replaced by another discrete entity, that is maximal speed. Therefore any variation in speed must be due to rests. The author then concludes that those who deny rests are committed to a gradualist view of motion, which is invalid. The passage ends with the necessity of answering their objections.

Key Terms
بطء
سرعة
حركة
سكون
سكنات
تخلل
حصولات متعاقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness in motion can result from the thing entering existence gradually or from a slower pace of atomic motion without rests.
Correction: The mutakallimun hold that entry into existence is instantaneous, so any slowness must come from rests between atomic motions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Slowness in motion is necessarily caused by intervening rests between instantaneous atomic motions; there is no gradual motion.

Answer to the First Objection: The Horse Example
Refutes the objection that fast motions would be hidden by numerous rests by distinguishing existents from non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that numerous rests would hide fast motions by distinguishing between existent attributes (motions) and non-existents (rests), and why the color mixing analogy is invalid.

Core Idea
Rests are non-existents; non-existents are imperceptible, so they cannot hide motions.

This passage presents the author's response to the first objection against the theory that slowness is due to intervening rests. The objector uses the example of a fast horse to argue that if rests are many, motions would be invisible. The author concedes that motions are fewer than rests but denies that this prevents visibility, because rests are non-existents and thus not perceived. This refutation hinges on the key distinction between existent and non-existent attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the objector's argument
Locate the objector's claim: if slowness is due to rests, then fast motions would be hidden by many rests.
Look for 'لو كان بطء الحركات... لوجب أن لا تظهر...'
3
Note the author's concession and rebuttal
The author concedes motions are fewer than rests but rebuts that rests are non-existents and imperceptible.
Key phrase: 'الحركات صفات موجودة... السكنات عدم الحركات'
4
Understand the color analogy difference
Compare with mixing black and white: both colors are existent and perceptible, so they can mix and hide. Rests are non-existent, so they do not hide.
Contrast: 'هذا بخلاف ما إذا اختلطت الأجزاء الموصوفة بالبياض...'
5
Summarize the argument
In your own words, explain why the objection fails using the distinction between existent and non-existent.
Use the key terms: حركات, سكنات, وجود, عدم, محسوس

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الوجه الأول : وهو قوله : « لو كان بطء الحركات ، لأجل تخلل السكنات ، لوجب أن تكون حركات الفرس الذي يكون شديد العدو ، أقل من سكناته بكثير، فنقول : هذا مسلم . لا نزاع فيه . أما قوله : « لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن لا تظهر تلك الحركات الكثيرة ، فيما بين تلك السكنات ، فنقول : هذا غير مسلم . وذلك لأن الحركات صفات موجودة . وأما السكنات فإنها عبارة عن عدم الحركات والاعدام لا تكون محسوسة ولا مرئية البتة. وعلى هذا التقدير، فإن الحركات مرئية محوسة ، والكنات غير محسوسة البتة . فلم يلزم ما ذكرتموه . وهذا بخلاف ما إذا اختلطت الأجزاء الموصوفة بالبياض ، بالأجزاء الموصوفة بالواد، وكان الغالب هو الأجزاء الموصوفة بالسواد . وذلك لأن في هذه الصورة : كلا اللونين أعني السواد والبياض محسوساً فيلزم ما ذكرتم . أما ههنا فالحركة محسوسة . إما بالذات وإما بالعرض. وأما السكون فإنه غير محسوس . فظهر الفرق
As for the first argument: which is his saying: 'If the slowness of motions were due to the interposition of rests, then the motions of a horse that is very fast would necessarily be much fewer than its rests.' We say: This is conceded; there is no dispute about it. As for his saying: 'If this were the case, then those many motions would necessarily not appear among those rests.' We say: This is not conceded. That is because motions are existing attributes. As for rests, they are expressions of non-existence of motions, and non-existents are not sensibly perceived nor seen at all. On this assumption, motions are visible and perceptible, while rests are not perceptible at all. So what you mentioned does not follow. This is different from the case when parts described by whiteness are mixed with parts described by blackness, and the prevailing is the parts described by blackness. That is because in this case, both colors, whiteness and blackness, are perceptible, so what you mentioned follows. But here, motion is perceptible either essentially or accidentally. As for rest, it is not perceptible. So the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the first objection against the theory that slowness is due to intervening rests. The objector argues that if a fast horse's motions are few and rests many, the motions would not be visible. The author concedes that motions are fewer than rests, but denies that this prevents visibility. The key point: motions are existing attributes, hence perceptible, while rests are non-existents (ʿadam) and thus not perceived. The analogy with mixing colors is rejected because both colors are existent and perceptible, whereas rests are not. Therefore the objection fails.

Answer to the Second Objection: The Heavy Body Example
Refutes the objection that increased weight increases speed without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that there is a maximum speed for falling bodies, beyond which additional weight does not increase speed.

Core Idea
Speed has a maximum; beyond it, more weight adds nothing.

In this passage, the author addresses a second objection against his theory of motion. The objector claims that if a heavy body falls without any intervening rests, increasing its weight should increase its speed. This seems to challenge the idea that speed variation is due to rests. The author responds by introducing a crucial limit: speed cannot increase indefinitely. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector presents a scenario where a heavy body falls without any rests (sakanat). They argue that increasing the weight must increase speed, proving speed variation can occur without rests.
The objection is quoted in the passage.
2
Identify the author's key claim
The author responds that a cause (muqtadi) only produces its effect when the effect is possible to obtain. Speed has a maximum limit; beyond that, extra weight has no effect.
This is the core rebuttal.
3
Understand the rebuttal's logic
The author challenges the objector to prove that speed can increase forever. If they cannot, the objection fails because it assumes an unlimited capacity for speed.
The burden of proof shifts to the objector.
4
Consider the counter-argument
The author anticipates a counter-argument: if the weight is already enough for maximum speed, adding more weight either has no effect (impossible because weight is effective) or leads to a paradox (the body not falling).
The passage ends here, but the reasoning is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قولهم : ( إن الجسم إذا بلغ في الثقل ، بحيث صارت [ حركاته (۲) ] عند الهوتى خالية عن السكنات ، فإذا ازدادا ثقله ، وجب أن تزداد سرعة حركاته. فقد حصل التفاوت ههنا في السرعة والبطء ، لا لتخلل السكنات ، فالجواب عنه : إن المقتضي إنما يعمل عمله إذا كان الأثر ممكن الحصول . فإذا بلغت الحركة في السرعة إلى حد معين ، لا يمكن الزيادة عليه . لم يلزم من ازدياد الثقل، ازدياد السرعة. فإن بينتم أن السرعة تقبل الزيادة أبداً ، حصل مقصودكم . إلا أن هذا هو أول المسألة لا يقال : الدليل عليه : وهو أن الثقل الوافي بإيجاب السرعة الخالصة في الحركة تام لحصول تلك السرعة . فإذا جعل ذلك الجسم أثقل مما كان ، فهذا القدر الزائد من الثقل . لو انفرد لكان مستقلاً باقتضاء لا لمؤثر . فهو أيضاً محال . لأن على التقدير الأول يلزم أن يقال : لما جعل الجسم الثقيل ، أثقل) مما كان . فإنه لا ينزل ولا يهوي. وذلك محال . وعلى هذا التقدير الثاني ، يلزم
As for the second argument: which is their saying: 'When a body reaches a weight such that its motions in falling become free of rests, then when its weight increases, the speed of its motions must increase. So the variation here in speed and slowness occurs not due to the interposition of rests.' The answer to it: The efficient cause only does its work when the effect is possible to obtain. When motion reaches a certain limit of speed beyond which increase is not possible, increase in weight does not necessitate increase in speed. If you demonstrate that speed accepts increase forever, then you have achieved your aim; except that this is the very beginning of the issue. It cannot be said: The proof of it: The weight sufficient to necessitate pure speed in motion is complete for the occurrence of that speed. So when that body is made heavier than it was, this excess weight, if considered alone, would be independent in necessitating no effect. That is also impossible. Because on the first assumption, it would be said: when the heavy body is made heavier than it was, it does not descend or fall. That is impossible. And on this second assumption, it necessitates...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection: that a heavy body falling without rests can still increase speed when weight increases, so speed variation is not due to rests. The author responds that a cause operates only when the effect is possible. Speed has an upper limit; beyond that, more weight does not produce more speed. The objector would need to prove that speed has no maximum—which is exactly the point in dispute. The author then anticipates a counter-argument: if the weight is enough to produce maximum speed, then adding weight should either have no effect (if speed is at maximum) or cause a paradox (the body not falling). The passage ends incomplete, but the reasoning is clear.

The Philosophers' First Argument and Its Three Conclusions
Philosophers' first argument deriving three conclusions about time, space, and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers derive three conclusions about time, distance, and motion from a simple scenario of moving parts, and why these conclusions are important for their cosmology.

Core Idea
A single motion scenario yields three radical claims about time, space, and rest.

The philosophers use a thought experiment involving two parts moving in different numbers of spaces to argue for fundamental properties of the physical world. This passage presents their first argument, which leads to three striking conclusions about time, distance, and motion. Pay close attention to how they reason from one simple premise to multiple implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Imagine one part (lower) moves in one space while another part (upper) moves in two spaces in the same time. Visualize this motion.
The scenario is a thought experiment, not an actual observation.
2
Contrast the leap proponents' view
Those who affirm the leap (tumra) see this scenario as evidence for their theory. The philosophers, however, use it to derive something different.
The leap theory involves discontinuous motion through an intervening space.
3
Examine the first conclusion
Time must be infinitely divisible: because in the same time the upper part covers two spaces, so the time is split into two parts corresponding to each space.
This challenges the idea of an indivisible atom of time.
4
Examine the second conclusion
Distance must be infinitely divisible: since time is divisible, the motion in half the time covers half the distance, dividing the distance without end.
Space is thus continuous, not composed of indivisible points.
5
Examine the third conclusion
Two motions can exist without any admixture of rest, yet one faster than the other. Both the lower and upper motions are continuous and pure.
This shows that speed difference does not require intervening rests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد تحرك في حيزين، حال ما تحرك الجزء السفلاني في حيز واحد. وعند هذا قال القائلون بالطفرة: إن هذا يدل على القول بالطفرة. وأما الفلاسفة: فإنهم احتجوا بهذا الكلام على إثبات أمور. أحدهما: إن هذا يدل على أن الزمان قابل للقسمة أبداً، وذلك لأن في الزمان الذي تحرك الجزء التحتاني في حيز واحد، فقد تحرك الجزء الفوقاني في جزءين، فيكون ذلك الزمان منقسماً إلى قسمين. وثانيها: إن هذا يدل على أن المسافة قابلة للقسمة أبداً. وذلك لأن الزمان لما كان منقسماً، وقد تحرك فيه الجزء السفلاني، في حيز واحد. فالواقع في نصف ذلك الزمان، هو الحركة على نصف تلك المسافة. فيلزم كون تلك المسافة منقسمة. وثالثها: إن هذا يدل على أنه قد توجد حركتان خاليتان عن مخالطة السكنات، مع أنه تكون إحداهما أسرع من الأخرى. لأن ههنا حركة الجزء التحتاني، خالية عن مخالطة السكنات. وكذلك أيضاً حركة الجزء الفوقاني خالية عن مخالطة السكنات، وأن حركة الجزء الفوقاني أسرع من حركة الجزء التحتاني. فهذه الحجة أفادت هذه المطالب الثلاثة.
It moved in two spaces while the lower part moved in one space. At this, those who affirm the leap said: This indicates the affirmation of the leap. As for the philosophers, they used this statement to establish things. First: This indicates that time is always divisible, because in the time in which the lower part moved in one space, the upper part moved in two parts, so that time is divided into two parts. Second: This indicates that distance is always divisible, because since time is divided, and the lower part moved in one space in it, what occurs in half of that time is motion over half of that distance, so it follows that that distance is divisible. Third: This indicates that there can exist two motions free from the admixture of rests, while one of them is faster than the other, because here the motion of the lower part is free from the admixture of rests, and likewise the motion of the upper part is free from the admixture of rests, and that the motion of the upper part is faster than the motion of the lower part. So this argument yielded these three demands.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-hujja) used by the philosophers (falasifa) to derive three conclusions from a simple scenario of motion. The scenario: one part (the lower) moves in one space (hiz) while the other (the upper) moves in two spaces. The proponents of the leap (tumra) use this to support their theory, but the philosophers use the same premise to argue for three things: (1) time is infinitely divisible, because the same time period in which the lower part covers one space sees the upper part covering two spaces, implying that time can be split into two subintervals; (2) distance is infinitely divisible, because the time interval's divisibility implies that the distance covered in half that time is half the original distance, so distance too is divisible without end; (3) there exist two motions each pure (without any admixture of rest) yet one faster than the other—the upper part's motion is faster but both are continuous, unbroken motions. The passage ends by stating that this single argument yielded all three conclusions.

Key Terms
طفرة
حيز
زمان
مسافة
سكنات
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument presupposes the leap theory.
Correction: The philosophers use the same scenario but derive conclusions about divisibility, not the leap; the leap proponents are a different group.

Checkpoint

A) The leap, indivisible time, and indivisible distance.
B) Divisibility of time, divisibility of distance, and existence of two motions without rest.
C) The leap, the existence of vacuum, and the speed of light.
D) That motion is impossible, that rest is necessary, and that time is finite.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first argument uses a simple motion scenario to argue for the infinite divisibility of time and space and the existence of motions without rest.

Second Argument: Line Moving Right and Left
A geometric argument about a line moving right while its upper part moves left, yielding the same three conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second geometric argument demonstrates the same three conclusions about the divisibility of space, time, and motion without requiring a leap.

Core Idea
Motion without rest yields divisibility of space and time.

This passage presents a second geometric argument that reinforces the conclusions of the first. A line moves to the right while its upper part moves left, forcing a conclusion about which point it contacts. This argument, like the first, establishes the three necessary demands without relying on a leap.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the setup of the line, points Alif, Ba, Jim, and the movement.
The Arabic uses technical terms like مماسة (contact) and ملاقاة (adjacent).
2
Understand the initial condition and movement
Note that the whole line moves to the right, but the upper part is forced to move left. This sets up a conflict that reveals something about the continuity of motion.
The upper part moves contrary to the lower line.
3
Consider the two possibilities for the upper part
After moving, the upper part must become adjacent either to Ba or to Jim. These are the only logical possibilities given the geometry.
Ba and Jim are points on the line.
4
Analyze why adjacency to Ba is rejected
If the upper part became adjacent to Ba, that would mean Ba occupied the place of Alif, so the upper part did not actually move—contradicting the assumption that it moved opposite to the lower line.
This rejection forces the other possibility.
5
Conclude the three demands
Since the upper part becomes adjacent to Jim, it moves from part 1 to part 3 while the lower part moves from part 1 to part 2. This proves divisibility of space, time, and two motions without rest, one faster than the other.
Compare these conclusions with the first argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: ليكن الخط المفروض بحالته الأولى، وعلى صفته المذكورة. إلا أنه عندما تحركت كلية الخط إلى الجانب الأيمن، تحرك الجزء الفوقاني إلى الجانب الأيسر. فنقول: إن الجزء الفوقاني لما انتقل من مماسة الألف إلى الجانب الثاني، فإما أن يصير ملاقياً للباء أو للجيم. والأول باطل. لأن على هذا التقدير، قد دخل الباء في مكان الألف. فالجزء الفوقاني لو بقي ملاقياً للباء، لزم أن يقال: إنه لم يتحرك البتة. لكنا قد فرضناه متحركاً على مضادة حركة الخط الأسفل. ولما بطل هذا، ثبت: أن الجزء الفوقاني يصير عند هذا الفرض ملاقياً للجيم. فالجزء الفوقاني قد انتقل من الجزء الأول إلى الجزء الثالث، حال ما انتقل الجزء التحتاني من الجزء الأول إلى الجزء الثاني. وحينئذ تحصل المطالب الثلاثة المذكورة في الوجه الأول.
The second argument: We say: Let the assumed line be in its first state, and in its described condition. Except that when the whole line moved to the right side, the upper part moved to the left side. We say: When the upper part moved from contact with Alif to the second side, either it becomes adjacent to Ba or to Jim. The first is false, because on this supposition, Ba has entered the place of Alif. If the upper part remained adjacent to Ba, it would necessarily be said that it did not move at all. But we assumed it moving contrary to the motion of the lower line. Since this is false, it is established that the upper part in this hypothesis becomes adjacent to Jim. So the upper part moved from the first part to the third part, while the lower part moved from the first part to the second part. Then the three demands mentioned in the first approach are obtained.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides a second argument (al-hujja al-thāniya) that employs a geometric scenario: a line (khatt) initially positioned in a certain way, with parts labelled Alif, Ba, Jim (likely points on the line). The whole line moves to the right, but the upper segment moves left (against the motion of the lower segment). The argument examines the possible positions of the upper part relative to points on the line. It considers whether the upper part ends up adjacent to Ba or to Jim. The first possibility is ruled out because if it were adjacent to Ba, that would mean Ba occupies the spot where Alif was, and then the upper part would not have moved—contradicting the assumption that it moved counter to the lower line. Therefore, the upper part must become adjacent to Jim, meaning it skipped a portion (the second part) and moved from part 1 to part 3, while the lower part moved from part 1 to part 2. This again yields the three same demands (al-matalib al-thalatha) as the first argument: divisibility of time, divisibility of space, and two motions without rest, one faster than the other.

Correction: The argument does not require a leap; it logically deduces that the upper part skips the second part due to the impossibility of remaining adjacent to Ba, thus proving the three conclusions without postulating a leap.

Key Takeaway
The second geometric argument shows that without a leap, a line's parts moving contrarily force the conclusion that motion implies discrete space and time parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Third Argument: The Well and Pulley Example
An argument using a well and pulley to illustrate leap or faster motion without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the well and pulley thought experiment demonstrates either the concept of leap (al-tafra) or the possibility of motion with different speeds without intermediate rests.

Core Idea
A simple pulley system can reveal motion without rests or even a leap.

This passage presents a clever thought experiment from the Kalam tradition. By examining a well with a block, ropes, bucket, and pulley, the author illustrates a key debate about the nature of motion: does it occur in leaps or continuous speeds? The example is designed to show that either one motion is faster than the other without any rests, or that leaps occur. Let's work through the setup step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Visualize the well of 100 cubits deep, a block at the midpoint, a rope of 50 cubits hanging from it with a bucket at the end. Another rope of 50 cubits from the top with a pulley at its end.
The block (khashaba) acts as a support point.
2
Follow the action
When the pulley on the second rope is attached to the first rope and pulled to the top, the bucket rises from bottom to top in the same time the pulley rises from mid-depth to top.
3
Grasp the implication
The bucket covers twice the distance in the same time. This implies either a leap (the bucket instantly covers the extra distance) or that the bucket moves faster without any rests.
The author presents both possibilities neutrally.
4
Examine the halving argument
The passage then extends the idea: starting with a 64-cubit well, repeatedly halve the upper portion with blocks and ropes, reducing the depth until one cubit, maintaining the same logic.
This iterative process emphasizes the same point at smaller scales.
5
Note the cutoff
The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear: the argument aims to show that the speed difference or leap holds even at the smallest scale.
The incomplete ending is due to the source page cutoff.

Source Text

الحجة الثالثة: إن البئر الذي عمقها مائة ذراع، إذا كان في منتصفها خشبة، وعلق عليها حبل مقداره خمسون ذراعاً، وعلق بالطرف الآخر من الحبل دلو. فإذا أرسلنا حبلاً آخر مقداره خمسون ذراعاً من رأس البئر، وشددنا بالطرف الثاني من هذا الحبل الثاني معلاقاً. فإذا علقنا ذلك المعلاق على طرف الحبل الأول، ثم جررناه إلى رأس البئر فإن الدلو ينتهي من أسفل البئر إلى أعلاه في الزمان الذي ينتهي المعلاق فيه، من وسط البئر إلى أعلاه. وذلك يفيد القول بالطفر، عند من يقول به. أو كون إحدى الحركتين أسرع من الأخرى، مع خلو كل واحد منهما عن مخالطة السكنات. واعلم: أنا إذا قدرنا بئراً طوله أربعة وستون ذراعاً. فإذا نصفنا هذا البئر بالخشبة المذكورة بنصفين، بحيث يكون طول كل واحد من هذين النصفين: اثنين وثلاثين ذراعاً. ثم نصفنا النصف الفوقاني، وجعلنا في منتصفه خشبة، بالصفة المذكورة وعلقنا عليه حبلاً، مقداره ستة عشر ذراعاً، ثم علقنا على أسفله معلاقاً، على طرف الجبل الأول، ثم نصفنا النصف الفوقاني، بالطريق الذي تقدم. وعملنا بالنصف الباقي ما ذكرناه، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الذراع الواحد. فإذا أخذنا حبلاً بمقدار ذراع، وعلقنا على طرفه معلاقاً، وأرسلناه إلى البئر، وعلقنا معلاقه بالحبل المشدود بالخشبة الأولى. فإذا
The third argument: A well whose depth is one hundred cubits, if there is a block in its middle, upon which a rope of fifty cubits is hung, and a bucket is hung at the other end of the rope. If we send down another rope of fifty cubits from the top of the well, and we tighten a pulley at the second end of this second rope. Then if we hang that pulley on the end of the first rope, then pull it to the top of the well, the bucket reaches from the bottom of the well to its top in the time that the pulley reaches from the middle of the well to its top. That implies the affirmation of the leap, for those who affirm it, or that one of the two motions is faster than the other, while each of them is free from the admixture of rests. And know: If we imagine a well whose length is sixty-four cubits. Then we divide this well in half by the mentioned block, so that the length of each of these two halves is thirty-two cubits. Then we divide the upper half, and put a block in its middle, in the described manner, and hang upon it a rope of sixteen cubits, then hang at its bottom a pulley, on the end of the first rope. Then we divide the upper half in the previously mentioned way, and do with the remaining half what we mentioned, until it ends at a single cubit. Then if we take a rope of the length of a cubit, and hang a pulley on its end, and send it into the well, and hang its pulley on the rope tied to the first block. Then if...

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument involving a well, a block (khashaba), ropes, a bucket, and a pulley (mi‘lāq). The setup: a well of 100 cubits deep, a block at midpoint, a rope of 50 cubits from it, a bucket at the end. Another rope of 50 cubits from the top with a pulley at its end. When the pulley is attached to the first rope and pulled up, the bucket rises from bottom to top in the same time the pulley rises from mid-depth to top. This illustrates either the leap (al-tafra)—since the bucket covers the full depth while the pulley covers only half—or that one motion (the bucket's) is faster than the other (the pulley's), with both motions free from any admixture of rest. The passage then extends the example: starting with a well of 64 cubits, repeatedly halving the upper part with blocks and ropes, until reaching a single cubit, then using a rope of one cubit with a pulley to attach to the first block. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is meant to further demonstrate the same point about speed without intermediate rests.

The Third Argument for the Leap: The Well and Ropes
Al-Razi presents the third argument for the leap using a well and ropes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third argument for the leap, how a thought experiment involving a well and ropes proves the infinite divisibility of space and time and the occurrence of speed differences without rests.

Core Idea
A well and ropes show that faster motion can occur without any resting points.

Al-Razi presents a clever thought experiment to challenge those who deny the leap or insist that differences in speed require interspersed rests. By analyzing the motion of a bucket and a hook in a well, he demonstrates that the bucket must either leap over intermediate points or move faster without any rests, thereby proving three key doctrines: the infinite divisibility of distance and time, and the possibility of speed variation without rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial experiment
Read the description of the 100-cubit well with a beam at the midpoint, a 50-cubit rope with a bucket, and a second rope with a hook. Note the setup carefully.
The hook is attached to the first rope and pulled to the top.
2
Follow the deduction
Notice that when the hook is pulled to the top, the bucket also reaches the top in the same time. This implies either a leap (the bucket skips intermediate points) or one motion is faster without any rests.
Al-Razi presents two possibilities: leap (al-ṭafra) or faster motion without rests.
3
Examine the generalization by halving
Read the detailed example with a 64-cubit well that is successively halved. Understand how the hook moves only one cubit while the bucket moves the entire depth in the same time.
The argument is extended to any well length, even millions of cubits.
4
Identify the three conclusions
The passage concludes that this proves three things: infinite divisibility of distance, infinite divisibility of time, and that speed differences can occur without any resting points (sakanāt).
These are the three claims the argument aims to establish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : [ إن (۱) ] البئر الذي عمقها مائة ذراع. إذا كان في منتصفها خشبة ، وعلق عليها حبل مقداره خمسون ذراعاً ، وعلق بالطرف الآخر من الحبل : دلو . فإذا أرسلنا حبلاً آخر مقداره خمسون ذراعاً من رأس البئر ، وشددنا بالطرف الثاني من هذا الحبل الثاني معلاقاً . فإذا علقنا ذلك المعلاق على طرف الحبل الأول ، ثم جررناه إلى رأس البئر فإن الدلو ينتهي من أسفل البئر إلى أعلاه في الزمان الذي ينتهي المعلاق فيه ، من وسط البئر إلى أعلاه . وذلك يفيد القول بالطفر ، عند من يقول به . أو كون إحدى الحركتين أسرع من الأخرى ، مع خلو كل واحد منهما عن مخالطة السكنات . واعلم : أنا إذا قدرنا بثراً [ يكون طولها ، مقداراً ينتهي عند التنصيف ، إلى الواحد . مثلاً : قدرنا بئراً (۲) ] طوله أربعة وستون ذراعاً . فإذا نصفنا هذا البشر بالخشبة المذكورة بنصفين، بحيث يكون طول كل واحد من هذين النصفين : اثنين وثلاثين ذراعاً . ثم نصفنا النصف الفوقاني ، وجعلنا في منتصفه خشبة ، بالصفة المذكورة وعلقنا عليه حبلاً ، مقداره ستة عشر ذراعاً ، ثم علقنا على أسفله معـلاقـاً ، على طرف الجبل الأول ، ثم نصفنا النصف الفوقاني ، بالطريق الذي تقدم . وعملنا بالنصف الباقي ما ذكرناه ، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الذراع الواحد. فإذا أخذنا حلاً بمقدار ذراع ، وعلقنا على طرقه معلاقا ، وأرسلناه إلى البئر ، وعلقنا معلاقة بالحبل المشدود بالخشبة الأولى . فإذا انجر ذلك المعلاق إلى رأس البئر ، انجر الدلو من أسفل البشر إلى أعلاه ففي الزمان الذي انتقل المعلاق الأعلى إلى رأس البئر وهو مقدار ذراع انتقل الدلو من أسفل البئر إلى أعلاه ، وهو أربعة وستون ذراعاً . واعلم : أنا لو فرضنا طول البئر مائة ألف ألف ذراع، وعملنا العمل المذكور ، فإنه حال ما يتحرك المعلاق الأعلى : شبراً ، فإنه يجب أن ينتقل الدلو من الأسفل إلى الأعلى ، مع كون تلك المسافة مائة ألف ألف ذراع . وإذا عرفت ذلك ، فهذا يدل على ثبوت المطالب الثلاثة : وهي انقسام المسافة أبداً ، وانقسام الزمان أبداً ، وحصول التفاوت في السرعة والبطء من غير تخلل السكنات
The third argument: A well whose depth is one hundred cubits. If there is a beam at its midpoint, and a rope of fifty cubits is hung on it, and a bucket is attached to the other end of the rope. Then if we send down another rope of fifty cubits from the top of the well, and we fasten a hook at the second end of this second rope. Then if we attach that hook to the end of the first rope, then pull it to the top of the well, the bucket goes from the bottom of the well to its top in the time in which the hook goes from the middle of the well to its top. That supports the claim of the leap (al-ṭafr), according to whoever holds it. Or that one of the two motions is faster than the other, while each is free from intermixing with rests. Know: if we suppose a well whose length, when halved, ends at one. For example, we suppose a well sixty-four cubits long. Then we halve this well with the mentioned beam into two halves, so that the length of each of these two halves is thirty-two cubits. Then we halve the upper half, and place a beam at its midpoint in the described manner, and hang a rope of sixteen cubits on it, then we attach a hook at its bottom to the end of the first rope, then we halve the upper half in the manner that preceded, and do with the remaining half what we mentioned, until it ends at the single cubit. Then if we take a rope of a cubit, attach a hook to its end, and send it into the well, and attach its hook to the rope tightened by the first beam. Then when that hook is pulled to the top of the well, the bucket is pulled from the bottom of the well to its top. So in the time in which the upper hook moves to the top of the well, which is a cubit, the bucket moves from the bottom of the well to its top, which is sixty-four cubits. And know: if we suppose the well's length to be one hundred thousand thousand cubits, and we do the described operation, then at the moment the upper hook moves a span, it is necessary that the bucket moves from the bottom to the top, despite that distance being one hundred thousand thousand cubits. And when you know that, this indicates the establishment of the three claims: the infinite divisibility of distance, the infinite divisibility of time, and the occurrence of difference in speed and slowness without the intercalation of rests.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the existence of the leap (al-ṭafra) or, alternatively, for the possibility of motion faster than motion without any intermixed rests. The argument uses a thought experiment with ropes and hooks in a well. First, a well of 100 cubits is described: a beam at the middle (50 cubits deep) supports a 50-cubit rope with a bucket. A second 50-cubit rope with a hook is lowered from the top and attached to the first rope. When the hook is pulled to the top, the bucket also rises to the top in the same time. Since the hook travels only 50 cubits while the bucket travels 100 cubits, either the bucket leaps (jumps) over intermediate points, or its motion is faster than the hook's motion but without any rest states. The argument then generalizes using successive halving: a 64-cubit well is divided into halves repeatedly until reaching a single cubit, with hooks at each division. Finally, the hook at the top moves only 1 cubit while the bucket moves 64 cubits. The same is extended to a huge well of 100 million cubits: when the top hook moves a span, the bucket must move the entire depth. This proves three theses: space is infinitely divisible, time is infinitely divisible, and speed differences occur without any rests. The argument is directed against those who deny these theses, and it shows that without accepting either the leap or the absence of rests in faster motion, the scenario would be impossible.

Key Terms
الطفرة (al-ṭafra)
السكنات (al-sakanāt)
السرعة (al-surʿa)
البطء (al-buṭʾ)
انقسام المسافة (inqisām al-masāfa)
انقسام الزمان (inqisām al-zamān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on a physical machine being constructed.
Correction: The argument is a conceptual thought experiment; it does not require actual construction. It shows logical consequences regardless of practical feasibility.

Checkpoint
What three claims does the third argument aim to prove?
A) The leap, the infinite divisibility of space, and the infinite divisibility of time
B) The existence of rest, the finitude of space, and the finitude of time
C) The impossibility of faster motion, the indivisibility of space, and the indivisibility of time
D) The existence of a vacuum, the speed of light, and the continuity of motion

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that without accepting either the leap or speed variation without rests, one cannot explain the simultaneous motion of the bucket and hook in the well thought experiment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to accept that either 'leaping' occurs or motion can speed up without any moments of rest. Which of these is harder for you to accept, and why? Does your reluctance reveal a deeper attachment to familiar intuitions about motion?
?
If you were to face a Muslim philosopher who denies these conclusions, what would you say to persuade them? How confident are you that rational arguments like this can truly support faith-based beliefs?
?
The halving process can continue indefinitely, pointing to a reality beyond your physical experience. Are you willing to trust your intellect even when it leads to conclusions that seem impossible? What does that mean for your relationship with Allah as the creator of both your intellect and the universe?

The Fourth Argument: Ship and Man
Al-Razi enumerates the fourth argument using a ship and a man moving in opposite directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses relative motion of a man on a ship to argue for either the existence of a leap (al-ṭafra) or a difference in speed without intermixed rests.

Core Idea
Relative motion forces a choice between leap and differential speed.

Al-Razi presents a clever thought experiment: a man walking in the opposite direction of a moving ship. This simple scenario becomes a powerful argument that challenges the Aristotelian view of motion. By analyzing the net displacement of the man relative to the ground, al-Razi forces us to confront two competing explanations for how motion can be continuous. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi structures his argument with two hypothetical scenarios.
The argument is conceptual, not a real experiment.
2
Visualize the scenario
Imagine a ship moving to the right, and a man on the ship walking to the left. The ship moves a certain distance, and the man moves either the same distance or more.
3
Examine the first case: equal movements
If the man moves the same distance as the ship in the same time, his net displacement relative to the ground is zero. He remains stationary. This explains why the wandering planets appear stationary at times.
4
Examine the second case: greater movement
If the man moves more than the ship, his net displacement is positive. This leads to two possible explanations: either a leap (al-ṭafra) occurs, or there is a difference in speed without any rests.
5
Reflect on the two conclusions
Al-Razi presents both explanations without favoring one. The argument forces a choice between the atomistic leap (some) and the philosophical differential speed (the philosophers).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إذا فرضنا سفينة تتحرك ، إلى جانب . وفرضنا إنساناً كان في تلك السفينة ، وهو يتحرك إلى ضد حركة السفينة . ففي الزمان الذي تحركت السفينة بمقدار جزء. إن تحرك الرجل بمقدار جزء ، ذهب الزائد بالناقص . فيلزم : أن يبقى الرجل واقفاً في مكانه . وهذا هو السبب في وقوف الكواكب المتحيرة في الرؤية . وأما إن تحرك أكثر ، لزم القول بالطفرة على قول البعض ، والتفاوت في السرعة والبطء على قول الحكماء
The fourth argument: If we suppose a ship moving to one side, and suppose a man in that ship moving in the opposite direction to the ship's motion. Then in the time when the ship moved by a certain amount, if the man moved by the same amount, the excess cancels the deficiency. So it follows that the man remains stationary in his place. And this is the reason for the apparent stationariness of the wandering planets. But if he moves more, then the claim of the leap is necessary according to some, and the difference in speed and slowness according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which uses the relative motion of a man on a ship. The ship moves in one direction, and the man moves in the opposite direction. If the man moves exactly the same distance as the ship moves in the same time, then relative to the ground, the man's net displacement is zero, so he appears stationary. This, the author notes, explains why the 'wandering planets' (the classical planets like Mercury, Venus, Mars, etc.) sometimes appear stationary in retrograde motion. If the man moves a greater distance than the ship, then his net motion is faster, which leads to two possible interpretations: either there is a leap (al-ṭafra), as some claim, or there is a difference in speed without any intermixed rests, as the philosophers (ḥukamāʾ) hold. The argument thus forces a choice between these two alternatives to explain how the man can cover a net distance greater than the ship's displacement in the same time.

Key Terms
السفينة (al-safīna)
حركة ضد (ḥaraka ḍidd)
الكواكب المتحيرة (al-kawākib al-mutaḥayyira)
الطفرة (al-ṭafra)
الحكماء (al-ḥukamāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument describes a real physical experiment.
Correction: It is a conceptual argument about relative motion, not a real experiment.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, if the man on the ship moves more than the ship moves, what two possibilities arise?
A) He stays stationary, or he moves backward
B) There is a leap, or there is a difference in speed without rests
C) The ship sinks, or the man falls
D) Time stops, or space contracts

Key Takeaway
The ship-and-man argument shows that relative motion can lead to either a leap or differential speed, depending on relative distances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that opposing motions can cancel out, leaving you stationary. In your spiritual journey, what 'motions' (distractions, sins, or even good deeds with wrong intentions) are opposing your forward movement toward Allah? Will you identify and remove one counter-motion this week?
?
You are forced to choose between the leap and the steady speed. When faced with a difficult truth (like a sin you cling to), do you try to 'leap' over it impulsively, or do you slowly work on it with patient, consistent effort? Which approach does this lesson suggest is more rational?
?
The man on the ship could be stationary relative to the ground without realizing it. Are there areas of your life where you think you're making progress but, viewed from Allah's perspective (the 'ground'), you are actually standing still? What would it take to gain that perspective today?

The Fifth Argument: Instantaneous Light
Al-Razi presents the fifth argument based on instantaneous sunlight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument from instantaneous sunlight and the two possible explanations he offers: the leap theory or infinite speed.

Core Idea
Instant sunlight forces a choice: either light leaps or speed is unbounded.

Al-Razi presents a fifth argument for a controversial conclusion. He observes that sunlight reaches us instantly upon sunrise. Assuming light is corporeal, he argues that such instantaneous traversal of a great distance requires either that light jumps over intermediate points (the leap theory) or that there is no upper limit to speed. This argument sets up a dilemma for those who reject the leap theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a premise followed by a conclusion.
The premise is that sunlight arrives instantly.
2
Understand the premise and assumption
Al-Razi assumes light is a body (a common view in his atomist framework). The instantaneous arrival is taken as a given. Ask yourself why he considers this a valid starting point.
3
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities: the leap (al-ṭafra) and unlimited degrees of speed (lā nihāyat al-marātib al-sur'a). Understand each: the leap skips intermediate points; infinite speed would cover the distance in zero or imperceptible time.
4
Consider the argument's purpose
This argument is meant to support the leap theory by showing that the alternative (infinite speed) is problematic for other reasons. Reflect on why Al-Razi might prefer the leap theory.
The dilemma aims to force acceptance of the leap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الشمس كلما تطلع ، وصلت الأنوار في الحال إلينا ، دفعة واحدة . والأنوار أجسام. وقطع هذه الأجسام ، هذه المسافة العظيمة ، في هذه اللحظة اللطيفة ، لا يمكن إلا بالطفرة ، أو لأجل أنه لا نهاية المراتب السرعة
The fifth argument: Whenever the sun rises, the lights reach us immediately, all at once. And lights are bodies. And these bodies traversing this great distance in this brief moment is possible only through the leap, or because of the unlimited degrees of speed.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, based on the instantaneous arrival of sunlight. The author states that whenever the sun rises, its light (considered as bodies, since he holds light to be corporeal) reaches us instantly. Traversing the enormous distance between the sun and the earth in such a brief moment is impossible according to ordinary motion. Therefore, either the light leaps (jumps over intermediate points) or there is an infinite hierarchy of speeds such that light travels at an infinite speed (or so fast that it appears instantaneous). This argument again pushes for either the leap theory or the theory of unbounded speed differences without rests.

Key Terms
الشمس (al-shams)
الأنوار (al-anwār)
أجسام (ajsām)
الطفرة (al-ṭafra)
لا نهاية المراتب السرعة (lā nihāyat al-marātib al-surʿa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument can be refuted by noting that light speed is finite (as modern physics holds).
Correction: The argument takes instantaneous arrival as a given premise based on everyday observation; it is a philosophical argument, not a scientific claim about actual light speed. The debate is about the logical implications of that premise.

Checkpoint

A) Light is immaterial, or it travels through a vacuum
B) There is a leap, or there are unlimited degrees of speed
C) The sun is close, or our eyes are slow
D) Time is an illusion, or space is not real

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fifth argument presents a dilemma: either light leaps across space or its speed is unlimited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Sixth Argument: Light and the Leap
Al-Razi presents the sixth argument about light and concludes the chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument for the leap theory based on the instantaneous exit of light particles and how it concludes the chapter's discussion.

Core Idea
Light leaves a window instantly; only a leap can explain that.

Al-Razi presents his final argument in this section, drawing on a simple observation: when a window is closed, light particles emerge all at once, not sequentially. This phenomenon, he argues, cannot be accounted for by continuous motion but only by the leap (al-ṭafra). The passage also summarizes the chapter's arguments and notes that proponents of the leap—and even those who advocate faster motions—find support in them.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth argument
Identify the scenario presented and the conclusion Al-Razi draws. Notice the key observation: light particles emerge 'all at once' (دفعة واحدة).
The argument is concise but relies on the assumption that light is composed of particles.
2
Reflect on the logic
Why does instantaneous exit necessarily imply a leap? Consider ordinary motion: if particles moved continuously, they would exit sequentially. Only a discontinuous leap allows simultaneous emergence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Note that this argument is one of several in the chapter. Al-Razi then states that these are all the arguments, and highlights two groups that use them: advocates of the leap and advocates of motion faster than motion without rests.
The text cuts off at 'قد', suggesting Al-Razi continues to discuss the second group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنا إذا سددنا الكوة . خرجت الأجزاء النورانية ، دفعة واحدة . وهذا لا يمكن إلا بالطفرة . فهذه جملة الوجوه المذكورة في هذا الباب . والقائلون بالطفر يتمسكون بها في إثبات الطفرة ، والقائلون بحصول حركة ، أسرع من حركة أخرى ، مع خلوها عن مخالطة كل السكنات . قد

Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains the sixth and final argument in this section, followed by a concluding summary. The sixth argument is simple: when we close a window (or a small opening), light particles exit instantaneously in a single burst. Since they all leave at once without any time delay, this cannot be explained by ordinary motion; only a leap (al-ṭafra) accounts for their simultaneous emergence. The author then concludes stating that these are all the arguments mentioned in this chapter. He notes that two groups use these arguments: those who advocate the leap theory use them to prove the leap, while those who advocate motion faster than motion without any intermixed rests also use them, though the sentence is cut off. The 'قد' at the end indicates the argument continues, likely stating that the latter group also finds support in these arguments.

The Two Horns of the Millstone Dilemma
Razi sets up the dilemma with two impossible options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two initial possibilities Razi considers in the millstone argument and why both are rejected.

Core Idea
A rotating millstone forces us to choose between two absurd options, paving the way for infinite divisibility.

Razi introduces a clever geometric argument to challenge the notion of indivisible parts. He imagines a rotating millstone with two concentric circles—a large hoop and a small hoop. The argument turns on a simple question: when the large hoop moves a certain amount, what does the small hoop do? The two obvious answers both lead to contradictions, setting up the need for a third possibility.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the millstone
Imagine a millstone with two concentric circles. The outer large hoop and the inner small hoop are fixed together. When the millstone rotates, both hoops must move in some relation.
Think of a CD with an outer and inner track.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Razi presents two options: either the small hoop moves the same amount as the large hoop (exactly one part each), or the large hoop moves while the small hoop stays still.
These are the only two initial possibilities he considers.
3
See why each is rejected
The first option implies equal circumferences, which is geometrically false. The second would tear the millstone apart, which experience shows does not happen.
Both lead to absurdities, so neither is acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : إذا دارت الرحى. فإما أن يقال : مهما تحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً ، فإنه يتحرك الطوق الصغير جزءاً . وذلك محال . وإلا لزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة الصغيرة ، مساوياً لمدار الدائرة العظيمة . وإما أن يقال : إنه قد يتحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً مع أنه لا يتحرك من الطوق الصغير شيء البتة وذلك باطل . لأن هذا يقتضي تفكك أجزاء الرحى بعضها عن البعض . وذلك باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن الحس يدل على أن الحجر الصلب ، بل الأملس ، لا يصير عند استدارته ، كالدقيق الذي لا يتصل بعض أجزائه بالبعض . الثاني : إنا نفرض الكلام في الفلك . وحينئذ يمتنع عليه التفرق والتمزق . لأنا بينا : أن الفرق على الفلك محال . وأيضاً : فإنا نتمسك بقوله تعالى : ( وبنينا فوقكم سبعاً شدادا ) ، وإذا كانت الأفلاك أبداً متحركة على الاستدارة ، وكانت الحركة على الاستدارة توجب التفرق والتمزق . وجب أن لا تكون الأفلاك موصوفة بالشدة والإحكام ، وذلك على خلاف نص القرآن . وأيضاً : نتمسك بقوله تعالى في صفة السموات : ( فارجع البصر هل ترى من فطور ) ؟ ولو كانت الأفلاك متخرقة متمزقة ، لوجب أن تكون كلها خروقاً . وذلك على نقيض قوله تعالى : ( هل ترى من فطور ) ؟ والثالث : إن الإنسان لو وضع عقبه على الأرض ، ثم أدار نفسه على عقبه ، دورة تامة. لزم أن يقال : إنه في تلك الحالة ، تفككت أجزاؤه وتفرقت وتمزقت . والحس يدل على أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذا الإنسان يعلم من نفسه : أنه بقي متصل الأجزاء ، كما كان قبل ذلك . لا سيما وعند المعتزلة : أن افتراق أجزاء البنية يوجب الموت . الرابع : إن القول بتفكك أجزاء الرحى ، يقتضي أن يقال : إن كل واحدة من تلك الدوائر ، يجب أن يعلم : أنه كم ينبغي أن يسكن ؟ وكم ينبغي أن يتحرك ؟ حتى لا تتغير تلك الأجزاء عن مساماتها ومناسباتها ، التي كانت موجودة . ومعلوم : أن أعقل الناس لا يهتدي إلى هذه الحالة ، فضلا عن أجزاء الحجر ، مع أنها جمادات خالية عن الفهم والإدراك . إلا أن المتكلمين يقولون : إن إله العالم يحرك كل واحد منها في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكنها في البعض ، على وجه تبقى تلك المسامات والممناسبات كما كانت . ومثل هذا الفعل من الإله الحكيم غير مستبعد . ولما ثبت بالدليل : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً ، تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة أيضاً جزءاً تاماً . وثبت : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً لا يتحرك من الصغيرة شيء البتة . بقي أن يقال : إن عند حركة العظيمة تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة أقل من جزء . وذلك يفيد كون المقدار قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . واعلم : أن هذه الحجة تقتضي انقسام الزمان والمسافة معاً إلى غير النهاية
The first proof: We say: if the millstone rotates, then either it is said: whenever the large hoop moves a part, the small hoop moves a part. That is impossible, for then the circumference of the small circle would be equal to the circumference of the large circle. Or it is said: the large hoop may move a part while nothing of the small hoop moves at all. That is false because this would necessitate the separation of the parts of the millstone from each other. That is false for reasons: First: sensation indicates that a hard stone, indeed a smooth one, does not become, when it rotates, like flour whose parts are not connected to each other. Second: we posit the discussion concerning the celestial sphere. Then separation and fragmentation are impossible for it. For we have shown that division is impossible for the celestial sphere. Also, we adhere to His statement exalted: 'And We built above you seven strong ones' (78:12). And if the celestial spheres are always moving in a circle, and circular motion necessitates separation and fragmentation, then it would be necessary that the celestial spheres not be described with strength and firmness, and that contradicts the text of the Qur'an. Also, we adhere to His statement exalted describing the heavens: 'Then return the gaze, do you see any rifts?' (67:3). And if the celestial spheres were torn and fragmented, they would have to be all holes. That contradicts His statement: 'Do you see any rifts?' Third: if a man placed his heel on the ground, then turned himself around on his heel a complete rotation, it would be necessary to say that in that state his parts became disintegrated, separated, and fragmented. But sensation indicates that this is false. For this man knows from himself that he remained continuous in parts, as he was before. Especially since with the Mu'tazilites, the separation of the parts of the structure necessitates death. Fourth: the statement that the parts of the millstone become disintegrated necessitates saying that each one of those circles must know how much it should remain stationary and how much it should move, so that those parts do not change from their pores and proportions that existed. And it is known that the most intelligent of people do not guide to this state, let alone the parts of stone, while they are inanimate, devoid of understanding and perception. Except that the theologians (mutakallimun) say: The God of the world moves each one of them in some places and makes it stationary in some, in such a way that those pores and proportions remain as they were. And such an act from the wise God is not unlikely. And when it has been established by proof that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part, the small circle also moves a complete part, and it has been established that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part, nothing of the small moves at all, there remains to say: when the large moves, the small circle moves less than a part. That yields that magnitude is divisible to infinity. And know: this proof necessitates the division of time and distance together to infinity.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the denial of the part (presumably the indivisible atom), using the analogy of a rotating millstone. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum to establish the infinite divisibility of magnitude and, by extension, time. The proof begins by stating the scenario: if a millstone rotates, there are two possibilities regarding the movement of its concentric circles (the large hoop and the small hoop). The first possibility: whenever the large hoop moves by some amount (a 'part'), the small hoop moves by exactly the same amount. This is deemed impossible because it would imply that the circumference of the small circle equals that of the large circle, contradicting geometry. The second possibility: the large hoop moves a part while the small hoop does not move at all. This is also false, because it would cause the millstone to disintegrate—its parts would separate. The author then provides four reasons (wujuh) to demonstrate why the second possibility leads to absurdity. The first reason: empirical observation shows that a hard, smooth stone does not crumble into unconnected pieces when it rotates, unlike flour. The second reason: if we consider the celestial spheres (al-falak), which constantly rotate, their fragmentation is impossible because they are described in the Quran as 'strong' (shidad) and without 'rifts' (futur). If rotation caused disintegration, the heavens would not be firm. The third reason: a human turning on his heel does not suffer the disintegration of his limbs; sensation confirms bodily continuity. The Mu'tazilite view that separation of bodily parts causes death is cited as further evidence. The fourth reason: if the millstone were to disintegrate, each part would need to 'know' how much to move or rest to preserve its relative positions—an impossible knowledge for inanimate stone. Thus both initial possibilities are rejected. At this point, the author notes that the theologians (mutakallimun) might object that God could orchestrate the movements of each part to prevent disintegration, which is not impossible for the wise God. However, this does not refute the core argument. The author then concludes: since the first two options are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that when the large circle moves a part, the small circle moves less than a part. This implies that magnitude is divisible without end (infinite divisibility). Finally, the author states that this proof also necessitates the infinite divisibility of time and distance simultaneously, extending the conclusion beyond mere spatial magnitude.

Key Terms
الرحى (millstone)
الطوق العظيم (large hoop)
الطوق الصغير (small hoop)
الدائرة (circle)
الجزء (part)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the first possibility (both hoops move equally)?
A) It would cause the millstone to disintegrate.
B) It would make the small circle's circumference equal to the large one.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It is impossible for a stone to rotate.

Key Takeaway
The two obvious relationships between the hoops both lead to contradictions, forcing us to consider a third option.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a dilemma in your faith where both options seem impossible, do you have the courage to accept that there must be a third option, even if you cannot see it yet? How does this argument from Razi challenge you to rethink your assumptions about the limits of your understanding?
?
This argument forces you to reject both horns because they lead to absurdity. In your own life, what assumptions have you clung to that lead to contradictions? Are you willing to let them go to find the truth, even if it uncovers a deeper mystery?
?
The millstone example shows that ignoring geometry leads to falsehood. In your spiritual life, are there 'geometries'—principles or scriptures—that you are tempted to ignore because they seem to create a dilemma? How will you commit to embracing the third option that God provides?

7 min
Four Reasons Against Disintegration and the Conclusion
Razi provides four reasons and draws the conclusion of infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four reasons Razi gives to show that the millstone does not disintegrate, and how this leads to the conclusion of infinite divisibility.

Core Idea
Four proofs—from sensation, the Quran, human anatomy, and divine wisdom—show that the millstone remains intact, so the small hoop must move less than a part, implying infinite divisibility.

Having rejected the two obvious possibilities, Razi now must defend the claim that the second option (no movement of the small hoop) is false because it would cause disintegration. He offers four distinct arguments to prove that disintegration does not occur: from empirical observation, from the nature of the celestial spheres, from a human turning on his heel, and from the impossibility of inanimate parts coordinating their movements. After countering an objection from the theologians, he presents the only remaining possibility: the small hoop moves less than a part, which proves that magnitude is infinitely divisible.

Guided Path
1
Examine the four reasons one by one
Read each of the four reasons carefully. The first is sensory evidence, the second uses the Quran and celestial spheres, the third uses a human example, and the fourth involves the need for intelligent coordination.
Notice how each reason independently shows that the millstone does not fall apart.
2
Consider the theologians' objection
Razi mentions that the mutakallimun might say God could move each part to prevent disintegration. He acknowledges this is not impossible but implies the argument still stands.
This objection does not refute the core logic.
3
Understand the conclusion
With both initial options rejected, the only possibility is that when the large hoop moves a part, the small hoop moves less than a part. This means a part can be divided further, so magnitude is divisible without end.
Razi also extends this to time and distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : إذا دارت الرحى. فإما أن يقال : مهما تحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً ، فإنه يتحرك الطوق الصغير جزءاً . وذلك محال . وإلا لزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة الصغيرة ، مساوياً لمدار الدائرة العظيمة . وإما أن يقال : إنه قد يتحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً مع أنه لا يتحرك من الطوق الصغير شيء البتة وذلك باطل . لأن هذا يقتضي تفكك أجزاء الرحى بعضها عن البعض . وذلك باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن الحس يدل على أن الحجر الصلب ، بل الأملس ، لا يصير عند استدارته ، كالدقيق الذي لا يتصل بعض أجزائه بالبعض . الثاني : إنا نفرض الكلام في الفلك . وحينئذ يمتنع عليه التفرق والتمزق . لأنا بينا : أن الفرق على الفلك محال . وأيضاً : فإنا نتمسك بقوله تعالى : ( وبنينا فوقكم سبعاً شدادا ) ، وإذا كانت الأفلاك أبداً متحركة على الاستدارة ، وكانت الحركة على الاستدارة توجب التفرق والتمزق . وجب أن لا تكون الأفلاك موصوفة بالشدة والإحكام ، وذلك على خلاف نص القرآن . وأيضاً : نتمسك بقوله تعالى في صفة السموات : ( فارجع البصر هل ترى من فطور ) ؟ ولو كانت الأفلاك متخرقة متمزقة ، لوجب أن تكون كلها خروقاً . وذلك على نقيض قوله تعالى : ( هل ترى من فطور ) ؟ والثالث : إن الإنسان لو وضع عقبه على الأرض ، ثم أدار نفسه على عقبه ، دورة تامة. لزم أن يقال : إنه في تلك الحالة ، تفككت أجزاؤه وتفرقت وتمزقت . والحس يدل على أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذا الإنسان يعلم من نفسه : أنه بقي متصل الأجزاء ، كما كان قبل ذلك . لا سيما وعند المعتزلة : أن افتراق أجزاء البنية يوجب الموت . الرابع : إن القول بتفكك أجزاء الرحى ، يقتضي أن يقال : إن كل واحدة من تلك الدوائر ، يجب أن يعلم : أنه كم ينبغي أن يسكن ؟ وكم ينبغي أن يتحرك ؟ حتى لا تتغير تلك الأجزاء عن مساماتها ومناسباتها ، التي كانت موجودة . ومعلوم : أن أعقل الناس لا يهتدي إلى هذه الحالة ، فضلا عن أجزاء الحجر ، مع أنها جمادات خالية عن الفهم والإدراك . إلا أن المتكلمين يقولون : إن إله العالم يحرك كل واحد منها في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكنها في البعض ، على وجه تبقى تلك المسامات والممناسبات كما كانت . ومثل هذا الفعل من الإله الحكيم غير مستبعد . ولما ثبت بالدليل : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً ، تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة أيضاً جزءاً تاماً . وثبت : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال : عند حركة الدائرة العظيمة جزءاً لا يتحرك من الصغيرة شيء البتة . بقي أن يقال : إن عند حركة العظيمة تتحرك الدائرة الصغيرة أقل من جزء . وذلك يفيد كون المقدار قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . واعلم : أن هذه الحجة تقتضي انقسام الزمان والمسافة معاً إلى غير النهاية
The first proof: We say: if the millstone rotates, then either it is said: whenever the large hoop moves a part, the small hoop moves a part. That is impossible, for then the circumference of the small circle would be equal to the circumference of the large circle. Or it is said: the large hoop may move a part while nothing of the small hoop moves at all. That is false because this would necessitate the separation of the parts of the millstone from each other. That is false for reasons: First: sensation indicates that a hard stone, indeed a smooth one, does not become, when it rotates, like flour whose parts are not connected to each other. Second: we posit the discussion concerning the celestial sphere. Then separation and fragmentation are impossible for it. For we have shown that division is impossible for the celestial sphere. Also, we adhere to His statement exalted: 'And We built above you seven strong ones' (78:12). And if the celestial spheres are always moving in a circle, and circular motion necessitates separation and fragmentation, then it would be necessary that the celestial spheres not be described with strength and firmness, and that contradicts the text of the Qur'an. Also, we adhere to His statement exalted describing the heavens: 'Then return the gaze, do you see any rifts?' (67:3). And if the celestial spheres were torn and fragmented, they would have to be all holes. That contradicts His statement: 'Do you see any rifts?' Third: if a man placed his heel on the ground, then turned himself around on his heel a complete rotation, it would be necessary to say that in that state his parts became disintegrated, separated, and fragmented. But sensation indicates that this is false. For this man knows from himself that he remained continuous in parts, as he was before. Especially since with the Mu'tazilites, the separation of the parts of the structure necessitates death. Fourth: the statement that the parts of the millstone become disintegrated necessitates saying that each one of those circles must know how much it should remain stationary and how much it should move, so that those parts do not change from their pores and proportions that existed. And it is known that the most intelligent of people do not guide to this state, let alone the parts of stone, while they are inanimate, devoid of understanding and perception. Except that the theologians (mutakallimun) say: The God of the world moves each one of them in some places and makes it stationary in some, in such a way that those pores and proportions remain as they were. And such an act from the wise God is not unlikely. And when it has been established by proof that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part, the small circle also moves a complete part, and it has been established that it is not permissible to say that when the large circle moves a part, nothing of the small moves at all, there remains to say: when the large moves, the small circle moves less than a part. That yields that magnitude is divisible to infinity. And know: this proof necessitates the division of time and distance together to infinity.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the denial of the part (presumably the indivisible atom), using the analogy of a rotating millstone. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum to establish the infinite divisibility of magnitude and, by extension, time. The proof begins by stating the scenario: if a millstone rotates, there are two possibilities regarding the movement of its concentric circles (the large hoop and the small hoop). The first possibility: whenever the large hoop moves by some amount (a 'part'), the small hoop moves by exactly the same amount. This is deemed impossible because it would imply that the circumference of the small circle equals that of the large circle, contradicting geometry. The second possibility: the large hoop moves a part while the small hoop does not move at all. This is also false, because it would cause the millstone to disintegrate—its parts would separate. The author then provides four reasons (wujuh) to demonstrate why the second possibility leads to absurdity. The first reason: empirical observation shows that a hard, smooth stone does not crumble into unconnected pieces when it rotates, unlike flour. The second reason: if we consider the celestial spheres (al-falak), which constantly rotate, their fragmentation is impossible because they are described in the Quran as 'strong' (shidad) and without 'rifts' (futur). If rotation caused disintegration, the heavens would not be firm. The third reason: a human turning on his heel does not suffer the disintegration of his limbs; sensation confirms bodily continuity. The Mu'tazilite view that separation of bodily parts causes death is cited as further evidence. The fourth reason: if the millstone were to disintegrate, each part would need to 'know' how much to move or rest to preserve its relative positions—an impossible knowledge for inanimate stone. Thus both initial possibilities are rejected. At this point, the author notes that the theologians (mutakallimun) might object that God could orchestrate the movements of each part to prevent disintegration, which is not impossible for the wise God. However, this does not refute the core argument. The author then concludes: since the first two options are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that when the large circle moves a part, the small circle moves less than a part. This implies that magnitude is divisible without end (infinite divisibility). Finally, the author states that this proof also necessitates the infinite divisibility of time and distance simultaneously, extending the conclusion beyond mere spatial magnitude.

Key Terms
الفلك (celestial sphere)
المتكلمون (theologians)
المسافة (distance)
الزمان (time)
الانقسام (divisibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the millstone argument only proves spatial infinite divisibility, but Razi explicitly extends it to time and distance.
Correction: Razi states at the end that the proof necessitates the division of time and distance together to infinity.

Checkpoint
What is the third reason Razi gives against disintegration?
A) The Quran mentions strong heavens.
B) A person turning on his heel remains intact.
C) Hard stones do not become like flour.
D) The mutakallimun say God prevents it.

Key Takeaway
The only remaining possibility after rejecting two absurd options is that the small hoop moves less than a part, proving that magnitude (and time/distance) is divisible without end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi uses four reasons to show that the millstone does not disintegrate, including the fact that your own body remains intact when you turn on your heel. Are you aware that every movement you make is sustained by God's power? In what area of your life do you fear 'falling apart' despite God's proven faithfulness? What is one step you will take this week to trust that God holds you together?
?
The fourth reason highlights that only the wise God can maintain the precise proportions of inanimate parts. How does this challenge your tendency to take your own abilities and planning for granted? Will you pause today to thank God for the countless hidden details He manages in your body and world, and ask for help to rely more on His wisdom than your own?
